### Electric Charge and Conservation

*Concept ID: `phys.em.electric-charge` · Difficulty: developing · Bloom level: understand · Mastery threshold: 0.75 · Estimated study time: 3h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to describe electric charge as a conserved, quantized, two-signed property of matter, state the attraction/repulsion rules, explain charging by friction, conduction, and induction, and distinguish conductors from insulators.

Electric charge is a fundamental property of matter that is quantised and conserved in all interactions.

Electric charge is the property that powers the domain: matter carries it in two kinds, positive (protons) and negative (electrons), with likes repelling and opposites attracting. Two conservation-grade facts govern all charge bookkeeping. Charge is CONSERVED: it is never created or destroyed, only transferred — rubbing a balloon on hair moves electrons; the balloon's gain is exactly the hair's loss, and the universe's total never changes. Charge is QUANTIZED: it comes in integer multiples of the elementary charge e = 1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹ C — the coulomb is a colossal unit (one coulomb is ~6 × 10¹⁸ electrons; everyday static involves nano- to microcoulombs). Materials divide by electron mobility: conductors (metals — a sea of free electrons) let charge flow and spread over surfaces; insulators (glass, plastic, dry air) pin charge where it lands. Three charging mechanisms follow: friction (contact transfer of electrons between different materials), conduction (touching a charged object shares its charge), and induction (a charged object REARRANGES a conductor's charges at a distance, and grounding one side leaves a net opposite charge — charging without contact, and the reason a rubbed balloon sticks to a NEUTRAL wall: induced polarization brings opposite charge nearer than like). Only electrons move in ordinary solids — 'positive charging' means electron deficit, not proton flow.

**Key ideas**

- Two signs: like charges repel, unlike attract — the force rule beneath all electrostatics.
- Conservation: charge is only transferred, never created or destroyed — every charging event is a zero-sum electron ledger.
- Quantization: q = ne with e = 1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹ C — the coulomb is huge; static electricity lives at nC-μC scales.
- Conductors (free electrons, charge spreads and flows) versus insulators (charge stays put) — the material dichotomy behind circuits and safety alike.
- Charging mechanisms: friction (contact transfer), conduction (sharing by touch), induction (rearrangement at a distance + grounding → opposite net charge, no contact).
- In solids only electrons move: positive charge means electron deficit; a charged object attracts NEUTRAL matter by inducing polarization (balloon on wall, comb and paper bits).

**Vocabulary**

- **electric charge** — The two-signed, conserved, quantized property of matter that sources electric force; SI unit the coulomb (C).
- **elementary charge e** — 1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹ C — the electron's charge magnitude; all charge is an integer multiple.
- **conductor / insulator** — Materials with free / immobile electrons — charge spreads and flows versus stays where placed.
- **charging by induction** — Charging a conductor without contact: rearrange with a nearby charge, ground away one sign, remove ground then rod.
- **polarization (electrostatic)** — The induced charge rearrangement in neutral matter that lets charged objects attract it.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* Rubbing creates charge.
  *Correction:* Friction only TRANSFERS electrons between materials: the balloon's negative gain exactly equals the hair's positive deficit. Conservation holds to the electron — nothing is created.
- *Misconception:* Positive charging means protons moved onto the object.
  *Correction:* Protons are locked in nuclei; in ordinary matter only electrons transfer. A positively charged rod has LOST electrons. All everyday electrostatics is electron bookkeeping.
- *Misconception:* Charged objects attract only oppositely charged objects.
  *Correction:* They also attract NEUTRAL objects by induced polarization: the charge rearranges the neutral body's charges so the opposite kind sits nearer, and the net force is attractive — combs lift paper, balloons stick to walls.
- *Misconception:* Insulators cannot be charged since charge can't move in them.
  *Correction:* Insulators charge beautifully — by friction — precisely BECAUSE the deposited charge cannot leak away. What they resist is charge FLOW, not charge residence; conductors, conversely, must be held by insulating handles to stay charged.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Creating or destroying charge in ledger problems.
- Moving protons.
- Wrong induction sign (same as the rod) or wrong removal order.
- Claiming insulators can't be charged.
- Forgetting that attraction alone doesn't prove the attracted object is charged (polarization suffices).

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- A two-panel electron-ledger animation of balloon-on-hair friction: electrons hopping one way, the ± totals staying zero-sum.
- An induction sequence: charged rod approaching a conductor, charges rearranging, ground wire touched, rod withdrawn — net opposite charge remaining, step by step.
- A conductor/insulator contrast: deposited charge spreading over a metal sphere versus staying as a patch on a plastic one.
- The balloon-on-neutral-wall polarization diagram: induced surface charge, near-opposite/far-like, net attraction.
- An electroscope demonstration (or video): leaves diverging under charge, used to test all three charging methods.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A polythene rod rubbed with wool acquires −4.8 nC. (a) How many electrons transferred, and which way? (b) What charge does the wool carry? (c) The rod is brought near (not touching) a grounded metal sphere; the ground is removed, then the rod. What is the sphere's final charge sign, and why?

1. (a) n = q/e = 4.8 × 10⁻⁹/1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹ = 3.0 × 10¹⁰ electrons — thirty billion electrons moved FROM the wool TO the rod.
2. (b) Conservation: the wool is left with exactly +4.8 nC (electron deficit equal to the rod's gain).
3. (c) The negative rod repels the sphere's free electrons; with the ground attached they escape to earth.
4. Removing the ground first traps the electron deficit; removing the rod then leaves the sphere POSITIVE — charged by induction, opposite to the rod, without contact.
5. Order check: removing the rod first would let the electrons return — the grounding-first order is what makes induction stick.
6. Scale note: 30 billion electrons sounds vast yet is 5 × 10⁻⁹ of a coulomb — the coulomb's enormity calibrated.

*Outcome:* The student computes 3 × 10¹⁰ electrons with correct direction, assigns +4.8 nC to the wool by conservation, and derives the sphere's positive sign from the induction sequence with the order-of-operations logic.

**Real-world intuition**

- Photocopiers and laser printers place toner by engineered electrostatic charging and induction.
- Fuel-truck grounding chains and anti-static wristbands manage friction charge that would otherwise spark — electronics factories and operating theatres regulate it.
- Electrostatic precipitators strip smoke particles from power-station flues by charging and collecting them.
- Paint spraying and crop dusting charge droplets so they wrap around targets by induced attraction.
- Lightning is friction charging at cloud scale — ice collisions building charge separation until megavolt breakdown.

**Practice progression**

Item types: quantization computations (q = ne, both directions), conservation-ledger problems across charging events, induction-sequence reasoning (predict signs, justify order), classification items (conductor/insulator, charging method identification). Suggested item count: 10.

Begin with q = ne and ledger arithmetic, add mechanism identification, then multi-step induction sequences, ending with polarization-attraction explanations.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, sequence-reasoning problems, conceptual quiz. Bloom alignment: Understand — students must explain charge's conservation, quantization, and transfer mechanisms, and predict induction outcomes, beyond recalling the two-sign rule.

Mastery signal: The student runs electron ledgers correctly, predicts induction signs with order justification, and explains neutral-body attraction by polarization, at 75% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Electrostatics rewards live theatre: balloons, electroscopes, and a rubbed comb over paper bits make every rule visible in minutes (dry air permitting). Enforce the electron-only-movers rule from day one — 'positive charge flowed' talk sows confusion that circuits will reap. The induction sequence's ORDER (ground off before rod away) is the discriminating question; drill it. Calibrate the coulomb's enormity early (nC static, but 1 C in a second is an ordinary ampere) — it preps current. The balloon-on-wall polarization case answers the commonest 'but the wall is neutral!' objection preemptively.

**Student notes**

Bookkeep electrons: rubbing, touching, and inducing only move them around — totals never change, and 'positive' always means electron-poor. Compute counts with q = ne (e = 1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹ C) and expect billions per nanocoulomb. Know your materials: metals spread and leak charge, plastics hold it. For induction, remember the order — ground away the unwanted sign FIRST, then remove the rod — and expect the final charge OPPOSITE to the inducer. Charged things attract neutral things too: polarization brings the opposite sign closer.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.meas.units
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): phys.em.coulombs-law, phys.em.electric-current
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: Charge is the domain's root: Coulomb's law (phys.em.coulombs-law) quantifies its force, fields (phys.em.electric-field) carry its influence, and current (phys.em.electric-current) is its flow. Conservation of charge joins the great conservation laws of mechanics (phys.mech.conservation-of-momentum) and energy; quantization previews the atomic granularity of modern physics (phys.mod).

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.meas.units

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one q = ne computation, one induction sequence with signs and order, one polarization explanation. Monthly, recite the two ironclad rules — conserved, quantized — and the electron-only-movers clause; they audit every later circuit and field problem.

### Coulomb's Law

*Concept ID: `phys.em.coulombs-law` · Difficulty: developing · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 3h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to state and apply Coulomb's law F = kq₁q₂/r² with k = 9 × 10⁹ N·m²/C², compute forces between point charges including vector superposition along a line, compare its structure with Newtonian gravity, and use inverse-square ratio reasoning.

Coulomb's law states that the electrostatic force between two point charges is proportional to their product and inversely proportional to the square of their separation.

Coulomb's law prices the force between point charges: F = kq₁q₂/r², with k = 1/4πε₀ ≈ 9.0 × 10⁹ N·m²/C², directed along the line joining the charges — repulsive for like signs, attractive for unlike. The structure is gravitation's inverse square reborn with two upgrades: charge replaces mass as the coupling property, and the force comes in BOTH signs (attraction and repulsion) where gravity only pulls. The constants' contrast is staggering — the electric force between a proton and electron beats their gravitational attraction by ~10³⁹ — which resolves an apparent paradox: electricity, though overwhelmingly stronger, hides at large scales because matter is neutral to exquisite precision (both signs cancel), while gravity, unscreenable, rules planets. Working the law is inverse-square craft transplanted: distances centre-to-centre and SQUARED (double r, quarter F), the coulomb-scale k making even microcoulomb pairs push with newtons at centimetres. Forces obey Newton's third law (equal and opposite on the pair, whatever the charge sizes) and SUPERPOSE: with several charges, compute each pairwise force and add as vectors — collinear cases (this concept's scope) reduce to signed one-dimensional sums, with the classic three-charge equilibrium problem as the capstone. The law is exact for point charges and spheres (acting from centres, the shell theorem's electric twin) — the seed from which fields, Gauss, and potential all grow.

**Key ideas**

- F = kq₁q₂/r² along the joining line; k = 1/4πε₀ ≈ 9.0 × 10⁹ N·m²/C² — like signs repel, unlike attract.
- Inverse-square discipline transplants from gravity: centre-to-centre r, squared — ×2 distance → ×¼ force.
- Both forces of the pair are equal and opposite (Newton's third law), regardless of which charge is bigger.
- Electric ≫ gravitational: ~10³⁹ for proton-electron — but neutral matter screens electricity, so gravity rules astronomy while Coulomb rules atoms.
- Superposition: net force on a charge = vector sum of pairwise Coulomb forces — collinear problems become signed sums; equilibrium points balance them to zero.
- Valid for points and uniform spheres (centre-acting); the microcoulomb-at-centimetres scale delivers tangible newtons — k's enormity calibrated.

**Vocabulary**

- **Coulomb's law** — F = kq₁q₂/r²: the inverse-square force between point charges, repulsive for like signs, along the joining line.
- **Coulomb constant k** — 1/4πε₀ ≈ 9.0 × 10⁹ N·m²/C² — the enormous coupling that makes microcoulombs push in newtons.
- **superposition (forces)** — The net force on a charge is the vector sum of independent pairwise Coulomb forces.
- **screening** — The near-perfect cancellation of electric effects by neutral matter's balanced signs — why gravity rules large scales.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* The bigger charge exerts the bigger force on the smaller one.
  *Correction:* Coulomb forces are a third-law pair: exactly equal magnitudes on both charges, always. The smaller charge ACCELERATES more (a = F/m if lighter), but the forces match — the truck-and-fly lesson, electrified.
- *Misconception:* Doubling the distance halves the force.
  *Correction:* Inverse-SQUARE: doubling r quarters F; tripling cuts it to a ninth. Linear-decay intuition systematically overprices long-range electric force — ratio-reason with the square every time.
- *Misconception:* With several charges present, Coulomb's law needs modification.
  *Correction:* The law never changes; forces SUPERPOSE. Each pair interacts as if alone, and the net force is the vector sum — three-body electric problems are bookkeeping, not new physics.
- *Misconception:* Since electricity is 10³⁹ times stronger than gravity, gravity should be negligible everywhere.
  *Correction:* Bulk matter is almost perfectly neutral: positive and negative cancel, screening electric force at large scales. Gravity, with one sign only, cannot cancel — so the weakling wins astronomy while Coulomb wins chemistry.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Forgetting to square r.
- Unequal 'forces' on unequal charges.
- Dropping signs (or units — μC left unconverted).
- Placing equilibrium nearer the stronger charge.
- Adding force magnitudes when directions oppose.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- A side-by-side law card: Coulomb and Newton gravity, structure identical, coupling and sign behaviour contrasted, the 10³⁹ ratio stamped.
- An inverse-square dilution graphic reused from gravitation: the same flux-through-spheres picture with charge at the centre.
- A charged-balloon force demonstration with a hanging pith ball at measured distances — halving distance visibly quadrupling deflection.
- A collinear three-charge diagram with signed force arrows on the middle charge, summed to locate equilibrium.
- A neutral-matter screening cartoon: two 'huge' + and − columns cancelling to a whisper, beside gravity's single unstoppable column.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* Charges q₁ = +2.0 μC and q₂ = +8.0 μC are fixed 0.30 m apart. (a) Find the force each exerts on the other. (b) Where on the line between them does a third charge q₃ feel zero net force? (c) Does the answer to (b) depend on q₃'s sign or size?

1. (a) F = kq₁q₂/r² = 9 × 10⁹ × 2 × 10⁻⁶ × 8 × 10⁻⁶/0.09 = 0.144/0.09 = 1.6 N — on EACH charge, equal and opposite (repulsive pair).
2. (b) Zero net force needs the two repulsions/attractions on q₃ to cancel: kq₁q₃/x² = kq₂q₃/(0.30 − x)².
3. q₃ and k cancel: (0.30 − x)²/x² = q₂/q₁ = 4 → (0.30 − x)/x = 2.
4. Solve: 0.30 − x = 2x → x = 0.10 m — the null sits 10 cm from the SMALLER charge (nearer the weaker source, as balance demands).
5. (c) Both q₃'s magnitude and sign cancelled in step 3: ANY third charge floats force-free there (though stability differs by sign) — the null is geometry, not chemistry.
6. Ratio check: distances 0.10 vs. 0.20 m — force ratio (2/1)² = 4 exactly offsets the charge ratio 8/2 ✓.

*Outcome:* The student computes the 1.6 N third-law pair, locates the null 0.10 m from the smaller charge by the square-root ratio method, and shows the result independent of the test charge.

**Real-world intuition**

- Chemistry IS Coulomb's law: ionic bonds, lattice energies, and molecular shapes are electrostatic force budgets.
- Electrostatic precipitators, photocopier toner placement, and paint-spray wrap-around run on computed Coulomb attractions.
- Atomic force microscopy and ion traps position matter with engineered Coulomb forces at nanometre precision.
- Particle accelerators steer and focus beams against mutual Coulomb repulsion (space charge) — beamline design is superposition at scale.
- Protein folding and DNA packing are biology's Coulomb problems — salt bridges and charge screening decide structure.

**Practice progression**

Item types: direct Coulomb computations across charge/distance scales, inverse-square ratio-reasoning items, collinear superposition and equilibrium-point problems, gravity-comparison and screening conceptual questions. Suggested item count: 12.

Begin with single-pair computations and ratio reasoning, add signed collinear sums, then equilibrium hunts (like and unlike pairs), ending with the gravity comparison and scaling arguments.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, equilibrium-analysis problems, conceptual quiz on pairs and screening. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must execute the law with inverse-square and superposition discipline on configurations beyond single pairs.

Mastery signal: The student computes pairwise forces with correct third-law pairing, ratio-reasons with the square, and solves one collinear equilibrium, at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Teach it as gravitation's twin — same structure, new coupling, both signs — and collect the transplant dividends (centre-to-centre r, inverse-square ratios, third-law pairs) explicitly; students who did the gravity domain own half this concept already. The 10³⁹ comparison plus the screening resolution is the conceptual centrepiece — pose the paradox before resolving it. Keep superposition collinear here (2D belongs to fields and beyond); the equilibrium-point problem with the √-ratio method is the standard capstone. Microcoulomb-newton scale calibration prevents both disbelief and unit slips.

**Student notes**

Same skeleton as gravity: multiply the couplings, divide by centre-distance squared — but now with signs (like repel) and a colossal k. The forces on both charges are always equal and opposite; sizes decide accelerations, not forces. For several charges, sum pairwise forces as vectors — on a line, that's signed arithmetic, and equilibrium points sit nearer the weaker charge by the square-root of the charge ratio. Ratio-reason before computing: ×2 r → ×¼ F.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.electric-charge
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): phys.em.electric-field, phys.mod.bohr-model
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: Coulomb's law transplants the inverse-square machinery of gravitation (phys.mech.universal-gravitation) to charge (phys.em.electric-charge), and is re-expressed per-unit-charge as the electric field next (phys.em.electric-field). Its superposition rule and third-law pairing come from mechanics (phys.mech.newtons-third-law); its sphere-as-point validity is the shell theorem's twin; Gauss's law (phys.em.gauss-law) will repackage its inverse square as flux.

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.electric-charge

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 2 days, 1 week, and 1 month: one pair computation, one ratio drill, one equilibrium hunt. Monthly, retell the strength-vs-screening paradox — the story that places electricity and gravity correctly in the universe's org chart.

---

### Electric Field and Field Lines

*Concept ID: `phys.em.electric-field` · Difficulty: proficient · Bloom level: understand · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 4h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to define the electric field E = F/q as force per unit positive test charge, compute point-charge fields E = kq/r², draw and interpret field-line diagrams, superpose fields from multiple charges, and compute the force qE on charges placed in known fields.

The electric field at a point is the electrostatic force per unit positive test charge placed at that point.

The electric field re-stages Coulomb's law in the two-step architecture learned at gravitation: a charge modifies the space around it (creating a field), and other charges respond to the field AT THEIR OWN LOCATION — F = qE, with the field defined as force per unit positive test charge, E = F/q (N/C). A point charge's field is Coulomb's law per-unit-charge: E = kq/r², radially OUTWARD from positive charges and INWARD toward negative ones (the positive-test-charge convention doing the sign work). Field lines map the geography: they start on positive charge and end on negative, their direction gives E's direction, their density its strength, and they never cross (E is single-valued) — the dipole's arcing pattern and the uniform field's parallel lines between charged plates are the two canonical portraits. Fields superpose as vectors point by point, exactly as forces did, and the payoff of the field view is division of labour: compute E once from the sources, then price the force on ANY charge as qE — negative charges feeling force OPPOSITE to E (the sign trap the convention sets). Between parallel plates the field is uniform, making qE constant-force problems — electron deflection in old CRTs, inkjet steering, Millikan's droplets — mechanics problems with an electric engine. The field is also the object that will carry energy, waves, and light: this concept is where electromagnetism's true protagonist enters.

**Key ideas**

- Two-step causality: sources make E; charges at a point feel F = qE — Coulomb's law split into field creation and field response.
- Definition: E = F/q (N/C), the force per unit POSITIVE test charge — direction conventions all flow from that choice.
- Point charge: E = kq/r², radially out of + and into − ; superposition adds source fields as vectors, point by point.
- Field lines: begin on +, end on −, direction along E, density ∝ strength, never crossing — dipole arcs and parallel-plate uniformity as the standard portraits.
- Response rule: positive charges accelerate along E, negative OPPOSITE to E — the electron's anti-field habit is the domain's standing sign trap.
- Uniform fields (parallel plates) turn qE into constant-force mechanics: projectile-style electron deflection, Millikan suspension (qE = mg) — old toolkits, new engine.

**Vocabulary**

- **electric field E** — Force per unit positive test charge, E = F/q (N/C); the space-filling condition sources create and charges respond to.
- **field line** — A curve along E: from + to −, never crossing, density encoding strength — a sampled map of a continuous field.
- **test charge** — A vanishingly small positive probe defining E's direction and magnitude without disturbing the sources.
- **uniform field** — Constant-E region (parallel plates): qE mechanics with constant force.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* The field exists only where there is a charge to feel it.
  *Correction:* The field is the source's modification of space, present at every point regardless of occupancy — the same ontology as the gravitational field. A test charge REVEALS E; removing it removes the force, not the field.
- *Misconception:* Field lines are trajectories charges follow.
  *Correction:* Lines map force direction, not paths: a charge with sideways velocity curves ACROSS lines (electron in a CRT), just as satellites cross gravity's radial lines. Only a charge released from rest starts along a line.
- *Misconception:* Electrons move in the direction of the electric field.
  *Correction:* E points as a POSITIVE test charge would be pushed; electrons, negative, feel F = qE with q < 0 — force and acceleration OPPOSITE to E. Half of all field sign errors are this one habit unlearned.
- *Misconception:* Where field lines are drawn is where the field is; between lines there's none.
  *Correction:* Lines are a sampling of a continuous field — E exists between them, with density-of-lines encoding strength. Doubling drawn lines changes the picture, not the physics.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Electron pushed along E.
- Reading field lines as trajectories.
- Forgetting vector addition in superposition (adding magnitudes).
- r-squared slips and μC/nC conversion errors.
- Crossing field lines or starting them on negative charge in diagrams.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- The two-step architecture card reused from gravitation: source → field → response, with E = F/q and F = qE as the two interfaces.
- The canonical field-line gallery: single +, single −, dipole, like pair, parallel plates — with never-cross and density rules annotated.
- A vector-superposition construction at three points near two charges: source arrows added tip-to-tail into the net E.
- An electron-in-plates deflection animation: parabola across the uniform field, force arrow anti-parallel to E throughout.
- Semolina-in-oil (or simulation) field-line visualization around electrodes — nature drawing the diagrams.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* (a) Find E at point P, 0.30 m from a +2.0 μC point charge, and the force on a −5.0 nC charge placed there. (b) Two plates 2.0 cm apart create a uniform field; an electron (m = 9.1 × 10⁻³¹ kg) between them feels E = 1.0 × 10⁴ N/C. Find its acceleration and compare with g. (c) State E's direction and the electron's acceleration direction.

1. (a) E = kq/r² = 9 × 10⁹ × 2 × 10⁻⁶/0.09 = 2.0 × 10⁵ N/C, pointing radially AWAY from the positive source.
2. Force on the negative charge: F = qE = 5 × 10⁻⁹ × 2 × 10⁵ = 1.0 × 10⁻³ N, directed TOWARD the source (negative charge, anti-field) — attraction recovered through the field.
3. (b) a = qE/m = 1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹ × 1.0 × 10⁴/9.1 × 10⁻³¹ ≈ 1.8 × 10¹⁵ m/s².
4. Compare: ~1.8 × 10¹⁴ g — electric fields bully electrons so hard that gravity is irrelevant inside every device.
5. (c) E points from + plate to − plate; the electron accelerates OPPOSITE — toward the + plate: the anti-field habit exercised.
6. Method recap: sources → E (step 1); occupant × E → force with sign care (step 2) — never recompute Coulomb pairs when a field is already known.

*Outcome:* The student computes 2 × 10⁵ N/C and the 10⁻³ N attractive force via the two-step split, derives the 10¹⁵ m/s² electron acceleration dwarfing gravity, and states both directions correctly.

**Real-world intuition**

- Inkjet printers and mass spectrometers steer charged droplets/ions with computed qE deflections.
- Capacitive touchscreens sense your finger's distortion of a designed electric field.
- Electrostatic air purifiers and smokestack precipitators drive charged particles across fields to collectors.
- Millikan's oil-drop suspension (qE = mg) measured e itself — the field as a precision balance.
- Cell membranes maintain ~10⁷ V/m fields that gate ion channels — electrophysiology is applied field response.

**Practice progression**

Item types: point-charge field computations and superposition (collinear and simple 2D), F = qE response problems with sign discipline, field-line diagram drawing and interpretation, uniform-field mechanics (deflection, suspension qE = mg). Suggested item count: 12.

Begin with single-source fields and qE responses, add two-source superposition and null points, then line-diagram work, ending with uniform-field mechanics hybrids.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, diagram construction/interpretation tasks, hybrid mechanics problems. Bloom alignment: Understand — students must explain the field ontology and conventions and operate the two-step architecture, with computations in support.

Mastery signal: The student computes fields and responses with correct directions (including electron anti-field cases), draws legal field-line diagrams, and solves one uniform-field mechanics item, at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Collect the gravitation dividend explicitly: the two-step architecture, field ontology, and lines-vs-trajectories distinction were all pre-paid in phys.mech.gravitational-field — this concept adds signs and the anti-field electron. Drill that electron habit relentlessly; it is the domain's most persistent error. The field-line gallery should be DRAWN by students under the three legality rules, not just viewed. The electron-acceleration-vs-g computation lands the scale of electric dominance inside matter. Keep dipole quantitation for the next concept; here the dipole is a portrait.

**Student notes**

Think in two steps always: sources build E (point charge: kq/r², out of +, into −; several sources: vector-sum), then occupants feel F = qE — positives along E, NEGATIVES OPPOSITE (tattoo the electron rule). Read and draw lines by the three laws: start +, end −, never cross, denser = stronger. In uniform fields you are back in mechanics with F = qE constant. And keep the ontology straight: the field is there whether or not anything feels it.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.coulombs-law
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): phys.em.electric-dipole, phys.em.gauss-law, phys.em.magnetic-force
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: The field re-architects Coulomb's law (phys.em.coulombs-law) exactly as the gravitational field re-architected Newtonian gravity (phys.mech.gravitational-field) — one template, second deployment. Dipoles (phys.em.electric-dipole) and Gauss's law (phys.em.gauss-law) build on it; potential (phys.em.electric-potential) adds its energy landscape; and the field itself becomes light in electromagnetic waves (phys.em.electromagnetic-waves).

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.coulombs-law

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 2 days, 1 week, and 1 month: one field computation with a response force, one line-diagram drawing, one electron-direction drill. Monthly, recite the two-step architecture and the anti-field electron rule — the sentence pair that carries the domain.

---

### Electric Dipole

*Concept ID: `phys.em.electric-dipole` · Difficulty: proficient · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 4h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to define the electric dipole and its moment p = qd (from − to +), compute the torque τ = pE sin θ on a dipole in a uniform field and its orientation energy U = −pE cos θ, describe the dipole's own field qualitatively (1/r³ falloff), and connect dipoles to molecular polarity and microwave heating.

An electric dipole consists of two equal and opposite charges separated by a small distance; it experiences torque in a uniform field.

The electric dipole — equal and opposite charges ±q separated by distance d — is electromagnetism's most important composite object, because neutral matter is made of them: every polar molecule (water above all) is a dipole, and every atom becomes one when a field polarizes it. The dipole's identity card is its moment, p = qd, a vector pointing from the NEGATIVE charge to the POSITIVE (magnitude charge × separation; water's is 6.2 × 10⁻³⁰ C·m). In a UNIFORM field the dipole feels zero net force (the ± forces cancel) but a nonzero TORQUE, τ = pE sin θ — the field grips the dipole and twists it toward alignment, with orientation energy U = −pE cos θ: minimum (−pE) aligned, maximum (+pE) anti-aligned, the energy difference 2pE being the flipping cost. This torque-without-force is the microwave oven's engine: an oscillating field twists water dipoles back and forth billions of times per second, and molecular friction banks the work as heat — dipole physics cooking dinner. The dipole's OWN field has a signature falloff: the ± contributions nearly cancel at distance, leaving a field ~1/r³ (faster than the point charge's 1/r²) with the characteristic arcing line pattern — on the axis, twice the equatorial strength at equal r... precisely, E_axis = 2kp/r³ versus E_equator = kp/r³ (far-field). In NON-uniform fields the cancellation of forces fails and dipoles feel a net pull toward stronger field — the mechanism behind the comb attracting neutral paper: induced dipoles, then gradient force.

**Key ideas**

- Definition and moment: ±q separated by d; p = qd, directed from − to + — the dipole's full identity for field purposes.
- Uniform field: net force ZERO (± forces cancel), torque τ = pE sin θ — maximal broadside (90°), zero aligned or anti-aligned.
- Orientation energy: U = −pE cos θ — aligned is the ground state (−pE), anti-aligned the summit (+pE); flip cost 2pE.
- Dipole's own far field: ~1/r³ (near-cancellation of ±), axis field twice the equatorial at equal r; the arcing line portrait from the field concept, quantified in scaling.
- Non-uniform fields exert net force: dipoles are pulled toward stronger field — the comb-and-paper attraction (polarize, then pull) mechanized.
- Matter is dipoles: polar molecules (H₂O: p = 6.2 × 10⁻³⁰ C·m) are permanent ones; any atom in a field becomes an induced one — this concept is the bridge from charges to materials (dielectrics ahead).

**Vocabulary**

- **electric dipole / dipole moment p** — ±q separated by d; p = qd directed from − to + — the composite object neutral matter is made of.
- **dipole torque** — τ = pE sin θ in a uniform field — rotation toward alignment with zero net force.
- **orientation energy** — U = −pE cos θ: −pE aligned (ground), +pE anti-aligned (summit), 2pE to flip.
- **polar molecule** — A molecule with a permanent dipole moment (water: 6.2 × 10⁻³⁰ C·m) — chemistry's charge asymmetry.
- **induced dipole** — The moment a field creates in neutral matter by displacing its charges — the first step of polarization and of comb-and-paper attraction.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* A dipole in a uniform field is pushed along the field.
  *Correction:* The + and − forces are equal and opposite: net force ZERO. What survives is torque — the field rotates the dipole toward alignment but takes it nowhere. Net translation needs a NON-uniform field (gradient pull toward stronger E).
- *Misconception:* The dipole moment points from + to −, like the field between the charges.
  *Correction:* Convention sets p from − to + (so aligned dipoles have minimum energy U = −pE). The INTERNAL field between the charges indeed runs + to − — the moment and the internal field are anti-parallel, and mixing them flips every torque and energy sign.
- *Misconception:* A dipole's field falls off as 1/r² like any charge distribution's.
  *Correction:* The ± contributions nearly cancel at distance: the surviving field decays as 1/r³. Neutral-but-polar matter therefore whispers where net charge shouts — why dipole effects are short-range and chemistry is a contact sport.
- *Misconception:* Microwaves heat food by making water molecules vibrate at their resonance.
  *Correction:* 2.45 GHz is far BELOW water's resonances; the oven drives dipole ROTATION against molecular friction (dielectric loss), not a resonant vibration. The frequency is chosen for penetration depth and legal allocation — 'resonance heating' is the popular myth.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Moment drawn + to − (flipping every sign downstream).
- Net force claimed in uniform fields (or torque forgotten).
- Using 1/r² for dipole far fields.
- Maximum torque placed at alignment.
- Explaining microwave heating as resonance or as field-locking of molecules.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- The dipole identity card: ±q, separation d, moment arrow − → + drawn boldly against the internal-field arrow to disarm the convention trap.
- A uniform-field torque sequence: broadside dipole with force couple shown, rotating to alignment, torque and U readouts tracking θ.
- The energy-vs-angle curve U = −pE cos θ with ground state, summit, and 2pE flip cost marked.
- A microwave-oven cartoon: oscillating E flipping water dipoles, friction heat glowing — with the 'not resonance' caption.
- A comb-and-paper mechanism strip: neutral scrap → induced dipole → gradient force toward the comb — the everyday non-uniform-field payoff.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A water molecule (p = 6.2 × 10⁻³⁰ C·m) sits in a microwave oven's field of E = 2.0 × 10³ V/m. (a) Find the maximum torque on the molecule. (b) Find the energy to flip it from aligned to anti-aligned. (c) Compare that energy with thermal energy kT at 300 K (k = 1.38 × 10⁻²³ J/K) and interpret. (d) Why does the oven still heat effectively?

1. (a) τ_max = pE (θ = 90°) = 6.2 × 10⁻³⁰ × 2.0 × 10³ = 1.24 × 10⁻²⁶ N·m — tiny absolutely, but applied to a single molecule.
2. (b) Flip cost: ΔU = 2pE = 2.48 × 10⁻²⁶ J.
3. (c) kT = 1.38 × 10⁻²³ × 300 ≈ 4.1 × 10⁻²¹ J — thermal energy beats the alignment energy by ~10⁵: molecules are NOT locked to the field; alignment is a slight statistical bias in a thermal riot.
4. (d) Heating needs no locking: the oscillating field (2.45 × 10⁹ flips/s) does a whisker of work against friction on each of ~10²⁵ molecules — tiny bias × enormous rate × Avogadro-scale count = kilowatts into the soup.
5. Sign audit: aligned U = −pE, anti-aligned +pE — the 2pE difference used, with p's −→+ convention keeping the signs honest.
6. Moral: single-molecule electromagnetism is feeble against kT; bulk effects are statistics — the bridge to dielectrics, where this bias becomes polarization.

*Outcome:* The student computes τ_max = 1.24 × 10⁻²⁶ N·m and ΔU = 2pE ≈ 2.5 × 10⁻²⁶ J, finds kT ~10⁵ larger, and explains microwave heating as rate-times-statistics rather than locking or resonance.

**Real-world intuition**

- Microwave ovens rotate water dipoles against molecular friction — dipole torque as domestic infrastructure.
- Chemistry's polarity rules (solubility, boiling points, 'like dissolves like') are dipole-dipole energetics.
- Liquid-crystal displays steer rod-like molecular dipoles with fields — every LCD pixel is applied dipole torque.
- Antenna engineering begins with the oscillating dipole — the elementary radiator behind broadcasting (and the EM-waves concept ahead).
- Dielectric spectroscopy diagnoses materials (moisture in grain, tissue properties) by their dipoles' frequency response.

**Practice progression**

Item types: moment, torque, and orientation-energy computations, angle-dependence items (where is torque maximal/zero; energy extremes), far-field scaling and axis/equator comparisons, mechanism explanations (microwave heating, comb-and-paper, polar molecules). Suggested item count: 10.

Begin with p, τ, U computations at set angles, add the energy-curve reasoning and flip costs, then far-field scaling comparisons, ending with the kT statistics and gradient-force mechanisms.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, angle-analysis problems, mechanism-explanation items. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must run the torque and energy formulas with the moment convention intact and deploy dipole mechanics in molecular and everyday contexts.

Mastery signal: The student computes τ and U with correct signs and extremes, uses the 1/r³ scaling correctly, and explains one mechanism (microwave or comb) accurately, at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Guard the moment convention (− to +) with a drawn contrast against the internal field; every torque/energy sign error traces there. The zero-force/nonzero-torque distinction in uniform fields, versus gradient force in non-uniform ones, organizes the whole concept — teach it as a two-row table. The kT comparison in the worked example is the deepest content: single-molecule EM is feeble, bulk effects are statistical bias — said now, it makes dielectrics (next cluster) almost self-evident. Debunk microwave 'resonance' explicitly; students will re-teach it at home.

**Student notes**

Carry the dipole's three numbers: p = qd (arrow − to +), τ = pE sin θ (max broadside, zero aligned), U = −pE cos θ (aligned lowest, 2pE to flip). Uniform fields twist but don't translate; only field GRADIENTS pull — and they pull toward stronger field, which is why charged combs lift neutral paper. Far away, dipole fields die as 1/r³ (near-cancellation). Against kT, single-dipole energies are minuscule: bulk electromagnetism is statistics.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.electric-field
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): none yet mapped
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: The dipole assembles charges (phys.em.electric-charge) under field response (phys.em.electric-field) with mechanics' torque and energy language (phys.mech.torque, phys.mech.potential-energy). It is the microscopic unit of dielectrics (phys.em.dielectrics), the magnetic dipole's template (phys.em.magnetic-dipole), and the elementary radiator of electromagnetic waves (phys.em.electromagnetic-waves); its chemistry face is molecular polarity.

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.electric-field

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 2 days, 1 week, and 1 month: one τ/U computation pair, one uniform-vs-gradient force ruling, one mechanism retelling. Monthly, redraw the identity card (charges, d, moment arrow) and the U(θ) curve — the two pictures that keep the signs honest.

---

### Gauss's Law

*Concept ID: `phys.em.gauss-law` · Difficulty: proficient · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.85 · Estimated study time: 5h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to define electric flux Φ = EA cos θ, state Gauss's law Φ = q_enc/ε₀, exploit symmetry to derive the fields of spheres, lines, and planes, and explain shielding (zero field inside a conductor's cavity).

Gauss's law relates the total electric flux through a closed surface to the enclosed charge: Φ = Q_enc/ε₀.

Gauss's law repackages Coulomb's inverse square as a counting statement: the electric FLUX through any closed surface — the net field-lines-through-area tally, Φ = ΣEA cos θ — equals the enclosed charge divided by ε₀ (Φ = q_enc/ε₀, with ε₀ = 8.85 × 10⁻¹² C²/N·m²). The law holds for every closed surface around any charges, but its POWER is unlocked by symmetry: choose a 'Gaussian surface' matching the source's symmetry so E is constant and perpendicular (or parallel) on each face, and the flux integral collapses to E × area — whence the classic trio falls out in three lines each: a sphere of charge acts as a point (E = kQ/r² outside — the shell theorem for free), an infinite line gives E = λ/2πε₀r (falling as 1/r), and an infinite plane gives E = σ/2ε₀ — constant, distance-independent, the parallel-plate field's origin. Gauss also legislates conductors: in electrostatic equilibrium E = 0 inside conductor material (else charges would still be moving), so all excess charge sits on the SURFACE, and any charge-free cavity is field-free — electrostatic shielding, the Faraday cage that protects electronics, aircraft, and microwave-oven kitchens. The law's deep content is geometry: flux counts source lines, and lines neither appear nor vanish in empty space — the inverse square IS the surface area of spheres growing as r². (Zero net flux does NOT mean zero field — a chargeless box in a uniform field has lines in one side and out the other.) Gauss's law is the first of Maxwell's equations, met here in its working clothes.

**Key ideas**

- Electric flux: Φ = EA cos θ — the net field-through-area count; outward positive, closed surfaces tally in-minus-out.
- Gauss's law: Φ_closed = q_enc/ε₀ — flux counts ENCLOSED charge only; external charges send lines in and out equally.
- Symmetry unlocks it: choose Gaussian surfaces (sphere, cylinder, pillbox) on which E is uniform — flux collapses to E × A and E solves in a line.
- The classic trio: sphere/point E = kQ/r² (shells act from centres); infinite line E = λ/2πε₀r; infinite plane E = σ/2ε₀ (uniform, distance-free).
- Conductors in equilibrium: E = 0 inside the material, excess charge on the surface, charge-free cavities field-free — shielding/Faraday cages.
- Zero net flux ≠ zero field: a chargeless surface in any external field still has E everywhere on it — the counting is net, not presence.
- Deep reading: Gauss ⇔ inverse square (spheres' area grows as r²); this is Maxwell equation #1 in working form.

**Vocabulary**

- **electric flux Φ** — The net field-through-area count, Φ = EA cos θ (N·m²/C); outward positive on closed surfaces.
- **Gauss's law** — Φ_closed = q_enc/ε₀ — flux through any closed surface counts only the enclosed charge.
- **Gaussian surface** — An imaginary closed surface chosen to match symmetry so the flux integral collapses to E × A.
- **permittivity of free space ε₀** — 8.85 × 10⁻¹² C²/N·m² — the vacuum constant relating flux to charge (k = 1/4πε₀).
- **electrostatic shielding** — The zero-field interior of conductor cavities — the Faraday cage principle.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* If the net flux through a surface is zero, the field is zero on it.
  *Correction:* Zero net flux means zero ENCLOSED charge: lines entering equal lines leaving. A chargeless box in a strong uniform field has flux in one face and out the other — field everywhere, tally zero.
- *Misconception:* Charges outside the Gaussian surface contribute to the flux.
  *Correction:* External charges' lines enter AND exit — net zero contribution. They shape E on the surface, but the flux tally reads only q_enc. (That is exactly why the law is a counting statement, not a field formula.)
- *Misconception:* Gauss's law only works for symmetric charge distributions.
  *Correction:* The LAW holds universally; symmetry is only what makes it USEFUL for extracting E by hand. For messy distributions the law still audits flux perfectly — it just won't hand you E on one line.
- *Misconception:* The field inside any hollow object is zero — shielding works for any material.
  *Correction:* Zero-field cavities are a CONDUCTOR privilege (free charges rearrange until E = 0 inside the metal); an insulating shell does not shield. And even conductors shield their inside from outside fields — a charge placed INSIDE the cavity produces field there and induces surface charge.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Counting external charges in q_enc.
- Zero-flux ⇒ zero-field leaps.
- Using Gaussian surfaces that don't match symmetry (then trusting E×A anyway).
- Forgetting cos θ in open-surface flux.
- Granting insulators shielding powers.
- Confusing the plane's σ/2ε₀ with the capacitor gap's σ/ε₀ (two plates superpose — flag for capacitance).

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- A flux-counter animation: field lines threading tilted areas with Φ = EA cos θ read off; then a closed box in a uniform field tallying to zero.
- The Gaussian-surface toolkit: sphere around a point charge, cylinder around a line, pillbox through a plane — each with 'E uniform here' faces highlighted.
- The three-geometry results plotted together: 1/r² (point/sphere), 1/r (line), constant (plane) — one graph, three symmetries.
- A conductor cross-section in an external field: induced surface charge, interior E = 0, cavity serene — the Faraday cage drawn.
- The classic car-struck-by-lightning / phone-in-a-cage demonstration footage as shielding's payoff.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A solid metal sphere of radius 0.10 m carries Q = +4.0 μC in equilibrium. Find E at (a) r = 0.05 m (inside the metal), (b) r = 0.10 m (surface), (c) r = 0.20 m; (d) find the flux through any closed surface enclosing the whole sphere, and (e) state where the charge resides and why.

1. (a) Inside conductor material: E = 0 — equilibrium forbids internal field; a Gaussian sphere at r = 0.05 m encloses zero charge (all charge is outside it, on the surface).
2. (b) Surface: Gaussian sphere just outside encloses all Q: E = kQ/r² = 9 × 10⁹ × 4 × 10⁻⁶/0.01 = 3.6 × 10⁶ N/C, radially outward.
3. (c) r = 0.20 m: E = 9 × 10⁹ × 4 × 10⁻⁶/0.04 = 9.0 × 10⁵ N/C — quarter of the surface value at double the radius: inverse square, audited.
4. (d) Φ = q_enc/ε₀ = 4.0 × 10⁻⁶/8.85 × 10⁻¹² ≈ 4.5 × 10⁵ N·m²/C — for ANY enclosing surface, sphere or potato-shaped: the tally is shape-blind.
5. (e) Excess charge sits entirely on the outer surface: were any inside, a Gaussian surface within the metal would enclose charge and demand E ≠ 0 there — contradicting equilibrium.
6. Collect the method: pick the surface to match symmetry, count the enclosed charge, and let Φ = EA solve for E — three lines per geometry.

*Outcome:* The student produces 0, 3.6 × 10⁶, and 9.0 × 10⁵ N/C with the enclosed-charge logic explicit, the shape-blind 4.5 × 10⁵ flux, and the surface-residence proof by contradiction.

**Real-world intuition**

- Faraday cages shield electronics labs, MRI rooms, aircraft, and cars from external fields and lightning.
- Coaxial cables confine signals by conductor geometry — Gauss's cylinder is their design calculation.
- Electrostatic shielding in microwave-oven doors and instrument cases is pillbox-and-cavity reasoning in sheet metal.
- Charged-particle beam pipes and detectors are engineered with line- and cylinder-symmetry fields.
- The parallel-plate σ/2ε₀ result underlies every capacitor calculation ahead — Gauss as the capacitor industry's first line.

**Practice progression**

Item types: flux computations through open and closed surfaces, symmetry derivations (sphere, line, plane) and field-profile problems, conductor/cavity reasoning (where is E zero; induced charges), enclosed-charge audits (what does this flux reveal?). Suggested item count: 12.

Begin with flux arithmetic, run the three symmetry derivations, then conductor and cavity scenarios, ending with mixed audits and shielding design questions.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation and derivation set, conductor-scenario problems, conceptual flux quiz. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must choose Gaussian surfaces by symmetry, execute the flux-to-field collapse, and deploy conductor consequences; this concept carries an elevated threshold (0.85) as electrostatics' power tool.

Mastery signal: The student derives the three classic fields via chosen surfaces, computes fluxes with enclosed-charge discipline, and resolves one conductor/cavity case, at 85% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Sell the law as counting before calculus: lines out of charge, tallied through surfaces — the zero-net-flux-in-uniform-field box is the decisive first exercise. Run all three symmetry derivations at the board with the surface CHOICE narrated as the skill. The conductor consequences deserve their own lesson beat: E = 0 inside by contradiction, surface residence, cavity serenity — then the cage footage as payoff. Flag honestly that non-symmetric cases keep the law true but not hand-solvable. Name it as Maxwell #1; the set completes at the domain's end.

**Student notes**

Read Gauss as an audit: net lines out = enclosed charge/ε₀, for ANY closed surface. To extract E, choose the surface where symmetry makes E uniform — sphere, cylinder, or pillbox — and solve Φ = EA in one line; memorize the trio of results (1/r², 1/r, constant). Inside equilibrium conductors E = 0, charge lives on surfaces, and empty cavities are shielded. Never read zero net flux as zero field — external charges tally to nothing while shaping everything.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.electric-field
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): phys.em.electric-potential, phys.em.maxwells-equations
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: Gauss's law repackages Coulomb's inverse square (phys.em.coulombs-law) exactly as flux geometry, echoing the shell theorem of gravitation (phys.mech.universal-gravitation). Its plane result builds capacitors (phys.em.capacitance), its conductor rules run shielding, and it stands as Maxwell equation #1 (phys.em.maxwells-equations); the flux vocabulary returns magnetically in phys.em.magnetic-flux.

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.electric-field

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 2 days, 1 week, and 1 month: one flux audit, one symmetry derivation, one conductor scenario. Monthly, re-derive the sphere-acts-as-point result — the three-line argument that carries the shell theorem, the trio, and the law's meaning at once.

---

### Electric Potential and Potential Difference

*Concept ID: `phys.em.electric-potential` · Difficulty: proficient · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 5h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to define electric potential V = U/q and potential difference as work per unit charge, compute point-charge potentials V = kq/r and superpose them as scalars, relate field and potential (E = −ΔV/Δd, equipotentials ⊥ E), and apply energy conservation qΔV = ΔKE to accelerated charges.

Electric potential is the work done per unit charge to bring a positive test charge from infinity to a given point.

Potential is electrostatics' energy landscape: where the field E says 'force per charge', the potential V says 'energy per charge' — V = U/q, in volts (1 V = 1 J/C), with potential DIFFERENCE ΔV the work per unit charge to move between points, the quantity every battery and outlet advertises. Point charges carpet space with V = kq/r (zero at infinity, positive hills around +q, negative wells around −q), and the great labour-saving fact is that potentials superpose as SCALARS — plain addition, no components — which is why energy methods often beat field methods exactly as they did in mechanics. The field-potential dictionary runs both ways: E points DOWNHILL in V (from high to low potential), with magnitude the steepness, E = −ΔV/Δd (V/m ≡ N/C — one unit, two costumes); equipotential surfaces (constant-V contours) cross field lines at right angles, and moving along them costs zero work — conductors in equilibrium are equipotential throughout, their surfaces the domain's natural contour lines. The workhorse application is energy conservation: a charge q falling through ΔV converts qΔV into kinetic energy (W = qΔV, ΔKE = |q|ΔV for a free charge) — electron guns, X-ray tubes, and accelerators are 'potential drops' cashed as speed, and the electron-volt (1 eV = 1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹ J) is this conversion minted as a unit. Signs carry the physics: positive charges fall toward low V, ELECTRONS fall toward HIGH V — the anti-field habit, now in energy dress.

**Key ideas**

- Definitions: V = U/q (volts = J/C); ΔV = work per unit charge between points — the battery's advertised number.
- Point charge: V = kq/r (zero at infinity), signed by the source; multiple sources ADD AS SCALARS — no components, the energy method's gift.
- Field-potential dictionary: E = −ΔV/Δd, pointing downhill in V; V/m and N/C are the same unit; steep contours = strong field.
- Equipotentials: constant-V surfaces ⊥ field lines; zero work along them; equilibrium conductors are equipotential (surfaces included).
- Energy workhorse: W = qΔV; free charges convert it — ΔKE = |q|ΔV; the electron-volt (1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹ J) is the atomic-scale currency.
- Sign discipline: positives roll toward low V, electrons toward HIGH V — the anti-field rule translated into the landscape; V can be negative (below the chosen zero) without drama.

**Vocabulary**

- **electric potential V** — Potential energy per unit charge (volts = J/C); point charge: V = kq/r, zero at infinity.
- **potential difference ΔV** — Work per unit charge between two points — the battery's number and the circuit's driver.
- **equipotential surface** — A constant-V surface, always perpendicular to E; zero work along it; conductors are equipotential in equilibrium.
- **electron-volt (eV)** — The energy gained by charge e through 1 V: 1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹ J — atomic physics' currency.
- **gradient relation** — E = −ΔV/Δd: the field is the downhill slope of the potential landscape (V/m ≡ N/C).

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* Potential and potential energy are the same thing.
  *Correction:* V is energy PER UNIT CHARGE — a property of the location; U = qV is the energy of a specific charge there. A point can sit at high V while a tiny charge has small U — and a negative charge has NEGATIVE U at positive V.
- *Misconception:* Where the field is zero the potential is zero (and vice versa).
  *Correction:* The dictionary relates E to V's SLOPE, not its value: midway between equal like charges E = 0 while V is large; inside a charged conductor E = 0 while V is constant and nonzero. Flat ≠ sea level.
- *Misconception:* Electrons drift from high potential to low, like everything else.
  *Correction:* POSITIVE charges fall toward low V; electrons, negative, fall toward HIGH V (their potential energy U = −eV decreases that way). The anti-field habit has an energy-landscape twin — half of circuit sign errors are born here.
- *Misconception:* Adding potentials needs vector components like adding fields.
  *Correction:* V is a scalar: signed plain addition, always. That is the method's entire advantage — superposing three charges' potentials is arithmetic where the field sum is trigonometry. Only slopes (E) need directions.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Vector-adding potentials or dropping source signs.
- Equating E = 0 with V = 0.
- Electrons sent toward low potential.
- Confusing V (per charge) with U (of a charge) — especially for negative charges.
- Unit slips between V/m and N/C (they are equal — use it, don't fear it).
- Forgetting work along equipotentials is zero.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- The landscape rendering: V as terrain — hills over +q, wells over −q — with a marble (positive test charge) rolling downhill along E.
- A contour-map overlay: equipotentials as contour lines with field-line 'fall lines' crossing at right angles — the hiker's map of electrostatics.
- A two-charge scalar-addition table at three points beside the same points' vector field addition — the labour contrast staged.
- An electron-gun schematic: cathode, anode, ΔV, with qΔV = ½mv² cashing the drop into speed.
- A charged conductor with its equipotential volume shaded flat and contours bunching at sharp points outside.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* (a) Find V at point P, 0.30 m from q₁ = +2.0 μC and 0.10 m from q₂ = −1.0 μC. (b) How much work moves a +5.0 nC charge from infinity to P? (c) An electron accelerates from rest through ΔV = 500 V in a display tube — find its kinetic energy in eV and joules and its final speed (mₑ = 9.1 × 10⁻³¹ kg).

1. (a) Scalar sum: V = kq₁/r₁ + kq₂/r₂ = 9 × 10⁹(2 × 10⁻⁶/0.30 − 1 × 10⁻⁶/0.10) = 9 × 10⁹(6.67 × 10⁻⁶ − 1.0 × 10⁻⁵) = −3.0 × 10⁴ V — no angles consulted.
2. (b) W = qV = 5 × 10⁻⁹ × (−3.0 × 10⁴) = −1.5 × 10⁻⁴ J — negative: the field does the work; the positive charge is pulled in (net attraction to the nearer −q).
3. (c) Energy: ΔKE = eΔV = 500 eV = 500 × 1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹ = 8.0 × 10⁻¹⁷ J.
4. Speed: v = √(2KE/m) = √(2 × 8.0 × 10⁻¹⁷/9.1 × 10⁻³¹) ≈ 1.3 × 10⁷ m/s — 4% of light speed from 500 V: electrons are cheap to hurl.
5. Sign audit: the electron fell toward HIGHER potential (cathode − to anode +), gaining KE — the anti-field rule cashing correctly.
6. Method recap: scalars for V, qV for energy, ½mv² to finish — no field components anywhere.

*Outcome:* The student computes V = −3.0 × 10⁴ V by scalar addition, the −1.5 × 10⁻⁴ J field-assisted work, and the 500 eV → 1.3 × 10⁷ m/s conversion with the electron's toward-high-V direction stated.

**Real-world intuition**

- Every battery, outlet, and USB port is specified in volts — potential difference as the retail unit of electricity.
- Electron guns in X-ray tubes and electron microscopes cash kilovolt drops into beam energy (keV language throughout).
- Particle accelerators from CRTs to the LHC are potential-difference ladders; the eV/MeV/GeV hierarchy is this concept's unit at scale.
- Nerve and cell physiology runs on ~70 mV membrane potentials — electrophysiology is potential bookkeeping.
- High-voltage engineering (insulator design, corona control) manages steep potential gradients — E = −ΔV/Δd as a safety code.

**Practice progression**

Item types: scalar superposition of point-charge potentials, work and energy computations (W = qΔV, eV conversions), field-potential dictionary items (E from V-slopes, equipotential sketching), accelerated-charge problems (guns, tubes, accelerators). Suggested item count: 12.

Begin with single- and multi-source V sums, add work/energy conversions, then dictionary and contour work, ending with acceleration chains and sign-trap items (electrons, negative V).

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, contour/diagram tasks, energy-chain problems. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must run scalar superposition, the E-V dictionary, and qΔV energy chains with full sign discipline on fresh configurations.

Mastery signal: The student computes superposed potentials and qΔV energies with correct signs (including one electron case), and translates between E and V correctly, at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

The landscape metaphor is the concept's engine — terrain, contours, marbles rolling downhill — and it pays twice: scalar superposition feels natural, and the E-V dictionary becomes map-reading. Stage the labour contrast (scalar V sum vs. vector E sum at one point) explicitly; students adopt energy methods when shown the saved trigonometry. Drill the two sign traps side by side (electrons toward high V; E = 0 ≠ V = 0). The eV deserves formal introduction here — modern physics will spend it constantly. Keep continuous distributions and formal gradients out; slopes and point charges suffice.

**Student notes**

Work the landscape: V = kq/r per source, added as SIGNED SCALARS — no components ever. Energy is U = qV and work is qΔV; free charges cash it as ½mv² (keep the eV shortcut loaded: charge e through ΔV volts = ΔV eV). Read fields as slopes: E points downhill in V, strong where contours crowd; along contours (and throughout conductors) V is constant and work is zero. Sign tattoos: positives roll downhill, electrons roll UPHILL in V, and flat (E = 0) is not sea level (V = 0).

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.gauss-law
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): phys.em.capacitance, phys.em.ohms-law
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: Potential is the electric twin of gravitational potential (phys.mech.gravitational-potential) — same landscape logic, richer signs — and the per-charge version of energy methods (phys.mech.conservation-of-energy). It prices capacitors next (phys.em.capacitance), drives currents through Ohm's law (phys.em.ohms-law), and its per-charge work definition is EMF's foundation (phys.em.emf); the eV becomes modern physics' unit (phys.mod).

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.gauss-law

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 2 days, 1 week, and 1 month: one scalar superposition, one qΔV energy chain (electron included), one contour sketch. Monthly, recite the dictionary both ways — E is V's downhill slope; V accumulates E along a path — and the two sign tattoos.

---

### Capacitance and Capacitors

*Concept ID: `phys.em.capacitance` · Difficulty: proficient · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 4h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to define capacitance C = Q/V (farads), derive the parallel-plate result C = ε₀A/d, combine capacitors in parallel (add C) and series (add 1/C), and analyze charge/voltage distribution in simple capacitor networks.

Capacitance measures a capacitor's ability to store electric charge; C = Q/V.

A capacitor stores charge and energy in an electric field: two conductors ('plates') separated by a gap, holding ±Q when a potential difference V spans them. Capacitance is the geometry's appetite for charge — C = Q/V, in farads (1 F = 1 C/V, so colossal that real devices live in μF, nF, pF) — a constant of the HARDWARE, not of the charging: double V and Q doubles, their ratio fixed. The parallel-plate archetype makes the geometry explicit: the gap's uniform field is E = σ/ε₀ = Q/ε₀A (the two plates' Gauss-plane fields superposing), giving V = Ed and hence C = ε₀A/d — bigger plates hold more, narrower gaps hold more (the field concentrates), the two dials of every capacitor datasheet. Networks combine by opposite rules to resistors (met later — flag the reversal now): PARALLEL capacitors share the same V and add their charges, so C_eq = C₁ + C₂ + … (plates effectively enlarged); SERIES capacitors carry the SAME charge Q (the linking conductor's induced ± must balance) while voltages add, so 1/C_eq = 1/C₁ + 1/C₂ — the equivalent smaller than the smallest, gaps effectively stacked. The two network invariants — same V in parallel, same Q in series — solve every distribution problem before algebra starts. Capacitors are electronics' short-term memory and timing element: camera flashes, defibrillators, smoothing filters, DRAM cells, and touchscreens all run on C = Q/V, with the stored ENERGY and the dielectric upgrade queued as the next two concepts.

**Key ideas**

- Definition: C = Q/V (farad = C/V) — the conductor pair's charge-per-volt appetite; fixed by geometry, not by how hard you charge.
- Parallel plates: E = Q/ε₀A uniform in the gap, V = Ed, so C = ε₀A/d — area grows appetite, gap starves it; the two design dials.
- Farads are huge: practical capacitors are μF–pF; a 1 F device was once a bench legend (supercapacitors changed that).
- Parallel combination: same V, charges add → C_eq = ΣC — bigger effective plate.
- Series combination: same Q (induced charges balance on the floating link), voltages add → 1/C_eq = Σ1/C — smaller than the smallest; the resistor rules' mirror image (flag now, collect at dc-circuits).
- Network invariants first: 'parallel shares V, series shares Q' — distribution problems fall to these two sentences plus Q = CV.

**Vocabulary**

- **capacitance C** — Charge stored per volt, C = Q/V (farads); a geometry constant — parallel plates: C = ε₀A/d.
- **farad (F)** — 1 C/V — so large that practice runs in μF, nF, pF.
- **parallel / series combination** — Same V, add C / same Q, add 1/C — the resistor rules mirrored.
- **network invariants** — 'Parallel shares V; series shares Q' — the two sentences that solve distributions.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* Capacitance depends on how much charge or voltage you apply.
  *Correction:* C is hardware: geometry (and dielectric, next). Doubling V doubles Q, leaving Q/V fixed — like a bucket's capacity not depending on the water poured. Only reshaping (A, d) or refilling the gap changes C.
- *Misconception:* A charged capacitor holds net charge Q.
  *Correction:* It holds +Q and −Q on facing plates: NET zero. 'The charge on a capacitor' names the magnitude on either plate — and the balanced ± is exactly why series capacitors must share one Q.
- *Misconception:* Series capacitors add their capacitances like series resistors add resistance.
  *Correction:* The rules MIRROR resistors: capacitors add in PARALLEL, reciprocal-add in SERIES (series stacks gaps, thinning the effective device). Memorize the reversal now, before resistors arrive to confuse it.
- *Misconception:* In a series string, the bigger capacitor takes the bigger voltage.
  *Correction:* Series capacitors share the same Q, so V = Q/C is LARGER on the SMALLER capacitor — the small one is the voltage hog (and the breakdown risk). Inverse, not proportional.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Treating C as charge- or voltage-dependent.
- Adding series capacitances directly.
- Giving the larger series capacitor the larger voltage.
- Forgetting the same-Q rule's origin (floating-link induction) and misassigning charges.
- μF/nF/pF conversion slips.
- Skipping the closing audit against C_eqV.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- A capacitor anatomy cutaway: plates, gap, ±Q, uniform field lines, with the C = ε₀A/d dials (A, d) as sliders driving a live C readout.
- The charging animation: electrons pumped from one plate to the other by a battery, ± building, V rising to match the source, current ceasing.
- A parallel-vs-series network diagram with the invariants stamped: same-V rail in parallel, same-Q chain in series, and the induced-charge argument on the floating link.
- The mirror-rules table (capacitors vs. resistors, held for later) as a two-column poster planted now.
- A capacitor zoo photo: electrolytics, ceramics, supercaps, a touchscreen layer — μF to farads in the flesh.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* (a) A parallel-plate capacitor has A = 0.020 m² and d = 1.0 mm — find C, and Q when charged to 12 V. (b) C₁ = 6.0 μF and C₂ = 3.0 μF are connected in series across 12 V — find C_eq, the charge, and each voltage. (c) The same pair in parallel across 12 V — find C_eq and each charge, and contrast.

1. (a) C = ε₀A/d = 8.85 × 10⁻¹² × 0.020/0.0010 = 1.77 × 10⁻¹⁰ F ≈ 177 pF; Q = CV = 177 pF × 12 ≈ 2.1 nC — picofarads and nanocoulombs: bench-scale reality.
2. (b) Series: 1/C_eq = 1/6 + 1/3 = 1/2 → C_eq = 2.0 μF (smaller than the smallest ✓).
3. Shared charge: Q = C_eqV = 24 μC on BOTH; voltages: V₁ = 24/6 = 4.0 V, V₂ = 24/3 = 8.0 V — the smaller capacitor hogs voltage; 4 + 8 = 12 ✓.
4. (c) Parallel: C_eq = 9.0 μF; shared V = 12 V on both; Q₁ = 72 μC, Q₂ = 36 μC — the bigger capacitor hogs charge; total 108 μC = 9 μF × 12 V ✓.
5. Contrast the invariants in action: series split V by inverse C at one Q; parallel split Q by C at one V — two sentences did all the work.
6. Audit: both nets checked against C_eqV — the closing habit for every network.

*Outcome:* The student computes 177 pF/2.1 nC, the series 2 μF with 24 μC and the 4/8 V inverse split, and the parallel 9 μF with the 72/36 μC proportional split — invariants stated before algebra.

**Real-world intuition**

- Camera flashes and defibrillators bank charge slowly and dump it in milliseconds — capacitors as energy sprinters.
- Power supplies smooth rectified AC with reservoir capacitors; every circuit board is peppered with decoupling caps.
- DRAM stores each bit as charge on a femtofarad capacitor — computing's memory is C = Q/V miniaturized.
- Touchscreens sense your fingertip as an added capacitance in a scanned grid.
- Supercapacitors bridge batteries and capacitors for regenerative braking and grid buffering.

**Practice progression**

Item types: C = Q/V and parallel-plate computations (all dials), series/parallel equivalents and distribution problems, invariant-reasoning items (who hogs V? who hogs Q?), mixed networks (one reduction step) and design questions. Suggested item count: 12.

Begin with definitional and plate computations, add two-capacitor networks of each type, then invariant-reasoning and mixed reductions, ending with design (choose A, d, topology for a target C).

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set with closing audits, network-analysis problems, design tasks. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must run the definition, the plate formula, and the network rules with the invariants leading, on fresh topologies.

Mastery signal: The student solves series and parallel distributions correctly (inverse-V and proportional-Q splits), reduces one mixed network, and audits totals, at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Anchor C as hardware with the bucket metaphor and the plate-formula dials; derive C = ε₀A/d from the Gauss plane result explicitly (collect the flagged σ/2ε₀ → σ/ε₀ superposition). The series same-Q argument (induced charge on the floating island) deserves its careful two minutes — it is the invariants' only non-obvious member. Plant the mirror-rules poster NOW, before resistors: taught in this order, the reversal immunizes rather than confuses. The smaller-cap-hogs-voltage result is the standard exam discriminator; drill it with the inverse logic said aloud.

**Student notes**

C is the device's appetite: Q = CV, with C set by geometry (ε₀A/d for plates — big and close is hungry). Networks: state the invariant first — parallel shares V (charges add, C's add); series shares Q (voltages add, 1/C's add, equivalent smaller than the smallest, and the SMALL cap takes the BIG voltage). Then Q = CV finishes everything. Audit totals at the end. And remember the plates hold ±Q — net zero, magnitude Q.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.electric-potential
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): phys.em.dielectrics, phys.em.energy-capacitor, phys.em.lc-circuits, phys.em.rc-circuits
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: Capacitance monetizes potential (phys.em.electric-potential) and the Gauss plane field (phys.em.gauss-law) into a device; energy storage (phys.em.energy-capacitor) and dielectrics (phys.em.dielectrics) upgrade it next. Its network rules mirror resistors (phys.em.dc-circuits), it times RC circuits (phys.em.rc-circuits), and it partners inductors in LC oscillation (phys.em.lc-circuits).

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.electric-potential

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 2 days, 1 week, and 1 month: one plate computation, one series and one parallel distribution with invariants recited, one mixed reduction. Monthly, re-derive C = ε₀A/d and the series same-Q argument — the two derivations that make the rules yours.

---

### Dielectrics and Polarization

*Concept ID: `phys.em.dielectrics` · Difficulty: advanced · Bloom level: understand · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 5h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to explain how a dielectric's polarization reduces the field inside a capacitor and multiplies capacitance by κ (C = κε₀A/d), distinguish the isolated-charge and connected-battery insertion cases, define dielectric strength and breakdown, and connect κ to molecular polarity.

Dielectrics are insulators that become polarized in an electric field, increasing capacitance by reducing the internal field.

Fill a capacitor's gap with an insulator and its capacitance MULTIPLIES: C = κε₀A/d, where κ (kappa) is the material's dielectric constant — 1 for vacuum, ~1.0006 for air, 2–3 for paper and plastics, ~80 for water. The mechanism is the dipole physics banked earlier: the applied field polarizes the dielectric (aligning permanent molecular dipoles or inducing them), and the material's surface acquires bound charge whose OWN field opposes the applied one — the net internal field drops to E₀/κ, so the same plate charge produces LESS voltage (V = V₀/κ), and Q/V grows κ-fold. Every dielectric question then splits by WHAT IS HELD FIXED, the concept's examinable fork: with the capacitor CHARGED AND ISOLATED (Q fixed), inserting the slab drops E and V by κ while Q stands — stored energy FALLS (the slab is sucked in, doing work); with the BATTERY CONNECTED (V fixed), the field must rebuild to V/d, so the battery pumps extra charge — Q rises κ-fold and stored energy RISES. Dielectrics also raise the ceiling: every insulator holds only so much field before it ionizes and arcs — its dielectric STRENGTH (air ≈ 3 × 10⁶ V/m: the spark threshold behind lightning gaps and the ~3 mm/10 kV rule) — and good fillers (mica, polymers, ceramics) both multiply C and withstand more volts, which is why real capacitors are dielectric sandwiches, not air gaps. Water's κ ≈ 80 is chemistry's solvent superpower: it collapses interionic Coulomb forces eighty-fold, dissolving salt — the same physics shelving ions instead of plates.

**Key ideas**

- Effect: C = κε₀A/d — the dielectric constant κ multiplies capacitance (air ~1, plastics 2–3, ceramics 10³ possible, water ~80).
- Mechanism: polarization → bound surface charge → opposing internal field → E = E₀/κ inside — the dipole concept industrialized.
- The fork (what's fixed?): isolated Q — V and E drop by κ, energy falls, slab pulled in; connected battery — V fixed, Q and energy rise κ-fold, battery pays.
- Dielectric strength: the maximum field before breakdown/arcing (air ≈ 3 MV/m) — the second datasheet number, setting voltage ratings.
- Real capacitors are dielectric sandwiches: κ multiplies C, strength raises the ceiling, thinness (d) exploits both — the engineering trifecta.
- κ is molecular: polar molecules (water!) polarize strongly — the same κ ≈ 80 that fattens capacitors dissolves salt by dividing interionic Coulomb forces.

**Vocabulary**

- **dielectric / dielectric constant κ** — An insulating gap-filler whose polarization multiplies capacitance: C = κε₀A/d (air ~1, water ~80).
- **polarization / bound charge** — The in-place alignment of molecular dipoles whose surface charge opposes the applied field (E → E₀/κ).
- **dielectric strength** — The breakdown field ceiling (air ≈ 3 MV/m) — sets a capacitor's voltage rating; distinct from κ.
- **the insertion fork** — Isolated (Q fixed: V, E, U fall) versus battery-connected (V fixed: Q, U rise) — declare the fixed variable first.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* Dielectrics work by conducting a little, letting charge cross the gap.
  *Correction:* Dielectrics are INSULATORS: no charge crosses. Their bound surface charge comes from in-place polarization — molecular dipoles rotating or stretching — which opposes the field without any transport. A conducting filler would short the capacitor, not improve it.
- *Misconception:* Inserting a dielectric always increases the stored energy — capacitance rose, after all.
  *Correction:* It depends on the fork: at fixed Q (isolated), U = Q²/2C FALLS by κ (the slab is pulled in, releasing energy); at fixed V (battery on), U = ½CV² RISES κ-fold — the battery supplies the difference plus the pumping work. State what is held fixed before touching the formulas.
- *Misconception:* The dielectric constant and dielectric strength are the same property.
  *Correction:* κ multiplies capacitance (a polarization measure); strength is the breakdown field ceiling (V/m). A material can be strong but low-κ (PTFE) or high-κ but fragile — datasheets quote both because engineering needs both.
- *Misconception:* The field inside the dielectric vanishes, as inside a conductor.
  *Correction:* It is REDUCED to E₀/κ, not killed: bound charge cannot fully cancel the applied field (only free conductor charge can). The dielectric tames the field; the conductor abolishes it — the distinction is the two materials' whole difference.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Solving fork problems without fixing the fixed variable (or mixing the two cases).
- Claiming energy always rises with C.
- Confusing κ with dielectric strength.
