- Letting the dielectric 'conduct' or fully cancel the field.
- Forgetting the battery's half-share ledger in the connected case.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- A polarization zoom: molecular dipoles aligning between plates, bound ± appearing on the slab faces, internal opposing field arrows netted to E₀/κ.
- The fork diagram: two identical charged capacitors, one isolated, one on a battery, slab inserted into each — Q, V, E, U scoreboards diverging.
- A slab being visibly pulled into a charged capacitor (demonstration or clip) — the energy drop as force.
- A datasheet card pair: κ and strength columns for air, paper, mica, ceramic, water — the two numbers distinguished.
- A salt-dissolving cartoon: water's κ ≈ 80 collapsing the Na⁺-Cl⁻ attraction — the capacitor physics as solvation.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A 200 pF air capacitor is charged to 100 V and ISOLATED. A slab with κ = 5.0 fills the gap. Find (a) Q before and after, (b) the new C, V, and E relative to before, (c) the stored energy before and after (U = Q²/2C). (d) Repeat (b)-(c) if instead the battery stays CONNECTED, and state who paid.

1. (a) Isolated: Q is locked at Q = CV = 200 pF × 100 V = 20 nC — before and after.
2. (b) C' = κC = 1000 pF; V' = Q/C' = 20 V (down ×5); E' = E/5 likewise — the bound charge tamed the field.
3. (c) U before = Q²/2C = (20 nC)²/(2 × 200 pF) = 1.0 μJ; after = (20 nC)²/(2 × 1000 pF) = 0.20 μJ — energy FELL ×5; the difference did work pulling the slab in.
4. (d) Battery case: V locked at 100 V; C' = 1000 pF → Q' = 100 nC (×5); U' = ½C'V² = 5.0 μJ (×5 the original 1.0 μJ).
5. Who paid: the battery drove 80 nC more through 100 V (8.0 μJ), of which 4.0 μJ landed in the capacitor and 4.0 μJ... audit: ΔU = 4 μJ, battery work 8 μJ — the other half went to pulling the slab in/other work: the classic half-share ledger.
6. Moral: the fork decides everything — same slab, same κ, opposite energy stories; always declare what is held fixed first.

*Outcome:* The student locks the right variable per case, computes the ×5 swings correctly (V↓, U↓ isolated; Q↑, U↑ connected), and runs the battery's half-share energy audit.

**Real-world intuition**

- Every practical capacitor is a dielectric sandwich — ceramic chip caps, film caps, and electrolytics are κ-and-strength engineering.
- Cable and transformer insulation is specified by dielectric strength; oil and SF₆ fill high-voltage gear to beat air's 3 MV/m.
- Water's κ ≈ 80 makes it life's solvent — ionic dissolution is Coulomb's law divided by kappa.
- Touchscreen and humidity sensors read κ changes (your finger, moisture) as capacitance shifts.
- DRAM's relentless shrink drove 'high-κ dielectric' materials into every processor — this concept as semiconductor roadmap vocabulary.

**Practice progression**

Item types: κ-multiplied capacitance and plate computations, fork problems (isolated vs. connected, full Q/V/E/U scoreboards), dielectric-strength and voltage-rating estimates, mechanism and material questions (polarization, κ vs. strength, water's κ). Suggested item count: 10.

Begin with κ-scaling computations, run both fork cases to full scoreboards, add strength/rating estimates, ending with mechanism explanations and the solvation connection.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: scoreboard problems (declare-the-fixed-variable required), computation set, conceptual quiz on mechanism and the two material numbers. Bloom alignment: Understand — students must explain the polarization mechanism and reason through the two insertion cases, with κ-scaling computations in support.

Mastery signal: The student completes both fork scoreboards correctly with the fixed variable declared, distinguishes κ from strength, and explains the bound-charge mechanism, at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Collect the dipole concept's deposit: polarization here is the statistical alignment planted there, now with a job. The FORK is the whole assessment surface — train the reflex 'what is held fixed?' as the first written line, and run both scoreboards side by side once in full. The slab-pulled-in demonstration (or clip) makes the isolated-case energy drop visceral. Keep κ and strength apart with the datasheet card. Water's double identity (capacitor filler / salt dissolver) is the connective tissue to chemistry — spend two minutes on it.

**Student notes**

A dielectric is an insulator that polarizes: bound surface charge fights the field, E drops to E₀/κ, and C jumps to κε₀A/d. Before computing anything, declare the fork: isolated means Q is king (V and U fall by κ; the slab gets sucked in); battery-connected means V is king (Q and U rise by κ; the battery pays double, half to the capacitor). Keep two datasheet numbers separate: κ (how much C multiplies) and strength (how many V/m before the arc). No charge ever crosses the gap.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.capacitance
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): none yet mapped
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: Dielectrics industrialize the dipole and polarization physics (phys.em.electric-dipole) inside the capacitor (phys.em.capacitance), re-pricing its energy (phys.em.energy-capacitor). Breakdown connects to sparks and lightning (phys.em.electric-charge applications); κ's molecular origin reaches chemistry's solvation; high-κ materials reach semiconductor engineering.

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.capacitance

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 2 days, 1 week, and 1 month: one κ-scaling computation, both fork scoreboards, one strength/rating estimate. Monthly, re-run the battery's energy audit in the connected case — the half-share ledger is the concept's subtlest and most examined arithmetic.

---

### Energy Stored in a Capacitor

*Concept ID: `phys.em.energy-capacitor` · Difficulty: proficient · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 3h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to compute the energy stored in a charged capacitor using U = ½CV² = Q²/2C = ½QV, choose the correct form for battery-connected versus isolated capacitors, explain the energy as residing in the electric field with density u = ½ε₀E², and analyze energy changes when capacitor geometry or dielectric changes.

The energy stored in a charged capacitor is U = ½CV² = Q²/2C, stored in the electric field between the plates.

Charging a capacitor costs work — each increment of charge must be pushed onto plates that already repel it — and that work is banked as stored energy: U = ½CV² = Q²/2C = ½QV. The three forms are one identity (via Q = CV), but each earns its keep in a different scenario: ½CV² when the VOLTAGE is pinned (battery stays connected), Q²/2C when the CHARGE is trapped (battery disconnected, plates isolated), ½QV when both are known. The factor ½ is the concept's first landmark: the battery does work QV in total, but only ½QV lands in the capacitor — because the plate voltage GROWS from 0 to V during charging, the average push is V/2 (graphically: the area of the triangle under the Q–V line, not the full rectangle). The other half is dissipated in the wiring resistance — an irreducible 50% charging tax for direct battery charging, independent of resistance value. The second landmark is WHERE the energy lives: not 'in the charge' but in the electric field filling the gap, with energy density u = ½ε₀E² (joules per cubic metre) — a claim that seems bookkeeping here but becomes physical fact when electromagnetic waves carry that field energy across empty space. The scenario game is the working skill: disconnect the battery and pull the plates apart — Q is fixed, C drops, so U = Q²/2C RISES (your muscles paid; the attracting plates resisted separation); do the same with the battery connected — V is fixed, C drops, U = ½CV² FALLS (charge and energy flow back into the battery). Capacitors store modest energies (millijoules to joules) compared to batteries, but release them FAST — that is their niche: camera flashes, defibrillators, pulsed lasers.

**Key ideas**

- U = ½CV² = Q²/2C = ½QV — three faces of one formula, linked by Q = CV; pick the form whose variable is held fixed in the scenario.
- The ½ comes from charging against a growing voltage: average push V/2, triangle area under the Q–V line — the battery supplies QV, and half is unavoidably lost in the wiring.
- Battery connected → V fixed → use ½CV²; battery disconnected → Q fixed → use Q²/2C — the single most-tested decision in the topic.
- The energy resides in the electric field itself, density u = ½ε₀E² per cubic metre of field — the gap, not the plates, is the tank.
- Pulling isolated plates apart raises U (work done against attraction); doing it battery-connected lowers U (charge retreats to the battery).
- Capacitors are low-energy, high-power storage: joules, not kilojoules — but deliverable in milliseconds (flash, defibrillator).

**Vocabulary**

- **stored energy U** — The work banked in charging a capacitor: U = ½CV² = Q²/2C = ½QV, held in the electric field of the gap.
- **energy density u** — Field energy per unit volume: u = ½ε₀E² in vacuum — the field itself is the reservoir.
- **charging efficiency (direct)** — Exactly 50% for resistive charging from a fixed battery: stored ½QV of the battery's QV, the rest heating the wiring regardless of resistance value.
- **fixed-Q vs fixed-V scenario** — Isolated capacitor pins charge (use Q²/2C); battery-connected pins voltage (use ½CV²) — the decision that governs every change problem.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* The battery's work QV all ends up stored in the capacitor.
  *Correction:* Only half does: U = ½QV. The capacitor's voltage grows from 0 to V during charging, so the average opposing voltage is V/2. The other ½QV is dissipated in the circuit resistance — and remarkably, this 50% loss is independent of how big that resistance is.
- *Misconception:* U = ½CV² and U = Q²/2C can be used interchangeably in change problems, since they're the same formula.
  *Correction:* They're equal only at a single instant. When C CHANGES, you must track which quantity stays constant: connected battery pins V (use ½CV²), isolation pins Q (use Q²/2C). Using the wrong form silently assumes the wrong invariant and flips the answer's direction.
- *Misconception:* Separating capacitor plates always decreases the stored energy, since capacitance decreases.
  *Correction:* For ISOLATED plates the energy INCREASES: Q is fixed and U = Q²/2C grows as C shrinks — your hands do positive work against the plates' mutual attraction, and that work becomes field energy filling the widened gap.
- *Misconception:* The energy is stored in the charges on the plates.
  *Correction:* The energy is stored in the electric FIELD between the plates, with density u = ½ε₀E². The field is a physical energy reservoir — the same accounting that lets light carry energy through vacuum, where there are no charges at all.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Using ½CV² with the old V after the battery was disconnected and C changed.
- Assuming stored energy always falls when C falls (true only at fixed V, false at fixed Q).
- Crediting the capacitor with the battery's full work QV.
- Dropping the square: U = ½CV², not ½CV.
- Unit slips with μF and mC — convert to SI before squaring.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- The Q–V charging triangle: a straight line V = Q/C, with the shaded triangle area ½QV (stored) versus the full rectangle QV (battery's work) — the missing half labelled 'heat in wires'.
- A two-scenario animation: plates pulled apart with battery connected (charge streaming back, U falling) versus isolated (charge trapped, field lines stretching, U rising).
- A field-energy shading of the gap: uniform E field rendered as a filled tank, u = ½ε₀E² per unit volume, doubling the gap doubling the tank at fixed E.
- A camera-flash circuit storyboard: slow charge from a small battery (seconds), fast dump through the flash tube (milliseconds) — same energy, wildly different power.
- An energy bar chart triptych for each scenario: battery work, capacitor energy, resistive loss — conservation audited in every case.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A 100 μF capacitor is charged by a 12 V battery. (a) Find the charge and stored energy. (b) Verify with U = Q²/2C. (c) The battery is disconnected and the plates are pulled apart until the capacitance halves to 50 μF. Find the new stored energy and account for the difference. (d) How much total work did the battery do during the original charging, and where did the rest go?

1. (a) Q = CV = 100 × 10⁻⁶ × 12 = 1.2 × 10⁻³ C. U = ½CV² = ½ × 100 × 10⁻⁶ × 12² = ½ × 10⁻⁴ × 144 = 7.2 × 10⁻³ J = 7.2 mJ.
2. (b) Cross-check: U = Q²/2C = (1.2 × 10⁻³)²/(2 × 100 × 10⁻⁶) = 1.44 × 10⁻⁶/2 × 10⁻⁴ = 7.2 × 10⁻³ J ✓ — the two forms agree at any instant.
3. (c) Battery disconnected → Q is FROZEN at 1.2 × 10⁻³ C. Use the Q-form: U' = Q²/2C' = 1.44 × 10⁻⁶/(2 × 50 × 10⁻⁶) = 1.44 × 10⁻⁶/10⁻⁴ = 1.44 × 10⁻² J = 14.4 mJ — the energy DOUBLED.
4. The extra 7.2 mJ is the work your hands did pulling the attracting plates apart — mechanical work converted to field energy in the enlarged gap. (Voltage check: V' = Q/C' = 24 V — halving C at fixed Q doubles V.)
5. (d) The battery's total work was W = QV = 1.2 × 10⁻³ × 12 = 14.4 mJ, of which only 7.2 mJ (exactly half) was stored; the other 7.2 mJ heated the connecting resistance — the universal 50% direct-charging tax.
6. Strategy replay: part (c) hinged on choosing Q²/2C because disconnection pins Q; had the battery stayed connected, V would be pinned, ½CV² would apply, and the energy would have HALVED to 3.6 mJ instead.

*Outcome:* The student computes Q = 1.2 mC and U = 7.2 mJ, verifies form-equivalence, correctly selects the fixed-Q form after disconnection to get 14.4 mJ, attributes the increase to mechanical work against plate attraction, and states the ½QV battery-loss accounting.

**Real-world intuition**

- Camera flashes charge a capacitor over seconds and dump ~10 J through the xenon tube in a millisecond — kilowatt-scale power from a pocket battery.
- Defibrillators store a few hundred joules in a high-voltage capacitor and deliver a controlled, life-restarting pulse through the chest.
- Supercapacitors bridge braking-energy recovery in buses and trams — fast-cycling energy banks that batteries are too slow for.
- Pulsed lasers and rail-launch research bank megajoules in capacitor arrays for microsecond release — the highest-power switches humans build.
- Computer power supplies use bulk capacitors to ride through millisecond mains dips, releasing stored ½CV² to keep the rails alive.

**Practice progression**

Item types: direct energy computations in all three forms (given any two of Q, V, C), scenario-split problems: same geometry change with battery connected versus disconnected, energy accounting: battery work versus stored energy versus resistive loss, field-energy-density items (u = ½ε₀E², energy from field volume). Suggested item count: 10.

Start with single-form plug-ins, add form selection from given quantities, then the connected/disconnected contrast pairs, ending with full energy audits (mechanical work + battery + heat) and energy-density integrations over the gap volume.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, scenario-contrast problems, energy-audit tasks. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must select the correct energy form for each constraint scenario and compute energy changes through geometry and connection changes, not merely recall the formulas.

Mastery signal: The student chooses ½CV² versus Q²/2C by identifying the pinned variable, computes energy changes with correct direction and magnitude, and closes the energy ledger (battery, field, heat, mechanical work) at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

The topic is one decision dressed in three formulas: WHAT IS PINNED? Drill the connected/disconnected contrast until it's reflexive — assign the same plate-separation problem both ways and have students explain why the answers move in opposite directions. Derive the ½ graphically (triangle under Q–V) rather than by calculus alone; the picture survives when the integral fades. The 50%-loss theorem (independent of R!) astonishes strong students — a worthwhile aside. Ground the field-energy claim physically: it is the down payment on electromagnetic waves, where field energy travels with no charges in sight. A charged-capacitor-and-neon-bulb demo (or defibrillator video) makes 'slow in, fast out' visceral.

**Student notes**

Memorize the trio U = ½CV² = Q²/2C = ½QV and the selection rule: battery connected → V fixed → ½CV²; disconnected → Q fixed → Q²/2C. Expect change problems to hinge entirely on that choice — same physical action, opposite energy direction. Remember the ½: the battery does QV but stores only half; the rest is heat. Pulling isolated plates apart RAISES the energy — you did the work. The energy sits in the field at u = ½ε₀E² per m³. Check magnitudes: real capacitors store mJ–J, released fast — that speed, not capacity, is their job.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.capacitance
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): none yet mapped
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: The energy forms extend capacitance (phys.em.capacitance) exactly as kinetic/potential energy extended force in mechanics (phys.mech.potential-energy) — and the ½ from a linearly growing opposition echoes ½kx² for springs (phys.mech.hookes-law). Field energy density u = ½ε₀E² is the electric half of the electromagnetic-wave energy budget (phys.em.electromagnetic-waves); RC charging dynamics (phys.em.rc-circuits) show WHEN the energy arrives, and dielectrics (phys.em.dielectrics) multiply what fits in the tank.

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.capacitance

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one three-form computation with cross-check, one connected-versus-disconnected contrast pair (opposite directions expected), one battery-work audit (QV in, ½QV stored, ½QV heat). Monthly, re-derive the charging triangle and restate where the energy physically lives — the field-energy idea returns at electromagnetic waves.

---

### Electric Current and Drift Velocity

*Concept ID: `phys.em.electric-current` · Difficulty: developing · Bloom level: understand · Mastery threshold: 0.75 · Estimated study time: 3h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to define electric current as the rate of charge flow I = Q/t = dQ/dt, state its SI unit and conventional direction, derive and apply the microscopic relation I = nAev_d, explain why drift velocities are tiny while signals travel near light speed, and distinguish drift from thermal motion.

Electric current is the rate of flow of charge; drift velocity is the average velocity of charge carriers under an electric field.

Electric current is charge on the move: I = Q/t (or dQ/dt for varying flow), measured in amperes — one ampere is one coulomb per second, and given the coulomb's enormity, an everyday ampere means ~6 × 10¹⁸ electrons crossing every second. Two conventions must be nailed at once. DIRECTION: conventional current points the way POSITIVE charge would flow — in metals, where electrons are the movers, current points opposite to the actual electron drift (a 200-year-old sign choice, kept because every law is written in it and it never causes error if used consistently). CARRIERS: electrons in metals, ions of both signs in electrolytes, electron–hole pairs in semiconductors. The microscopic picture is the concept's heart: a metal's free electrons are always racing thermally (~10⁵–10⁶ m/s) in random directions, netting zero; applying a field E superimposes a tiny organized DRIFT of average velocity v_d, and current is that drift times the carrier census: I = nAev_d, with n the free-electron number density (~10²⁸–10²⁹ per m³ for metals), A the cross-sectional area, e the elementary charge. Solving real numbers delivers the topic's great shock: in a household copper wire, v_d is a fraction of a MILLIMETRE per second — slower than a snail — yet the lamp lights the instant the switch closes, because the electric field that marshals the drift propagates along the wire at nearly light speed, setting the whole electron column moving at once (the marble-filled-tube picture: push one end, the far marble exits immediately, no marble crosses the tube). The enormous n rescues the tiny v_d: quadrillions of slow walkers still deliver coulombs per second. Current through a fixed cross-section is the same all along an unbranched wire — charge conservation in flow form, the seed of Kirchhoff's junction law.

**Key ideas**

- I = Q/t = dQ/dt, unit the ampere (1 A = 1 C/s ≈ 6 × 10¹⁸ electrons per second past a point).
- Conventional current points along positive-charge flow — opposite to electron drift in metals; consistent use makes the convention harmless.
- Microscopic law: I = nAev_d — carrier density × area × charge × drift speed; n ~ 10²⁸–10²⁹ m⁻³ in metals is the census that makes tiny drifts add up.
- Drift velocity is tiny (~0.1 mm/s in home wiring) while the field that organizes it travels near light speed — the switch-to-lamp paradox resolved: the whole electron column starts moving at once.
- Drift rides on top of huge random thermal motion (~10⁵ m/s netting zero) — the field adds a slight bias, not a stampede.
- In an unbranched conductor the current is the same at every cross-section — charge conservation flowing, the future junction rule.

**Vocabulary**

- **electric current I** — Rate of charge flow through a cross-section: I = Q/t = dQ/dt; SI unit the ampere (1 C/s).
- **conventional current** — The direction positive charge would flow — opposite to electron drift in metals; the frame all circuit laws are written in.
- **drift velocity v_d** — The tiny average velocity (~0.1 mm/s) the field superimposes on the electrons' random thermal motion; I = nAev_d.
- **carrier density n** — Free charge carriers per cubic metre (~8.5 × 10²⁸ m⁻³ for copper) — the census that makes slow drifts deliver amperes.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* Electrons race through the wire at nearly the speed of light — that's why the lamp lights instantly.
  *Correction:* The electrons DRIFT at ~0.1 mm/s; it is the electric FIELD (the signal) that propagates near light speed, setting electrons everywhere in the wire moving almost simultaneously. Push a full tube of marbles: the far one exits at once, though no marble sped through.
- *Misconception:* Current gets used up as it flows through a bulb or resistor.
  *Correction:* Current is conserved: the same current exits a series element as enters it — charge cannot pile up or vanish. What the bulb consumes is ENERGY (delivered per charge as potential difference), not charge. Same amperes, fewer joules per coulomb after.
- *Misconception:* In metals, positive charges flow in the direction of the current.
  *Correction:* In metals only electrons move; they drift OPPOSITE to the conventional current arrow. The arrow is a bookkeeping convention (the direction positive carriers WOULD move), retained from history — consistent use gives correct answers for every law in the domain.
- *Misconception:* With no applied voltage, the electrons in a wire sit still.
  *Correction:* They rush randomly at ~10⁵–10⁶ m/s from thermal energy alone — but with zero average, hence zero current. The applied field merely biases this chaos by a snail's-pace drift; current is organized motion, not motion itself.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Forgetting mm² → m² (answers off by 10⁶).
- Claiming electrons travel at light speed, or that one electron must run switch-to-lamp before it lights.
- Saying current is consumed by circuit elements.
- Mixing electron-flow and conventional-current frames within one problem.
- Confusing drift velocity with thermal speed (off by nine orders of magnitude).

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- A two-layer electron animation: frantic random thermal zigzags, then a field switched on adding a barely visible uniform drift — the bias, not the chaos, is the current.
- The marble-tube (or water-filled-hose) analogy animated: push at one end, immediate exit at the other, no individual marble traversing — signal speed versus carrier speed.
- A live I = nAev_d calculator: sliders for n, A, I showing v_d for copper at household currents landing under 1 mm/s.
- A conventional-current overlay: one wire drawn twice, electron drift arrows one way, conventional current arrow the other, laws labelled in the conventional frame.
- A cross-section counter: animated tally of charge crossing a wire slice per second, building 1 A = 1 C/s = 6.25 × 10¹⁸ electrons/s.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A copper wire of cross-sectional area 1.0 mm² carries a current of 1.7 A. Copper's free-electron density is n = 8.5 × 10²⁸ m⁻³. (a) Find the drift velocity of the electrons. (b) How long would one electron take to drift the 3.0 m from a switch to a lamp, and why does the lamp nonetheless light immediately? (c) How much charge, and how many electrons, pass a cross-section per minute?

1. (a) Convert area: A = 1.0 mm² = 1.0 × 10⁻⁶ m². Rearrange I = nAev_d: v_d = I/(nAe).
2. v_d = 1.7/(8.5 × 10²⁸ × 1.0 × 10⁻⁶ × 1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹) = 1.7/(1.36 × 10⁴) = 1.25 × 10⁻⁴ m/s ≈ 0.13 mm/s — literally slower than a snail.
3. (b) t = d/v_d = 3.0/1.25 × 10⁻⁴ = 2.4 × 10⁴ s ≈ 6.7 HOURS for one electron to make the trip.
4. Yet the lamp lights in nanoseconds: the field propagates along the wire at near light speed and sets the electrons already inside the lamp's filament drifting at once — the current starts everywhere almost simultaneously; no electron needs to arrive from the switch.
5. (c) Q = It = 1.7 × 60 = 102 C per minute; electron count = Q/e = 102/1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹ ≈ 6.4 × 10²⁰ electrons.
6. Sanity audit: a snail's-pace drift still delivers ~10²⁰ electrons a minute because n ~ 10²⁸ m⁻³ — the census, not the speed, carries the coulombs.

*Outcome:* The student computes v_d ≈ 1.25 × 10⁻⁴ m/s with correct unit conversion of mm², contrasts the hours-long single-electron transit with the near-instant field propagation to resolve the paradox, and converts current to charge and electron count over time.

**Real-world intuition**

- House wiring is sized by current: a 13 A circuit moves 13 coulombs per second through copper whose electrons drift slower than a minute hand.
- Electroplating and electrolysis run on ionic currents — chrome layers and aluminium smelting are I = Q/t turned into deposited atoms via charge counting.
- Nerve impulses are ionic currents across membranes — milliamps and milliseconds doing all of biology's signalling.
- Semiconductor engineering tunes n by doping: transistors work precisely because carrier density, hence current, is designable.
- Circuit breakers and fuses are current sentinels — they meter I continuously and open the circuit when the charge flow rate turns dangerous.

**Practice progression**

Item types: I = Q/t conversions (charge, electron counts, time), I = nAev_d computations solving for each variable in turn, paradox-explanation items (drift speed versus signal speed, current conservation along a wire), carrier-identification and direction items (metals, electrolytes, conventional versus electron flow). Suggested item count: 10.

Begin with I = Q/t and electron-count arithmetic, add nAev_d plug-ins with unit traps (mm² → m²), then comparative reasoning (same current, different area or n), ending with explanation tasks on the drift/signal distinction and current conservation.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, explanation prompts, conceptual quiz. Bloom alignment: Understand — students must explain the microscopic drift picture, the direction convention, and the drift-versus-signal-speed resolution, beyond plugging into I = Q/t.

Mastery signal: The student computes drift velocities with correct unit handling, explains why tiny v_d coexists with instant response and why current is conserved along a wire, and applies the conventional-direction rule without sign confusion, at 75% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Lead with the paradox — compute v_d for real copper FIRST, get the class to gasp at 0.1 mm/s, then resolve it with the marble tube. That single calculation inoculates against the 'electrons zoom at light speed' folklore better than any lecture. Be scrupulously consistent about conventional current from day one and say explicitly that electron-flow diagrams aren't wrong, just a different frame — then use conventional exclusively. 'Current gets used up' is the domain's most stubborn error; preempt it with the series-ammeter question (same reading both sides of the bulb) before circuits arrive. The mm² → m² conversion (×10⁻⁶) is the topic's arithmetic landmine — drill it.

**Student notes**

Current is a RATE: I = Q/t, amperes = coulombs per second. Learn I = nAev_d and respect the units — 1 mm² = 10⁻⁶ m², the classic trap. Expect v_d to come out absurdly small (~10⁻⁴ m/s) and know the resolution: the field, not the electrons, races down the wire; all electrons start drifting at once. The current arrow follows positive flow — electrons go the other way; pick the convention and never mix frames mid-problem. Current is NEVER used up along a series path — energy is. No voltage means zero CURRENT, not zero motion: thermal chaos always rages, it just averages to nothing.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.electric-charge
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): phys.em.magnetic-field, phys.em.ohms-law
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: Current is charge (phys.em.electric-charge) in motion, pushed by potential difference (phys.em.electric-potential) — Ohm's law (phys.em.ohms-law) prices the push per flow next. Current conservation along a wire seeds Kirchhoff's junction law (phys.em.kirchhoffs-laws); moving charge is also the source of magnetism (phys.em.magnetic-field), making this concept the bridge from electro- to -magnetism. The random-plus-drift picture echoes kinetic theory (phys.therm.kinetic-theory).

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.electric-charge

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one I = Q/t electron-count, one nAev_d computation with the mm² conversion, one paradox explanation in your own words (drift speed vs signal speed). Monthly, re-answer 'is current used up in a bulb?' with the conservation argument — it audits every circuit problem to come.

---

### Ohm's Law and Resistance

*Concept ID: `phys.em.ohms-law` · Difficulty: developing · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 3h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to state Ohm's law V = IR, define resistance and the ohm, compute any of V, I, R from the other two, interpret I–V graphs to distinguish ohmic from non-ohmic behavior, and explain resistance microscopically as carrier–lattice collisions.

Ohm's law states that the current through a conductor is proportional to the voltage across it: V = IR.

Ohm's law is the tariff of the circuit world: the potential difference needed to drive a current through a conductor is proportional to that current, V = IR, with the resistance R (unit: the ohm, Ω = volt per ampere) as the price per ampere. Read it as cause and effect — the battery's potential difference V is the push, the current I the response, and R the conductor's opposition: I = V/R says doubling the push doubles the flow, doubling the opposition halves it. Microscopically, resistance is friction by collision: drifting electrons repeatedly slam into the vibrating ion lattice, surrendering their field-won momentum as heat — which is why resistance exists, why it converts electrical energy to warmth, and why it grows with temperature (hotter lattice, more violent vibrations, more frequent collisions — the graded prediction: metal resistance rises with T). Two readings of the law must be separated with care. As a DEFINITION, R = V/I can be computed for anything at any operating point. As a LAW, Ohm's claim is stronger: for metals at steady temperature, R is CONSTANT — the I–V graph is a straight line through the origin, and such materials are called ohmic. Non-ohmic devices break the law while obeying the definition: a filament lamp's I–V curve bends over (self-heating raises R as I grows), a diode conducts one way only, a thermistor's R plunges with temperature. The graph is the diagnostic: straight-through-origin = ohmic; anything curved, kinked, or asymmetric = non-ohmic, with R = V/I still computable point by point but no longer a single number. Practically, Ohm's law is the first tool of circuit analysis — every series/parallel network, Kirchhoff loop, and power computation to come leans on V = IR at the component level.

**Key ideas**

- V = IR: potential difference = current × resistance; equivalently I = V/R (response = push/opposition) — one relation, three rearrangements.
- The ohm: 1 Ω = 1 V/A — the resistance that lets one ampere flow per volt applied.
- Microscopic origin: drifting electrons collide with the vibrating ion lattice, converting field energy to heat — resistance IS that friction, and it rises with temperature in metals.
- Definition versus law: R = V/I is always computable; OHMIC means R is constant (straight I–V line through the origin) — a property of metals at fixed temperature, not of all devices.
- Non-ohmic gallery: filament lamp (curve flattens as self-heating raises R), diode (one-way conduction), thermistor (R falls with T) — the I–V graph is the identification tool.
- V = IR is per-component: applied across each element of any network, it is the atom of all circuit analysis to come.

**Vocabulary**

- **Ohm's law** — For metallic conductors at constant temperature, V = IR with R constant — the I–V graph is a straight line through the origin.
- **resistance R** — Opposition to current flow: R = V/I; unit the ohm (Ω = V/A). Microscopically, electron–lattice collision friction.
- **ohmic / non-ohmic** — Ohmic: R constant, straight I–V line (metals at fixed T). Non-ohmic: R varies with operating point (lamp, diode, thermistor).
- **I–V characteristic** — The current-versus-voltage graph of a device — the diagnostic fingerprint that reveals ohmic or non-ohmic identity.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* R = V/I means resistance depends on the voltage and current you apply.
  *Correction:* For an ohmic conductor, R is a fixed property of the object (material, geometry, temperature); V/I merely MEASURES it, and returns the same value at every operating point. Doubling V doubles I, leaving the ratio — the resistance — unchanged.
- *Misconception:* All conductors obey Ohm's law.
  *Correction:* Ohm's law is an empirical rule for metals at steady temperature, not a law of nature. Filament lamps, diodes, thermistors, and electrolytes are non-ohmic: their I–V graphs are curved or asymmetric, and R = V/I changes with the operating point.
- *Misconception:* Current flows through a resistor because the resistor 'lets it through' — voltage just measures how much got through.
  *Correction:* Causality runs push → flow: the potential difference ACROSS the conductor drives the current THROUGH it. No V, no I. The resistor doesn't gate a pre-existing current; the applied V and the R jointly determine I = V/R.
- *Misconception:* A bigger resistor always has more voltage across it, whatever the circuit.
  *Correction:* V = IR needs the CURRENT through that specific element. In series (same I) the bigger R does take more V — but in parallel every branch has the SAME V and the bigger R simply carries less current. The law is per-component; the circuit decides what's shared.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Treating all devices as ohmic and extrapolating straight lines through curved data.
- Reading R as depending on applied V for ordinary resistors.
- Rearrangement slips (I = VR, R = IV).
- Applying one element's V with another element's I in multi-component circuits.
- Forgetting that R rises with temperature in metals — predictions overestimate current in heating elements.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- An I–V graph gallery: ohmic metal (straight through origin), filament lamp (flattening curve), diode (one-way knee) — side by side with 'compute R = V/I at two points' annotations.
- A collision animation: electrons accelerating in the field, slamming into vibrating lattice ions, the vibration (heat) growing — then the same at higher temperature with denser collisions.
- A water-slope analogy diagram: height difference (V) driving flow (I) through a gravel channel (R) — steeper slope, more flow; coarser gravel, less.
- A live V = IR triangle calculator: cover any one of V, I, R and read the formula for it; sliders showing proportionality and inverse proportionality.
- A filament-lamp measurement storyboard: ammeter/voltmeter readings at increasing V, R = V/I computed and RISING — non-ohmic behavior discovered from data.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A nichrome heating element draws 0.50 A when connected across 6.0 V. (a) Find its resistance. (b) Predict the current at 9.0 V, assuming ohmic behavior. (c) At 9.0 V the measured current is only 0.70 A, not the predicted value. Compute the actual resistance at this operating point and explain the discrepancy. (d) Sketch the expected I–V graph shape.

1. (a) R = V/I = 6.0/0.50 = 12 Ω.
2. (b) Ohmic prediction: I = V/R = 9.0/12 = 0.75 A — a straight-line extrapolation through the origin.
3. (c) Actual: R = V/I = 9.0/0.70 ≈ 12.9 Ω — the resistance has RISEN by about 7%.
4. Explanation: the higher current heats the wire; hotter lattice ions vibrate harder, electrons collide more often, so R grows with temperature. The element is slightly non-ohmic in operation — ohmic only at constant temperature.
5. (d) The I–V graph starts as a straight line near the origin (cool wire, R ≈ 12 Ω) and bends progressively toward the V-axis as self-heating raises R — the filament-lamp signature, mild here.
6. Method note: both parts used R = V/I — the DEFINITION always works point by point; what failed at 9 V was the LAW's promise that R stays constant.

*Outcome:* The student computes R = 12 Ω, makes the ohmic prediction 0.75 A, recomputes R ≈ 12.9 Ω from data, attributes the rise to temperature-driven lattice collisions, and articulates the definition-versus-law distinction with the correct graph shape.

**Real-world intuition**

- Every appliance rating plate is an Ohm's-law statement: a 230 V, 5 A heater is a 46 Ω element by design.
- Fuses and circuit breakers exploit V = IR sizing: fault resistance collapses, current spikes past the rating, and the protector opens.
- Electric kettles, toasters, and heaters are engineered resistances — nichrome chosen for stable R and heat tolerance.
- Multimeters measure resistance by applying a tiny known V and reading I — R = V/I automated in every technician's pocket.
- Thermistors and light-dependent resistors run thermostats and streetlights precisely by BREAKING Ohm's law — their R shifts with temperature or light, converting environment into signal.

**Practice progression**

Item types: V = IR computations solving for each variable, I–V graph interpretation (identify ohmic/non-ohmic, extract R from slope or point ratios), microscopic-explanation items (collisions, temperature dependence), prediction-versus-measurement problems exposing non-ohmic behavior. Suggested item count: 10.

Begin with single-step V = IR rearrangements, add graph reading and slope-based R extraction, then temperature reasoning and non-ohmic identification, ending with prediction-discrepancy analysis like the worked example.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, graph-interpretation tasks, explanation prompts. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must use V = IR fluently across components and operating points, and apply the ohmic/non-ohmic distinction to graphs and measured data, beyond reciting the formula.

Mastery signal: The student solves for any of V, I, R in context, reads I–V graphs correctly (including identifying non-ohmic curvature and its temperature cause), and applies the law per-component with series/parallel awareness, at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Separate the DEFINITION (R = V/I, always usable) from the LAW (R constant for metals at fixed T) explicitly — conflating them is the root of most student trouble, and the filament-lamp practical is the cleanest cure: let them measure R rising with V and discover non-ohmic behavior from their own data. Push the causal reading (V drives I through R) over the algebraic one; students who see V = IR as mere symbol-shuffling misassign cause in circuit reasoning later. The water-slope analogy is serviceable but flag its limits before misconceptions ossify. Keep the microscopic collision story present — it explains heating, temperature dependence, AND sets up resistivity next.

**Student notes**

One relation, three forms: V = IR, I = V/R, R = V/I — know all three cold. The ohm is a volt per ampere. For metals at steady temperature R is FIXED: double V, double I. But check the fine print — lamps, diodes, and thermistors are non-ohmic, and the I–V graph tells you: straight through the origin = ohmic, curved = not. When R = V/I gives different values at different points, that's non-ohmic behavior (usually self-heating), not an arithmetic error. Apply V = IR per component: in series elements share I, in parallel they share V — never assume both.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.electric-current, phys.em.electric-potential
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): phys.em.ac-basics, phys.em.dc-circuits, phys.em.electrical-power, phys.em.resistivity
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: Ohm's law fuses current (phys.em.electric-current) with potential difference (phys.em.electric-potential) into the workhorse of circuit analysis: series/parallel networks (phys.em.dc-circuits), power P = VI = I²R (phys.em.electrical-power), and resistivity's geometry scaling (phys.em.resistivity) all build directly on it, while Kirchhoff's laws (phys.em.kirchhoffs-laws) deploy it loop by loop. The collision picture connects to kinetic theory (phys.therm.kinetic-theory) and heating to energy conservation (phys.mech.conservation-of-energy).

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.electric-current
- phys.em.electric-potential

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one three-way V-I-R computation, one I–V graph identification (ohmic vs lamp vs diode), one temperature-dependence explanation via collisions. Monthly, restate the definition-versus-law distinction in one sentence each — it is the concept's examinable heart and the guard against extrapolating non-ohmic devices.

---

### Resistivity and Conductivity

*Concept ID: `phys.em.resistivity` · Difficulty: proficient · Bloom level: understand · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 3h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to separate material from geometry via R = ρL/A, define resistivity and conductivity σ = 1/ρ with correct units, compute resistance from wire dimensions, rank materials by resistivity class (conductor/semiconductor/insulator), and analyze reshaping problems such as wire stretching at constant volume.

Resistivity is a material property relating resistance to geometry; conductivity is its reciprocal.

Resistance conflates two things — what a conductor is made of and how it is shaped — and resistivity surgically separates them: R = ρL/A, where ρ (rho) is the MATERIAL's intrinsic opposition (unit: Ω·m) and L/A is the GEOMETRY's contribution. The shape logic is plumbing-intuitive: longer wire, more lattice to collide through, R ∝ L; fatter wire, more parallel lanes for the drift, R ∝ 1/A. What remains when geometry is divided out is ρ, a material fingerprint spanning the grandest range in applied physics: ~10⁻⁸ Ω·m for good metals (copper 1.7 × 10⁻⁸, silver a shade lower, nichrome ~10⁻⁶ for deliberate heating), ~10⁰–10³ for semiconductors like silicon, up to ~10¹⁶ for glass and polymers — twenty-four orders of magnitude between the wire and its insulation, the widest useful property spread in engineering. Conductivity σ = 1/ρ (unit: S/m, siemens per metre) is the same fact spoken as ease rather than opposition. Temperature sorts materials by mechanism: metals' ρ RISES with T (hotter lattice, more collisions — ρ ≈ ρ₀[1 + α(T − T₀)] with α ~ 0.004/K, the basis of resistance thermometers), while semiconductors' ρ FALLS with T (heat liberates new carriers, swamping the collision penalty — the thermistor's secret). The topic's signature puzzle is reshaping at constant volume: stretch a wire to double length and its area halves (V = LA fixed), so R' = ρ(2L)/(A/2) = 4R — resistance scales as the SQUARE of the stretch factor (R ∝ L²/V), a beloved exam trap that tests whether L and A are tracked jointly. Resistivity is why household copper is fat and short-run, why transmission lines are aluminium (cheap conductance per kilogram), and why the heating element and its insulating sheath can share one appliance.

**Key ideas**

- R = ρL/A: resistance = material fingerprint × geometry — longer means more R, fatter means less.
- Resistivity ρ (Ω·m) is intrinsic to the material; resistance R (Ω) belongs to a specific object. Conductivity σ = 1/ρ (S/m) is the reciprocal view.
- The resistivity ladder spans ~24 orders of magnitude: metals ~10⁻⁸, semiconductors ~10⁰–10³, insulators up to ~10¹⁶ Ω·m — conductor and insulator can share one cable.
- Metals: ρ rises with temperature (ρ ≈ ρ₀[1 + αΔT], α ~ 0.004/K) — collision friction grows; semiconductors: ρ FALLS with T — thermal carrier creation wins. Opposite signs, different mechanisms.
- Constant-volume stretching: double L → halve A → quadruple R (R ∝ stretch² at fixed volume) — track both variables or lose the factor.
- Design consequences: copper/silver for wiring, nichrome for heaters, aluminium for transmission (conductance per kilogram), doped silicon where ρ must be TUNED.

**Vocabulary**

- **resistivity ρ** — Intrinsic material opposition to current: R = ρL/A; unit Ω·m. Copper ~1.7 × 10⁻⁸, glass ~10¹⁶.
- **conductivity σ** — Reciprocal of resistivity, σ = 1/ρ; unit siemens per metre (S/m) — opposition restated as ease.
- **temperature coefficient α** — Fractional resistivity rise per kelvin for metals: ρ ≈ ρ₀[1 + αΔT], α ~ 0.004/K — the resistance-thermometer principle.
- **constant-volume reshaping** — Stretching or recasting a wire keeps V = LA fixed, coupling L and A: stretch factor s multiplies R by s².

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* Resistivity and resistance are the same thing with different names.
  *Correction:* Resistance belongs to an OBJECT (this wire: 1.7 Ω); resistivity belongs to a MATERIAL (copper: 1.7 × 10⁻⁸ Ω·m, whatever the shape). Cut a wire in half and R halves, but ρ is untouched — no geometry change can alter what the metal is.
- *Misconception:* Stretching a wire to double length doubles its resistance.
  *Correction:* It QUADRUPLES it. Volume is conserved, so doubling L halves A, and R = ρL/A gains a factor 2 from the numerator AND a factor 2 from the denominator: R' = 4R. Reshaping problems must track L and A together.
- *Misconception:* A thicker wire has more resistance because there's more material to push through.
  *Correction:* Thicker means MORE parallel paths for the drifting electrons, so resistance FALLS: R ∝ 1/A. It's length, not bulk, that adds opposition — a fat short wire beats a thin long one every time.
- *Misconception:* Heating any material raises its resistivity.
  *Correction:* Only metals (more violent lattice vibrations → more collisions). Semiconductors run the OTHER way: heat promotes new charge carriers, and ρ falls steeply — that opposite signature is exactly how thermistors sense temperature and why silicon devices misbehave when hot.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Doubling R for a doubled stretch length (forgetting the halved area — correct factor 4).
- Writing resistivity units as Ω/m or Ω·m⁻¹ instead of Ω·m.
- Using resistivity and resistance interchangeably in comparisons.
- Applying the metal temperature rule to semiconductors (sign is opposite).
- Skipping the mm² → m² conversion, inflating answers by 10⁶.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- A geometry-slider diagram: one material, adjustable L and A, with R = ρL/A recomputed live — doubling L versus doubling A pulling R opposite ways.
- The resistivity ladder: a log-scale tower from silver (10⁻⁸ Ω·m) through silicon to glass (10¹⁶), with everyday objects pinned at their rungs.
- The stretching animation: a wire drawn to 2L with its cross-section visibly thinning to A/2, the R readout climbing ×4 — volume conservation on screen.
- A two-curve temperature plot: metal ρ(T) rising gently and linearly, semiconductor ρ(T) plunging — annotated with the collision versus carrier-creation mechanisms.
- A cable cross-section cutaway: copper core (ρ ~ 10⁻⁸) inside polymer sheath (ρ ~ 10¹⁴) — 22 orders of magnitude in one centimetre.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A copper wire (ρ = 1.7 × 10⁻⁸ Ω·m) is 100 m long with cross-sectional area 1.0 mm². (a) Find its resistance. (b) The same wire is uniformly stretched to double its length (volume constant). Find the new resistance. (c) What length of nichrome wire (ρ = 1.1 × 10⁻⁶ Ω·m) of the same 1.0 mm² area would match the ORIGINAL copper wire's resistance?

1. (a) Convert: A = 1.0 mm² = 1.0 × 10⁻⁶ m². R = ρL/A = (1.7 × 10⁻⁸ × 100)/1.0 × 10⁻⁶ = 1.7 × 10⁻⁶/10⁻⁶ = 1.7 Ω — a hundred metres of household copper barely reaches 2 Ω.
2. (b) Volume fixed: L' = 2L = 200 m forces A' = A/2 = 0.5 × 10⁻⁶ m². R' = ρL'/A' = (1.7 × 10⁻⁸ × 200)/(0.5 × 10⁻⁶) = 3.4 × 10⁻⁶/5 × 10⁻⁷ = 6.8 Ω = 4R.
3. Shortcut check: R ∝ L² at constant volume, so (2)² = 4 → 4 × 1.7 = 6.8 Ω ✓ — the double hit (longer AND thinner) is the whole point.
4. (c) Set ρ_n L_n/A = 1.7 Ω: L_n = RA/ρ_n = (1.7 × 1.0 × 10⁻⁶)/1.1 × 10⁻⁶ ≈ 1.5 m.
5. Read the contrast: 100 m of copper ≡ 1.5 m of nichrome — a resistivity ratio of ~65 made tangible; this is why heating elements are short nichrome coils, not copper spools.
6. Sanity audit: units resolve as (Ω·m × m)/m² = Ω throughout; and the mm² → m² conversion (×10⁻⁶) was applied once, up front, where it can't be forgotten.

*Outcome:* The student computes 1.7 Ω with correct area conversion, quadruples it to 6.8 Ω by jointly tracking L and A under volume conservation, and solves the inverse problem to find ~1.5 m of nichrome, articulating the material-versus-geometry separation.

**Real-world intuition**

- Electrical codes size wire gauge by R = ρL/A: long runs and high currents demand fatter copper to keep voltage drop and heating within limits.
- Overhead transmission lines are aluminium, not copper — slightly higher ρ but far better conductance per kilogram and per rupee at grid scale.
- Heating elements (kettles, toasters, hair dryers) use nichrome precisely FOR its high, stable resistivity — concentrated heat in a short coil.
- Platinum resistance thermometers read temperature from ρ(T)'s linear rise — the metal coefficient α turned into an instrument.
- Strain gauges measure deformation via the stretching effect itself: elongate a foil track and its resistance climbs by the L²/V scaling — bridges and aircraft wings are wired with them.

**Practice progression**

Item types: R = ρL/A computations solving for each variable (with unit-conversion traps), reshaping problems (stretching, folding in half, recasting) at constant volume, material-comparison and ranking items across the resistivity ladder, temperature-dependence items (metal versus semiconductor, resistance-thermometer arithmetic). Suggested item count: 10.

Begin with direct plug-ins and unit conversion, add solve-for-L/A inversions and material comparisons, then constant-volume reshaping (the ×4 trap and its cousins), ending with temperature-coefficient computations and mechanism explanations.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, reshaping problems, conceptual quiz. Bloom alignment: Understand — students must explain the material/geometry separation, the resistivity ladder, and the opposite temperature behaviors of metals and semiconductors, beyond substituting into R = ρL/A.

Mastery signal: The student computes R from dimensions with clean unit handling, solves constant-volume reshaping with the squared scaling, ranks materials by resistivity class, and explains both temperature mechanisms, at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

The concept is one clean separation — material versus geometry — and the fastest way to teach it is to violate expectations: ask which has more resistance, 100 m of copper or 1.5 m of nichrome, and let the worked example's ~65× resistivity ratio land. The stretching trap (×4, not ×2) deserves a full class treatment with the volume-conservation drawing; it recurs on every board exam. Keep the two temperature mechanisms rigorously apart — metals (collisions ↑) versus semiconductors (carriers ↑) — because thermistor questions punish the conflation. The 24-orders-of-magnitude ladder is genuinely awe-inspiring; a log-scale poster earns its wall space. Unit discipline: Ω·m, not Ω/m — the m comes from A/L, and dimensional analysis of R = ρL/A settles it.

**Student notes**

Separate the two Rs: resistance (this object, in Ω) versus resistivity (this material, in Ω·m). Master R = ρL/A both ways — longer thinner wires resist more; shorter fatter ones less. The exam classic: stretching to n× length at constant volume multiplies R by n² (length up AND area down) — never answer 2× for a doubled length. Know the ladder by class: metals 10⁻⁸, semiconductors mid-range, insulators 10¹⁶ Ω·m. Temperature: metal ρ rises (collisions), semiconductor ρ falls (new carriers) — opposite signs, both examinable. Always convert mm² to m² (×10⁻⁶) before computing.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.ohms-law
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): none yet mapped
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: Resistivity factors Ohm's law (phys.em.ohms-law) into material and shape, and its collision mechanism deepens the drift picture of current (phys.em.electric-current). The metal ρ(T) rise powers resistance thermometry (cf. temperature scales, phys.therm.temperature), while the semiconductor opposite prepares modern-physics band ideas (phys.mod). Geometry scaling R ∝ L/A echoes the parallel-plate C ∝ A/d of capacitance (phys.em.capacitance) — the domain prices geometry twice, in mirror image.

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.ohms-law

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one R = ρL/A computation with unit conversion, one constant-volume stretch (state the n² rule before computing), one metal-versus-semiconductor temperature explanation. Monthly, redraw the resistivity ladder from memory with one material per class — it anchors every conductor/insulator judgement in circuits and beyond.

---

### Series and Parallel Circuits

*Concept ID: `phys.em.dc-circuits` · Difficulty: developing · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 4h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to compute equivalent resistance for series and parallel combinations, apply the shared-current rule for series and the shared-voltage rule for parallel, reduce mixed networks stage by stage, and predict brightness and meter readings in simple DC circuits.

In series circuits the same current flows through all components; in parallel circuits the same voltage appears across each branch.

Circuits are built from two grammatical structures, and everything follows from what each one SHARES. SERIES elements lie on one path: the same CURRENT threads them all (nowhere else for charge to go), voltages add along the chain, and resistances add — R_eq = R₁ + R₂ + ... — so every added element raises the total opposition and the equivalent exceeds the largest member. PARALLEL elements bridge the same two nodes: the same VOLTAGE spans each branch, currents add at the junction, and reciprocals of resistance add — 1/R_eq = 1/R₁ + 1/R₂ + ... — so every added branch opens a new lane, the equivalent DROPS below the smallest member, and for the beloved two-resistor case R_eq = R₁R₂/(R₁ + R₂) (product over sum), with N equal resistors giving R/N. The physical logic beats memorization: series stacks obstacles along one road; parallel adds roads. Working circuits is a two-pass craft: REDUCE the network stage by stage (collapse obvious series chains and parallel bundles until one equivalent faces the battery, giving the total current), then EXPAND back out, assigning currents and voltages at each stage using the sharing rules — series members inherit the current and split the voltage in proportion to resistance (the voltage divider: V_i = V·R_i/ΣR); parallel members inherit the voltage and split the current in inverse proportion (more current takes the easier path, but every path takes some). The classic intuitions all fall out: bulbs in series share the push and dim (and one failure darkens all — old fairy lights); bulbs in parallel each get the full push and burn at full brightness independently (house wiring); an ammeter goes in series (low resistance, must not add opposition), a voltmeter in parallel (high resistance, must not steal current).

**Key ideas**

- Series: one path, same CURRENT through all, voltages add, R_eq = R₁ + R₂ + ... — always bigger than the biggest member.
- Parallel: same two nodes, same VOLTAGE across all, currents add, 1/R_eq = Σ1/R_i — always smaller than the smallest member; two-resistor shortcut R₁R₂/(R₁+R₂); N equal → R/N.
- Roads picture: series stacks obstacles on one road; parallel opens new roads — more branches, easier flow, whatever their size.
- Solve by reduce-then-expand: collapse to one equivalent for total current, then walk back out applying the sharing rules stage by stage.
- Dividers: series splits VOLTAGE in proportion to R (V_i = V·R_i/ΣR); parallel splits CURRENT in inverse proportion to R — every branch carries some.
- Applications of the grammar: house wiring is parallel (full voltage, independent switching); ammeters sit in series (tiny R), voltmeters in parallel (huge R).

**Vocabulary**

- **series combination** — Elements on one path: same current through all, voltages add, R_eq = ΣR_i.
- **parallel combination** — Elements bridging the same two nodes: same voltage across all, currents add, 1/R_eq = Σ1/R_i.
- **equivalent resistance** — The single resistance that draws the same current from the source as the whole network — found by staged reduction.
- **voltage divider** — Series resistors splitting a source voltage in proportion to resistance: V_i = V·R_i/ΣR.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* Adding a resistor always increases the circuit's total resistance.
  *Correction:* Only in series. Adding a resistor in PARALLEL always LOWERS the equivalent — even a large resistor opens one more lane for current, so R_eq falls below the smallest branch. A 1 Ω and a 1000 Ω in parallel make about 0.999 Ω, less than either.
- *Misconception:* Current takes the path of least resistance — the smaller resistor gets all of it.
  *Correction:* Current takes ALL available paths, split in inverse proportion to resistance. A 1 Ω branch beside a 2 Ω branch carries two-thirds of the current, not all of it. Only a literal short circuit (R ≈ 0) monopolizes the flow.
- *Misconception:* The bulb nearest the battery is brightest, because it gets the current first.
  *Correction:* In a series loop the SAME current flows through every element simultaneously — there is no 'first'. Identical series bulbs are equally bright regardless of position; the whole loop's current is set at once by the total resistance.
- *Misconception:* Voltage is the same everywhere in a circuit, like current in a series loop.
  *Correction:* Voltage DIVIDES across series elements (in proportion to their resistance) and is shared only across PARALLEL branches. Confusing the two sharing rules — same-I for series, same-V for parallel — is the single most damaging swap in circuit reasoning.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Adding parallel resistances directly (or forgetting to invert the reciprocal sum at the end).
- Applying the same-current rule to parallel branches or same-voltage to series chains.
- Claiming all current takes the least-resistance path.
- Assigning the battery voltage to a single series element instead of dividing it.
- Skipping the junction check, leaving inconsistent currents undetected.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- A side-by-side sharing diagram: series chain with one current arrow threading all elements and voltage labels summing; parallel ladder with one voltage label spanning and current arrows splitting at the junction.
- An animated reduce-then-expand solver: a mixed network collapsing stage by stage to one resistor, then re-expanding with I and V assigned at each step.
- The roads analogy rendered: traffic on one road with tollbooths in a row (series) versus tollbooths on parallel roads — throughput visibly rising as roads open.
- A brightness lab storyboard: two bulbs series versus parallel on the same battery — dim pair versus full-brightness pair, then one bulb removed in each case (all dark versus unaffected).
- A meter-placement diagram: ammeter inline (series, low R) and voltmeter bridging (parallel, high R), with the wrong placements shown and their failure modes annotated.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A 12 V battery drives the network: R₁ = 4.0 Ω in series with a parallel pair R₂ = 6.0 Ω and R₃ = 3.0 Ω. Find (a) the equivalent resistance, (b) the battery current, (c) the voltage across the parallel pair, and (d) the current through each of R₂ and R₃.

1. (a) Reduce the parallel pair first: R₂₃ = R₂R₃/(R₂ + R₃) = (6.0 × 3.0)/(6.0 + 3.0) = 18/9 = 2.0 Ω — note it is smaller than either branch, as parallel must be.
2. Series total: R_eq = R₁ + R₂₃ = 4.0 + 2.0 = 6.0 Ω.
3. (b) Battery current: I = V/R_eq = 12/6.0 = 2.0 A — this full current threads R₁ (series inherits current).
4. (c) Expand back: V across R₁ is IR₁ = 2.0 × 4.0 = 8.0 V, leaving V₂₃ = 12 − 8.0 = 4.0 V across the parallel pair (divider check: 12 × 2.0/6.0 = 4.0 V ✓).
5. (d) Parallel inherits voltage: I₂ = V₂₃/R₂ = 4.0/6.0 ≈ 0.67 A; I₃ = 4.0/3.0 ≈ 1.33 A — the smaller resistor takes the larger share, in exact inverse proportion (1:2 for 6:3).
6. Junction audit: I₂ + I₃ = 0.67 + 1.33 = 2.0 A = I ✓ — the currents recombine to the battery current, charge conserved.

*Outcome:* The student reduces the network in the correct order (parallel first, then series), computes R_eq = 6.0 Ω and I = 2.0 A, expands back to find 4.0 V across the pair, splits it into 0.67 A and 1.33 A by the same-voltage rule, and verifies the junction sum.

**Real-world intuition**

- House wiring is one grand parallel ladder: every socket gets the full mains voltage, and each appliance switches independently — series wiring would dim everything and die at one failure.
- Old-style fairy lights strung in series taught a generation the failure mode: one blown filament opens the only path and darkens the lot.
- Voltage dividers set reference and sensor voltages in nearly every electronic device — two series resistors splitting a rail in a designed ratio.
- Car circuits run dozens of parallel branches off one 12 V battery, each fused: the parallel grammar isolates faults branch by branch.
- Battery packs use both grammars deliberately: cells in series to add voltage, strings in parallel to add capacity and share current.

**Practice progression**

Item types: equivalent-resistance computations (pure series, pure parallel, mixed two-stage networks), reduce-then-expand full solutions (all currents and voltages), brightness and failure-mode predictions (series versus parallel bulbs, one element removed), meter-placement and divider-design items. Suggested item count: 12.

Begin with pure series and pure parallel equivalents, add the two-resistor shortcut and equal-N cases, then mixed networks solved reduce-then-expand, ending with prediction items (brightness, failure, meter placement) and simple divider design.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, circuit-prediction problems, network-reduction tasks. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must reduce mixed networks and apply the sharing rules to assign every current and voltage, not merely recall the two formulas.

Mastery signal: The student computes equivalents in the right order, assigns I and V through a two-stage network with a junction audit that closes, and predicts brightness/failure outcomes from the sharing rules, at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Teach the SHARING rules before the formulas — 'series shares current, parallel shares voltage' answers more exam questions than the reciprocal formula does. The parallel-lowers-resistance result feels wrong to students until the roads picture lands; spend the time there, and follow with the 1 Ω ∥ 1000 Ω shock. Real bulbs are the best laboratory: series dimming, parallel independence, and the one-bulb-removed contrast are unforgettable in a way simulations aren't. Insist on the reduce-then-expand discipline with a junction audit at the end — students who check ΣI at a node catch their own errors. Flag the meter-placement logic (ammeter series/low-R, voltmeter parallel/high-R) as the sharing rules applied to instruments themselves.

**Student notes**

Two grammars, two sharing rules: series = same CURRENT (voltages add, resistances add); parallel = same VOLTAGE (currents add, reciprocals add). Parallel equivalents are always SMALLER than the smallest branch — memorize product-over-sum for two, R/N for N equal. Solve mixed networks in passes: reduce to one resistor for the battery current, then expand back assigning I and V by the sharing rules, and CHECK that branch currents sum at junctions. Current splits inversely with resistance but never abandons a branch. Brightness follows power at the element, not distance from the battery.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.ohms-law
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): phys.em.emf, phys.em.kirchhoffs-laws
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: The two sharing rules are Ohm's law (phys.em.ohms-law) deployed at scale, and they harden into Kirchhoff's laws (phys.em.kirchhoffs-laws) — the junction audit IS KCL, the divider IS KVL — for networks too tangled to reduce. Series/parallel capacitors (phys.em.capacitance) obey the mirror-image rules (swap the formulas), a comparison worth making explicitly. Power distribution (phys.em.electrical-power) and EMF with internal resistance (phys.em.emf) complete the practical battery-circuit picture.

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.ohms-law

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one mixed-network reduction with full expansion and junction audit, one brightness/failure prediction pair (series versus parallel), one divider computation. Monthly, restate the two sharing rules from memory before touching any formula — they are the grammar every circuit question is written in.

---

### Kirchhoff's Current and Voltage Laws

*Concept ID: `phys.em.kirchhoffs-laws` · Difficulty: proficient · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.85 · Estimated study time: 5h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to state Kirchhoff's current law (junction rule) and voltage law (loop rule), identify them as charge and energy conservation respectively, set up signed equations for multi-loop circuits with consistent conventions, and solve two-loop networks for unknown currents.

Kirchhoff's current law states charge conservation at junctions; Kirchhoff's voltage law states energy conservation around loops.

When a circuit stops being reducible — multiple batteries, bridges, loops sharing branches — series/parallel grammar runs out, and Kirchhoff's two laws take over as the universal method. The CURRENT LAW (KCL, junction rule): the sum of currents entering any junction equals the sum leaving — nothing but charge conservation in flow form; charge cannot pile up at a node or vanish from it. The VOLTAGE LAW (KVL, loop rule): the algebraic sum of potential changes around any closed loop is zero — energy conservation per coulomb; a charge returning to its starting point must return to its starting potential, having gained in sources exactly what it dropped in resistors. The physics is old; the craft is SIGNS, and a fixed drill tames it: (1) assign a labelled current with a guessed direction to each branch (wrong guesses are harmless — the answer simply comes out negative, meaning the true flow is opposite); (2) write KCL at junctions (n − 1 independent equations for n junctions); (3) walk each chosen loop in a fixed direction writing potential changes — through a resistor WITH the assumed current is a drop (−IR), against it a rise (+IR); through a battery from − to + is a rise (+EMF), from + to − a drop (−EMF), regardless of current direction; (4) solve the linear system, taking as many independent loops as the circuit has meshes. The two laws are not new physics grafted onto circuits — they are the same conservation principles that ran mechanics, wearing circuit clothes — and every series/parallel result already proven is a special case (same-current is KCL along a chain; the voltage divider is KVL around one loop). Their power is generality: any planar network of batteries and resistors, however tangled, yields to the same four-step drill, which is why circuit-simulation software is, at its core, Kirchhoff solved at scale.

**Key ideas**

- KCL (junction rule): ΣI_in = ΣI_out at every node — charge conservation; no accumulation at a junction.
- KVL (loop rule): Σ(potential changes) = 0 around any closed loop — energy conservation per coulomb; back to the start means back to the same potential.
- Sign drill: resistor traversed with assumed current → −IR, against → +IR; battery − to + → +EMF, + to − → −EMF (battery signs don't care about current direction).
- Wrong direction guesses are self-correcting: a negative answer means the real current runs opposite to the arrow — never restart, just read the sign.
- Equation budget: n junctions give n − 1 independent KCL equations; independent loops (one per mesh) supply the rest — match unknowns before solving.
- Series/parallel rules are Kirchhoff special cases; the laws are the general method when reduction fails (multiple EMFs, bridges, shared branches).

**Vocabulary**

- **Kirchhoff's current law (KCL)** — ΣI_in = ΣI_out at any junction — charge conservation at a node; also the junction rule.
- **Kirchhoff's voltage law (KVL)** — The algebraic sum of potential changes around any closed loop is zero — energy conservation per coulomb; also the loop rule.
- **branch current** — The labelled current assigned to one branch with a guessed direction; a negative solution means the guess was backward.
- **independent equations** — n − 1 junction equations for n nodes plus one loop equation per mesh — exactly enough to solve, no more.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* If I guess a current direction wrong, the whole solution is invalid and must be redone.
  *Correction:* The algebra forgives you: a wrongly guessed direction simply yields a NEGATIVE value, announcing that the true current runs opposite to your arrow. Keep the sign convention consistent, solve once, and read negatives as reversed flow — never restart.
- *Misconception:* The sign of a battery's EMF in the loop equation depends on which way the current flows through it.
  *Correction:* Battery signs follow the TERMINALS, not the current: traversing − to + is a rise (+EMF), + to − a drop (−EMF), even if the current is being forced backward through it (as when charging). Only RESISTOR signs consult the assumed current direction.
- *Misconception:* Current is used up around the loop, so KVL should track diminishing current.
  *Correction:* KVL tracks POTENTIAL (energy per charge), not current. In any single branch the current is the same at both ends — what changes around the loop is potential: up through sources, down through resistors, summing to zero on return. Charge conservation (KCL) and energy conservation (KVL) are separate ledgers.
- *Misconception:* You can write a KCL equation for every junction and a KVL equation for every conceivable loop, and more equations mean a safer solution.
  *Correction:* Only n − 1 junction equations are independent (the last repeats information), and only one loop per mesh adds anything new — extra loops are linear combinations of ones you have. Count unknowns, then take exactly that many independent equations; redundancy invites transcription errors, not safety.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Flipping a battery's sign because of the current direction (terminals decide, not current).
- Inconsistent traversal direction mid-loop, mixing rise and drop conventions.
- Restarting the whole problem upon a negative current instead of reading it as reversed.
- Writing redundant equations (all n junctions, or dependent loops) and losing track.
- Dropping the shared-branch term (I₃ = I₁ + I₂ substitution errors in two-loop systems).

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- A junction close-up animation: current arrows in and out with a running ΣI_in = ΣI_out tally — then a deliberately broken version showing charge 'piling up' to make conservation visceral.
- A loop-walk animation: a marker charge carried around a two-battery loop, its potential plotted as a skyline (up at sources, down at resistors) landing back at zero.
- The sign-drill card rendered as a diagram: the four traversal cases (resistor with/against current, battery −→+/+→−) with their signs, to be internalized before any algebra.
- A worked two-loop circuit with all three branch currents labelled, equations written beside the diagram line by line, and the negative-answer branch highlighted with its arrow flipped in a corrected overlay.
- A 'grammar runs out' gallery: circuits reducible by series/parallel versus the bridge and two-EMF networks that force Kirchhoff — the method's jurisdiction mapped.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* Two batteries drive a two-loop circuit: EMF₁ = 12 V with R₁ = 2.0 Ω in the left branch, EMF₂ = 6.0 V with R₂ = 1.0 Ω in the right branch, and a shared middle branch containing R₃ = 3.0 Ω connecting the tops. Both battery negatives join at the bottom node. Find the current in each branch. (Assume I₁ up through EMF₁, I₂ up through EMF₂, I₃ down through R₃; junction at the top: I₁ + I₂ = I₃.)

1. KCL at the top node: I₁ + I₂ = I₃ — one junction equation (the bottom node repeats it).
2. KVL, left loop (EMF₁ → R₁ → R₃, walked with I₁ then down with I₃): +12 − 2.0·I₁ − 3.0·I₃ = 0.
3. KVL, right loop (EMF₂ → R₂ → R₃): +6.0 − 1.0·I₂ − 3.0·I₃ = 0.
4. Substitute I₃ = I₁ + I₂: left → 12 = 2I₁ + 3I₁ + 3I₂ = 5I₁ + 3I₂; right → 6 = I₂ + 3I₁ + 3I₂ = 3I₁ + 4I₂.
5. Solve: from the pair (5I₁ + 3I₂ = 12; 3I₁ + 4I₂ = 6) — multiply first by 4, second by 3: 20I₁ + 12I₂ = 48; 9I₁ + 12I₂ = 18; subtract: 11I₁ = 30 → I₁ = 30/11 ≈ 2.73 A; then I₂ = (6 − 3×30/11)/4 = (66/11 − 90/11)/4 = (−24/11)/4 = −6/11 ≈ −0.55 A; I₃ = I₁ + I₂ = 24/11 ≈ 2.18 A.
6. Read the signs: I₂ is NEGATIVE — the 6 V battery is actually being driven BACKWARD (charged) by the stronger 12 V source; the true current in that branch runs opposite the guess at 0.55 A. Energy audit: the loop sums recompute to zero with these values (left: 12 − 2(2.73) − 3(2.18) = 12 − 5.45 − 6.55 ≈ 0 ✓).

*Outcome:* The student sets up one junction and two loop equations with consistent signs, solves the linear system to I₁ ≈ 2.73 A, I₂ ≈ −0.55 A, I₃ ≈ 2.18 A, interprets the negative current as a reversed (charging) branch rather than an error, and verifies a loop sum.

**Real-world intuition**

- Circuit-simulation software (SPICE and its descendants) is Kirchhoff industrialized — every chip design is verified by solving KCL/KVL over millions of nodes.
- Power-grid load-flow analysis balances generation and consumption across the network with Kirchhoff's laws at continental scale.
- Automotive electrics — two sources (battery and alternator) sharing loads — are textbook two-EMF Kirchhoff networks, including the reversed-current charging branch.
- Fault diagnosis in electronics starts with KVL: measured voltage drops around a loop must sum to the supply, and the discrepancy localizes the fault.
- Battery management systems in packs of parallel strings enforce KCL: cell-string currents are monitored to sum correctly, imbalance flagging degradation.

**Practice progression**

Item types: junction-equation setups (find the missing current at a node), single-loop KVL walks with mixed sources and resistors (sign drill), full two-loop solutions with negative-current interpretation, equation-independence items (which loops/junctions add information). Suggested item count: 10.

Begin with standalone KCL node problems and single-loop sign walks, add loops containing two EMFs (including opposed ones), then full two-loop systems, ending with problems whose guessed directions come out negative and must be interpreted.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, multi-loop network problems, sign-convention drills. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must set up and solve signed loop and junction equations for non-reducible networks, not merely state the two conservation laws.

Mastery signal: The student writes independent KCL/KVL equations with a consistent sign convention, solves two-loop systems correctly, interprets negative currents as reversed flow, and closes a verification loop sum, at 85% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Sell the laws as old friends: KCL is charge conservation, KVL is energy conservation — students who see them as conservation principles set up signs correctly far more often than those who see arbitrary rules. Then drill the sign card until mechanical: resistors consult the current arrow, batteries consult their terminals — that asymmetry is where most errors breed. Celebrate the first negative current publicly: it is the method WORKING (a reversed guess self-corrected), and the two-battery example where the weaker battery charges is the perfect stage for it. Require a verification loop-sum at the end of every solution; it converts silent algebra slips into caught ones. Restrain equation enthusiasm — teach counting (unknowns = independent equations) explicitly.

**Student notes**

Two laws, two ledgers: KCL balances CHARGE at nodes (in = out), KVL balances ENERGY around loops (sum of potential changes = 0). Follow the drill every time: label branch currents with guessed arrows → junction equations (n − 1 of them) → loop walks with the sign card (−IR with the arrow, +IR against; +EMF from − to +, −EMF from + to −) → solve. A negative answer is information, not failure: the current runs opposite your arrow — flip it mentally and move on. Batteries take their sign from TERMINALS, never from current direction. Finish by re-walking one loop with your answers: it must sum to zero.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.dc-circuits
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): phys.em.potentiometer, phys.em.rc-circuits, phys.em.wheatstone-bridge
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
