- Narrative: Kirchhoff's laws generalize the series/parallel sharing rules (phys.em.dc-circuits) into a method with no jurisdiction limits — and they ARE the conservation laws of mechanics (phys.mech.conservation-of-energy) in circuit dress, with KCL echoing current continuity from phys.em.electric-current. The Wheatstone bridge (phys.em.wheatstone-bridge) and potentiometer (phys.em.potentiometer) are Kirchhoff applied to precision measurement; RC circuits (phys.em.rc-circuits) extend the loop rule to time-varying charge.

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.dc-circuits

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one sign-card recitation with a single-loop walk, one full two-loop solve with verification sum, one negative-current interpretation. Monthly, restate both laws as conservation principles in one sentence each — that framing, not the formulas, is what transfers to bridges, potentiometers, and every network after.

---

### Wheatstone Bridge

*Concept ID: `phys.em.wheatstone-bridge` · Difficulty: proficient · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 4h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to draw and label the Wheatstone bridge, derive the balance condition P/Q = R/S from Kirchhoff's laws, use a balanced bridge to compute an unknown resistance, explain why balance makes the galvanometer current zero, and describe the metre bridge as its practical form.

The Wheatstone bridge is a circuit for precise measurement of an unknown resistance by balancing four resistances.

The Wheatstone bridge turns resistance measurement from arithmetic into a NULL experiment — the most precise kind physics owns. Four resistances form a diamond: P and Q up one side (the ratio arms), R (known, adjustable) and S (unknown) up the other, a battery across one diagonal driving the whole, and a sensitive galvanometer across the other diagonal listening between the two midpoints. Each side is a voltage divider: the P–Q chain fixes its midpoint at a potential set by the ratio P/Q; the R–S chain fixes its midpoint by R/S. If the ratios differ, the midpoints sit at different potentials and current trickles through the galvanometer; adjust R until the needle reads exactly ZERO, and the midpoints are at the SAME potential — the bridge is balanced, and the balance condition is the ratio equality P/Q = R/S, giving the unknown as S = R·Q/P. The null principle is the genius: at balance you measure NOTHING except the absence of current, so the galvanometer needs no calibration (only sensitivity), the battery voltage cancels out of the condition entirely (a sagging battery shifts both dividers identically), and the precision is limited only by how sharply zero can be judged and how well the known resistors are made. Kirchhoff underwrites it all — the balance condition drops out of two loop equations with I_g = 0 — and the bridge is thus Kirchhoff's laws forged into an instrument. The metre bridge is the schoolroom incarnation: P and Q become the two segments of a uniform 1 m resistance wire (resistance ∝ length, so P/Q = l₁/l₂), a sliding jockey hunts the null point, and S = R·l₂/l₁ follows from a ruler reading. Sensitivity is best when all four arms are comparable in size; a wildly unbalanced bridge barely nudges the needle near balance and the null blurs.

**Key ideas**

- Four arms in a diamond (P, Q ratio arms; R known; S unknown), battery across one diagonal, galvanometer across the other — two voltage dividers compared midpoint to midpoint.
- Balance = null: adjust R until the galvanometer reads zero; then both midpoints are at equal potential and P/Q = R/S, so S = R·Q/P.
- The null principle buys precision: no galvanometer calibration needed, and the battery's EMF cancels from the condition — supply drift cannot corrupt the measurement.
- Kirchhoff derivation: set I_g = 0 in the two loop equations; balance is their consistency condition — the bridge is KCL/KVL built into hardware.
- Metre bridge: P and Q replaced by segments of uniform wire (R ∝ length), null found with a jockey, S = R·l₂/l₁ from a length ratio — resistance measured with a ruler.
- Sensitivity is greatest with comparable arms; extreme ratios flatten the galvanometer response near balance and blur the null.

**Vocabulary**

- **Wheatstone bridge** — Four resistances in a diamond with battery and galvanometer across the diagonals; balanced when P/Q = R/S and the galvanometer reads zero.
- **balance (null) condition** — Equal midpoint potentials → zero galvanometer current → P/Q = R/S; the unknown follows as S = R·Q/P.
- **null method** — Measuring by adjusting to ZERO response — immune to supply drift and meter calibration; precision limited by standards and null sharpness.
- **metre bridge** — A Wheatstone bridge whose ratio arms are segments of a uniform 1 m wire: S = R·l₂/l₁ from the null-point lengths.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* At balance, no current flows anywhere in the bridge.
  *Correction:* Only the GALVANOMETER branch carries zero current. Both divider chains conduct steadily at balance — current flows through P–Q and through R–S; what vanishes is the flow BETWEEN the midpoints, because they sit at equal potential.
- *Misconception:* The battery voltage must be known accurately for the bridge to work.
  *Correction:* The balance condition P/Q = R/S contains no EMF at all — the battery drives both dividers identically, and its value cancels. That independence is the null method's whole point: a drifting supply shifts both midpoints together and leaves the balance untouched.
- *Misconception:* A more sensitive galvanometer gives a more accurate value of the unknown resistance.
  *Correction:* Sensitivity sharpens WHERE zero is, but accuracy of S = R·Q/P is set by the QUALITY of the known resistors (and the wire's uniformity in a metre bridge). A razor-sharp null through miscalibrated ratio arms yields a precisely wrong answer — null-finding and standards are separate error budgets.
- *Misconception:* Balance means the two midpoints are connected or at zero potential.
  *Correction:* The midpoints are at EQUAL potential — not zero, and not joined. Each sits partway down its divider (at the same fraction of the battery voltage when balanced); the galvanometer between equal potentials simply has no potential difference to drive current through it.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Claiming zero current everywhere at balance (only the galvanometer branch is zero).
- Inverting the ratio (S = R·P/Q) or mismatching lengths to gaps in the metre bridge.
- Thinking battery voltage or galvanometer calibration enters the result.
- Forgetting l₂ = 100 − l₁ and treating the null-point reading as both lengths.
- Assuming a sharper galvanometer fixes errors that live in the standards or the wire.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- The diamond diagram built in stages: first two independent voltage dividers side by side with midpoint potentials labelled, then the galvanometer bridged between them — imbalance current appearing, then vanishing at balance.
- A live-balance animation: R adjusted with a slider, the galvanometer needle swinging and settling to zero exactly as P/Q = R/S, the ratio equality displayed.
- A potential 'skyline' plot along both divider chains: two descending staircases from + to −, midpoint heights compared — equal heights = balance.
- The metre-bridge apparatus rendered: uniform wire, jockey, standard resistor, unknown — with the length ratio l₁/l₂ annotated as the live P/Q.
- A sensitivity heat map: needle deflection near balance for comparable arms versus a 1000:1 bridge — the blurred null made visible.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* In a metre-bridge experiment, an unknown resistance S is placed in the right gap and a standard R = 6.0 Ω in the left gap. The null point is found at l₁ = 40.0 cm from the left end. (a) Find S. (b) The experimenter then interchanges R and S; where should the new null point appear, and why is performing this swap good practice? (c) If the bridge wire were slightly thicker near one end, how would the result be affected?

1. (a) The wire segments are the ratio arms: P ∝ l₁ = 40.0 cm, Q ∝ l₂ = 100 − 40.0 = 60.0 cm. Balance: R/S = l₁/l₂ (left-gap resistance over right-gap resistance equals left length over right length).
2. So S = R·l₂/l₁ = 6.0 × 60.0/40.0 = 9.0 Ω.
3. (b) Swapped, the balance demands S/R = l₁'/l₂': l₁'/l₂' = 9.0/6.0 = 3/2, and l₁' + l₂' = 100 cm → l₁' = 60.0 cm. The null point mirrors from 40 to 60 cm.
4. The swap is error hygiene: averaging the two determinations cancels end-resistance and any systematic offset at the connection points — a null method further hardened by symmetry.
5. (c) A thicker (lower resistance-per-length) region breaks the R ∝ length assumption: the length ratio no longer equals the resistance ratio, biasing S wherever the null lands in or beyond the flaw. Wire uniformity is the metre bridge's standards budget — the galvanometer's sharpness cannot rescue a non-uniform wire.
6. Audit: at balance no galvanometer calibration entered, no battery value entered — only R (standard) and a length ratio: the null method's fingerprint.

*Outcome:* The student computes S = 9.0 Ω from the length ratio, predicts the mirrored 60 cm null after the swap and explains its error-cancelling purpose, and identifies wire uniformity (not galvanometer quality or battery stability) as the residual accuracy limit.

**Real-world intuition**

- Strain-gauge instrumentation runs on Wheatstone bridges: a gauge in one arm converts micro-strains to microvolt imbalances — aircraft wings, bridges, and load cells are all read this way.
- Electronic weighing scales are bridge circuits at heart: load cells with four strain gauges form a full bridge whose imbalance is the weight.
- Resistance thermometers (RTDs) sit in bridge arms so that tiny ρ(T) changes become measurable off-balance voltages.
- Gas sensors and hot-wire anemometers use self-heated bridge arms: what the flow or gas changes is a resistance, and the bridge reports it.
- Precision resistance metrology still uses bridge comparison against standards — the null principle remains the low-noise route to part-per-million resistance measurement.

**Practice progression**

Item types: balance-condition computations (find the unknown from three knowns or from lengths), null-point prediction after component swaps or series additions to one arm, error-analysis items (what does and does not affect a null measurement), Kirchhoff-derivation walkthroughs (recover P/Q = R/S from loop equations with I_g = 0). Suggested item count: 10.

Begin with direct S = R·Q/P and metre-bridge length computations, add swap and modification predictions, then error-budget reasoning (battery drift, galvanometer sensitivity, wire uniformity), ending with the Kirchhoff derivation reproduced.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, apparatus-reasoning problems, derivation task. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must use the balance condition in both diamond and metre-bridge forms and apply null-method reasoning to error analysis, beyond stating P/Q = R/S.

Mastery signal: The student computes unknowns from balance data, predicts null-point shifts under modifications, correctly separates what the null method cancels (EMF, galvanometer calibration) from what it cannot (standards, wire uniformity), at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Build the bridge conceptually as TWO VOLTAGE DIVIDERS COMPARED — students who see the midpoint-potential race understand balance; those shown a memorized diamond do not. Derive P/Q = R/S once from Kirchhoff with I_g = 0 on the board, then let the metre bridge make it tactile: hunting the null with a jockey is satisfying lab work and teaches sensitivity (watch the needle barely move near an unbalanced bridge's 'null'). Emphasize the null method as a PHILOSOPHY — what cancels (battery, galvanometer calibration) versus what doesn't (standards, wire uniformity) — because that error-budget thinking transfers to the potentiometer and all precision measurement. The interchange trick (average the two null points' determinations) is examinable and good science.

**Student notes**

Picture two voltage dividers racing to set their midpoints: the galvanometer between them reads their disagreement, and balance means the ratios agree — P/Q = R/S, so S = R·Q/P. At balance, current still flows in both chains; only the galvanometer branch is dead. The battery's value never enters, and the galvanometer needs no calibration — that's why null methods win at precision. Metre bridge: ratios become lengths, S = R·l₂/l₁; swap the gaps and re-average to cancel end errors. Sensitivity is best with comparable arms. Know the Kirchhoff derivation (set I_g = 0) — it's the standard proof question.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.kirchhoffs-laws
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): none yet mapped
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: The bridge is Kirchhoff's laws (phys.em.kirchhoffs-laws) as an instrument, and each side is a divider straight from dc-circuits (phys.em.dc-circuits). Its unknown-resistance mission leans on resistivity's R ∝ L (phys.em.resistivity) in metre-bridge form, and its null philosophy is shared by the potentiometer (phys.em.potentiometer) — the two precision instruments of DC measurement, both measuring by absence. Strain-gauge bridges connect forward to elasticity (phys.mech.stress-strain).

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.kirchhoffs-laws

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one S = R·Q/P computation (diamond and metre-bridge forms), one Kirchhoff derivation of the balance condition, one error-budget question (what cancels, what doesn't). Monthly, restate the null principle in one sentence — measuring by zero — and name the two things it cannot cancel; that pair of ideas returns with the potentiometer.

---

### Potentiometer

*Concept ID: `phys.em.potentiometer` · Difficulty: proficient · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 4h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to explain the potentiometer's principle (potential drop proportional to length along a uniform current-carrying wire), find balance points where the galvanometer reads null, compare two EMFs via E₁/E₂ = l₁/l₂, measure a cell's internal resistance, and articulate why the null condition makes the potentiometer an ideal (zero-draw) voltmeter.

A potentiometer measures EMF and internal resistance by comparing potential differences along a uniform resistance wire.

The potentiometer measures EMF the only honest way — by drawing NO current from the cell being measured. Its engine is a long uniform resistance wire carrying a steady current I from a driver battery: with resistance per unit length constant, the potential drop along the wire is strictly proportional to length, V(l) = (I·ρ_w)·l = k·l, where k is the potential gradient (volts per metre). The wire becomes a potential RULER: sliding a contact (jockey) along it taps off any voltage from zero to the full drop, smoothly and linearly. To measure an unknown EMF, connect the test cell between the wire's starting end and a galvanometer on the jockey, with like terminals together (both positives at the same end): where the wire's tapped potential exactly equals the cell's EMF, the cell can drive no current through the galvanometer — the NULL point. At balance, E = k·l_balance, and crucially the cell delivers zero current, so there is no internal-resistance drop inside it — the potentiometer reads the true open-circuit EMF, which no ordinary voltmeter can do (a voltmeter draws some current, so it reads the terminal voltage E − Ir, always a shade low). Two classic deployments follow. COMPARISON of EMFs: balance cell 1 at l₁, cell 2 at l₂ on the same wire and current — the gradient cancels, E₁/E₂ = l₁/l₂, a ratio measured with a ruler. INTERNAL RESISTANCE: balance the cell open (length l₁ ∝ E), then close a known R across it and rebalance (length l₂ ∝ terminal voltage V = ER/(R+r)); the lengths give r = R(l₁ − l₂)/l₂. Sensitivity is bought with wire: a longer wire (or smaller driver current) lowers k, stretching each millivolt over more millimetres. The standard checks are part of the craft: the driver EMF must exceed the unknown (else no balance exists anywhere on the wire), polarity must oppose, and a null that drifts signals an unsteady driver current.

**Key ideas**

- Principle: uniform wire + steady current → potential drop ∝ length, V = k·l with k the potential gradient — the wire is a linear potential ruler.
- Null balance: where tapped potential equals the cell's EMF, the galvanometer current is zero and E = k·l_balance — measurement by absence of current.
- Zero draw is the superpower: at null the cell supplies no current, so no Ir drop occurs inside it — the potentiometer reads TRUE EMF; any real voltmeter reads only E − Ir.
- EMF comparison: same wire, same current → E₁/E₂ = l₁/l₂ — the gradient (and driver battery value) cancels from the ratio.
- Internal resistance: balance open (l₁) and loaded with known R (l₂): r = R(l₁ − l₂)/l₂ — two lengths and one standard resistor.
- Craft rules: driver EMF must exceed the unknown, polarities opposed, gradient lowered (longer wire/smaller current) for sensitivity; a drifting null betrays an unsteady driver.

**Vocabulary**

- **potentiometer** — A uniform current-carrying wire used as a linear potential ruler; unknown EMFs are balanced against the tapped drop at a null point.
- **potential gradient k** — Potential drop per unit length of the wire, k = V_wire/L (V/m); the ruler's scale — smaller k, finer measurement.
- **balance (null) point** — The jockey position where the tapped potential equals the test cell's EMF and the galvanometer current is zero: E = k·l.
- **internal-resistance protocol** — Balance open (l₁ ∝ E) and with known load R (l₂ ∝ V): r = R(l₁ − l₂)/l₂.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* A potentiometer is just a fancy voltmeter — a good digital voltmeter does the same job.
  *Correction:* Every voltmeter draws SOME current, so it reads the terminal voltage E − Ir, not the EMF. The potentiometer at null draws exactly ZERO from the test cell — no current, no internal drop, true EMF. For precision cell work the difference is the whole measurement.
- *Misconception:* At the balance point, no current flows in the potentiometer wire.
  *Correction:* The driver current flows through the wire CONTINUOUSLY — it is what creates the potential gradient. What vanishes at balance is only the current through the galvanometer/test-cell branch. Kill the wire current and the instrument is dead: no gradient, no ruler.
- *Misconception:* If no balance point can be found along the whole wire, the apparatus is broken.
  *Correction:* Usually it's a design condition violated: the driver's full wire drop must EXCEED the unknown EMF (else the ruler is too short), and the cells' polarities must oppose through the galvanometer. Check driver EMF > unknown EMF and terminal orientation before suspecting hardware.
- *Misconception:* A longer balance length means the cell has a bigger internal resistance.
  *Correction:* Balance length measures EMF (or terminal voltage), not resistance directly. Internal resistance is extracted from the CHANGE between open and loaded balance lengths — r = R(l₁ − l₂)/l₂. A big EMF with tiny r gives a long l₁ that barely shrinks; the shrinkage, not the length, carries r.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Saying all current stops at balance (the driver current must keep flowing).
- Confusing the loaded balance (terminal voltage) with EMF, or swapping l₁ and l₂ in the r formula.
- Forgetting the driver-EMF-must-exceed-unknown condition when no null is found.
- Treating balance length as proportional to internal resistance rather than to EMF/terminal voltage.
- Ignoring gradient changes: comparing lengths taken at different driver currents.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- The potential-ruler rendering: the wire drawn as a ramp of potential from full drop to zero, the jockey sliding to tap any level, V = k·l as a live readout.
- A null-hunt animation: galvanometer deflecting one way early on the wire, the other way late, zero crossing at balance — with the test cell's current visibly dying at null.
- A side-by-side EMF measurement: voltmeter across a cell (current flowing, reading E − Ir, slightly low) versus potentiometer at null (zero draw, true E) — the difference annotated.
- The internal-resistance experiment storyboard: balance open at l₁, switch in the known R, rebalance at shorter l₂, the formula r = R(l₁ − l₂)/l₂ assembled from the two frames.
- A sensitivity comparison: the same 10 mV difference spread over a short wire (needle barely moves between marks) versus a long wire at low gradient — millivolts per millimetre made visible.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A potentiometer wire 10.0 m long carries a steady driver current giving a total drop of 5.0 V across the wire. A test cell balances at 6.00 m from the start. (a) Find the potential gradient and the cell's EMF. (b) With a 10.0 Ω resistor connected across the test cell, the balance point moves to 5.00 m. Find the cell's internal resistance. (c) Explain why a voltmeter across the same cell would have read less than the answer to (a).

1. (a) Potential gradient: k = 5.0 V/10.0 m = 0.50 V/m. EMF at null: E = k·l₁ = 0.50 × 6.00 = 3.0 V.
2. (b) Loaded, the cell drives current through R, and the wire now balances its TERMINAL voltage: V = k·l₂ = 0.50 × 5.00 = 2.5 V.
3. Terminal-voltage relation: V = ER/(R + r) → 2.5 = 3.0 × 10.0/(10.0 + r).
4. Solve: 2.5(10.0 + r) = 30.0 → 25.0 + 2.5r = 30.0 → r = 5.0/2.5 = 2.0 Ω. (Formula check: r = R(l₁ − l₂)/l₂ = 10.0 × (6.00 − 5.00)/5.00 = 2.0 Ω ✓ — the length form and the circuit form agree.)
5. (c) A voltmeter must draw current I_v to operate, so the cell's terminals show E − I_v·r < E: with r = 2.0 Ω even a 1 mA draw costs 2 mV, and cheap meters draw far more. The potentiometer at null draws nothing — the internal 2.0 Ω is invisible because no current crosses it.
6. Audit: at balance in (a) the galvanometer read zero while the DRIVER current still flowed in the wire — the two-current distinction that defines the instrument.

*Outcome:* The student computes k = 0.50 V/m and E = 3.0 V, extracts r = 2.0 Ω by both the circuit equation and the length-ratio formula, and explains the voltmeter's systematic under-reading versus the potentiometer's zero-draw fidelity.

**Real-world intuition**

- Standard-cell calibration chains historically rested on potentiometers: laboratory voltage standards were compared by null methods long before digital meters existed.
- High-impedance measurement in electrochemistry (pH electrodes, reference cells) still demands near-zero draw — modern electrometers are the potentiometer's zero-current principle in solid state.
- Thermocouple thermometry uses potentiometric measurement: microvolt EMFs from junctions read without loading errors.
- The volume knob's ancestor: the word 'potentiometer' survives in electronics for the three-terminal divider — audio faders are the tapped-wire principle in a carbon track.
- Battery quality control measures true open-circuit EMF and internal resistance — the open/loaded two-balance protocol is essentially the industrial test, automated.

**Practice progression**

Item types: gradient and EMF computations (E = k·l with unit care), EMF-comparison ratios (E₁/E₂ = l₁/l₂, including re-balancing after driver changes), internal-resistance extractions from open/loaded balance pairs, design and troubleshooting items (no balance found, drifting null, sensitivity improvement). Suggested item count: 10.

Begin with k and E = k·l plug-ins, add two-cell comparisons, then the internal-resistance protocol with both solution routes, ending with troubleshooting scenarios (driver too weak, reversed polarity, unsteady current) and sensitivity design.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, experiment-protocol problems, explanation prompts. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must run the potentiometer's measurement protocols (EMF, comparison, internal resistance) and apply the zero-draw principle to explain its superiority, beyond reciting V = k·l.

Mastery signal: The student computes gradients, EMFs, and internal resistances from balance data by both routes, diagnoses no-balance and drift conditions, and articulates why null measurement reads true EMF, at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Anchor everything to the zero-draw argument — it is the answer to 'why not just use a voltmeter?' and the concept's examinable soul. Keep the two currents rigorously distinct (driver current: always flowing, creates the ruler; test-branch current: zero at null) — the misconception that balance kills all current is near-universal until confronted. Run the internal-resistance experiment live if possible; watching l shrink when R is connected makes E versus terminal voltage concrete. Drill the two craft checks (driver EMF must exceed unknown; polarities opposed) as troubleshooting instinct. Connect to the Wheatstone bridge explicitly: both are null instruments, but the bridge compares RATIOS of resistances while the potentiometer measures a POTENTIAL against a calibrated drop.

**Student notes**

The wire is a ruler of potential: V = k·l with k = total drop/total length. At the null point the test cell pushes no current, so you read its TRUE EMF — this is the potentiometer's entire advantage over a voltmeter (which draws current and reads E − Ir). Keep the two currents straight: the driver current never stops; only the galvanometer branch is nulled. Know both protocols cold: comparison E₁/E₂ = l₁/l₂ (gradient cancels), and internal resistance r = R(l₁ − l₂)/l₂ from open/loaded balances. No balance anywhere? Check driver EMF > unknown and polarity before anything else. Longer wire or smaller driver current = finer sensitivity.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.kirchhoffs-laws
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): none yet mapped
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: The potentiometer is the second null instrument after the Wheatstone bridge (phys.em.wheatstone-bridge) — bridges compare resistances, potentiometers weigh potentials; both are Kirchhoff (phys.em.kirchhoffs-laws) turned into apparatus. Its reason for existing is the EMF/terminal-voltage split that internal resistance creates (phys.em.emf, next), and its ruler principle is resistivity's R ∝ L (phys.em.resistivity) put to work. The zero-draw ideal survives in every high-impedance instrument of modern electronics.

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.kirchhoffs-laws

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one E = k·l computation, one internal-resistance extraction by both routes, one 'why not a voltmeter?' explanation from the zero-draw principle. Monthly, restate the two-current distinction (driver flows, test branch nulls) and the two craft checks — they are the instrument in four sentences.

---

### Electrical Power and Joule Heating

*Concept ID: `phys.em.electrical-power` · Difficulty: developing · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 3h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to compute electrical power using P = VI = I²R = V²/R, choose the correct form for the quantity held fixed, convert power and time to energy and cost (kWh), explain Joule heating microscopically, and analyze why power transmission uses high voltage.

Electrical power is the rate of energy dissipation; Joule heating P = I²R converts electrical energy to heat in a resistor.

Electrical power is the rate at which a circuit converts electrical energy to other forms: P = VI — voltage is joules per coulomb, current is coulombs per second, so their product is joules per second, watts. With Ohm's law the one formula becomes three: P = VI = I²R = V²/R, and the craft is choosing by what is KNOWN or FIXED: I²R when the current through the element is in hand (series chains, transmission lines), V²/R when the voltage across it is pinned (parallel branches, mains appliances), VI when both are given. In a resistor all of this power becomes HEAT — Joule heating: the drifting electrons hand their field-won energy to the lattice collision by collision, and the wire warms. That is the working principle of every kettle, toaster, and fuse, and the enemy of every transmission engineer. The formulas carry a famous apparent paradox: P = I²R says more resistance means more heat, P = V²/R says less — resolved by asking what is held constant: at fixed CURRENT (series) power grows with R (the biggest resistor in a chain runs hottest); at fixed VOLTAGE (parallel, mains) power grows as R falls (the 'low' resistance kettle element draws more than the toaster). Energy is power × time — E = Pt — and the electricity meter's unit, the kilowatt-hour, is just a commercial joule: 1 kWh = 1000 W × 3600 s = 3.6 × 10⁶ J. The concept's crowning application is grid transmission: to deliver power P through a line of resistance R_line, the loss is I²R_line, so halving the current quarters the loss — hence transmit at maximum voltage and minimum current (P = VI fixed, V up, I down), the entire reason pylons run at hundreds of kilovolts and transformers exist.

**Key ideas**

- P = VI: joules per coulomb × coulombs per second = joules per second (watts) — power is the energy flow rate.
- Three forms via Ohm's law: P = VI = I²R = V²/R — choose by what's known/fixed: I²R for series/known current, V²/R for parallel/pinned voltage.
- Joule heating: in a resistor ALL delivered power becomes heat, electron collisions feeding the lattice — kettles and fuses by design, transmission losses by curse.
- The R-paradox resolved: at fixed I, power ∝ R (series: biggest resistor hottest); at fixed V, power ∝ 1/R (mains: lower-resistance appliance draws more).
- Energy = power × time: E = Pt; the kilowatt-hour is 3.6 × 10⁶ J — the meter's unit, and the one the bill is written in.
- Transmission at high voltage: line loss = I²R_line; delivering fixed P = VI at higher V slashes I and loss quadratically — the reason for 400 kV pylons and transformers.

**Vocabulary**

- **electrical power P** — Rate of electrical energy conversion: P = VI = I²R = V²/R; unit the watt (J/s).
- **Joule heating** — Conversion of electrical energy to heat in a resistance via electron–lattice collisions — P = I²R dissipated as warmth.
- **kilowatt-hour (kWh)** — The billing unit of energy: 1 kWh = 3.6 × 10⁶ J — one kilowatt sustained for one hour.
- **transmission loss** — Power wasted heating the line: P_loss = I²R_line — cut quadratically by raising transmission voltage at fixed delivered power.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* A higher-resistance appliance always dissipates more power, since P = I²R.
  *Correction:* Only at fixed CURRENT. Mains appliances sit in parallel at fixed VOLTAGE, where P = V²/R rules: the LOWER resistance draws more power. A 26 Ω kettle at 230 V pulls ~2 kW; a 1000 Ω lamp pulls ~53 W. Ask what's pinned before picking a formula.
- *Misconception:* Power and energy are the same thing — a 2 kW heater 'uses 2 kW of energy'.
  *Correction:* Power is a RATE (joules per second); energy is the accumulated total (joules, or kWh on the bill). The 2 kW heater converts 2000 J every second — run it two hours and it has used 4 kWh of ENERGY. Watts never appear on an electricity bill; kilowatt-hours do.
- *Misconception:* High-voltage transmission is dangerous overkill — transmitting at household voltage would be simpler and safer.
  *Correction:* Delivering the same power at 230 V instead of 230 kV means a thousand times the current — and a MILLION times the I²R line loss. High voltage is not bravado; it is the quadratic arithmetic of loss reduction, undone by transformers at the consumer's end.
- *Misconception:* A '100 W' bulb dissipates 100 W whatever circuit you put it in.
  *Correction:* The rating is a promise only at the rated voltage (100 W AT 230 V, implying a specific resistance). Put two such bulbs in series and each sees ~115 V — delivering roughly a quarter of rated power each (V²/R with half the V). Ratings are operating points, not properties.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Using P = V²/R with the supply voltage for a transmission line (the line sees only its own small drop).
- Confusing watts with watt-hours or joules — rate versus total.
- Applying I²R reasoning to parallel circuits (or V²/R to series) without checking what's fixed.
- Assuming rated power holds at any voltage.
- kWh arithmetic slips: forgetting to convert minutes to hours or W to kW.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- A formula-selector flowchart: 'What do you know/what is fixed?' branching to VI, I²R, V²/R, with a series-chain and a parallel-mains example at the leaves.
- The R-paradox split-screen: same three resistors in series (biggest glows hottest, P = I²R) versus in parallel (smallest glows hottest, P = V²/R).
- A transmission-loss slider: fixed delivered power, voltage slider from 230 V to 400 kV, line current and I²R loss recomputed live — loss collapsing quadratically.
- An energy-meter animation: a 2 kW kettle running with a kWh dial creeping and a running cost in currency — power as rate, energy as the accumulating bill.
- A Joule-heating microscopic view: electrons accelerating, colliding, lattice vibration (temperature) rising — the P = I²R energy hand-off frame by frame.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* An electric kettle is rated 2300 W at 230 V. (a) Find its operating current and element resistance. (b) How much energy does a 3.0-minute boil use, in joules and kWh? (c) The same 2300 W must be sent down a supply line of resistance 0.50 Ω. Compare the line loss when transmitted at 230 V versus at 2300 V.

1. (a) I = P/V = 2300/230 = 10 A; R = V/I = 230/10 = 23 Ω (check: V²/R = 230²/23 = 52900/23 = 2300 W ✓).
2. (b) E = Pt = 2300 × 180 = 4.14 × 10⁵ J. In commercial units: 2.3 kW × (3/60) h = 0.115 kWh — a boil costs about a tenth of a unit.
3. (c) At 230 V: line current I = P/V = 10 A → loss = I²R_line = 10² × 0.50 = 50 W.
4. At 2300 V: I = 2300/2300 = 1.0 A → loss = 1.0² × 0.50 = 0.50 W.
5. Ten times the voltage → one-tenth the current → ONE-HUNDREDTH the loss (2.2% down to 0.02% of delivered power): the quadratic payoff that justifies transformers and kilovolt lines.
6. Formula discipline replay: line loss used I²R (the line's current is what's known; the 230 V is NOT across the line, only ~5 V is) — using V²/R with the supply voltage here is the classic wrong turn.

*Outcome:* The student computes I = 10 A and R = 23 Ω, converts to 4.14 × 10⁵ J = 0.115 kWh, and demonstrates the 100× loss reduction from 10× voltage, explicitly using I²R_line with the line current rather than misapplying V²/R with the supply voltage.

**Real-world intuition**

- Every appliance nameplate is a power statement: kettles ~2 kW, phone chargers ~20 W, and the fuse is chosen from I = P/V.
- Electricity billing runs on E = Pt in kWh — energy audits and efficiency labels are this formula applied across a household.
- Fuses and thermal breakers are Joule heating harnessed: the fault current's I²R melts the sacrificial link before the wiring cooks.
- Grid design is loss arithmetic: 400 kV transmission, transformer substations, and thick low-R conductors all serve minimizing I²R_line.
- Electronics thermal design budgets every chip's V²/R and I²R dissipation — heat sinks and fans exist because Joule heating never sleeps.

**Practice progression**

Item types: three-form power computations (given any two of V, I, R, P), energy and cost conversions (Pt, kWh, tariff arithmetic), fixed-I versus fixed-V ranking problems (series/parallel brightness and heat), transmission-loss analyses at different voltages. Suggested item count: 10.

Begin with single-form plug-ins and kWh conversions, add form selection with the fixed-quantity question, then ranking problems across series/parallel, ending with two-voltage transmission comparisons and rating-versus-operating-point items.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, ranking and comparison problems, energy-cost tasks. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must select the correct power form for each constraint, compute energy and cost, and apply the loss arithmetic to transmission scenarios, beyond reciting P = VI.

Mastery signal: The student picks the right form by identifying the fixed quantity, converts power-time to joules and kWh correctly, resolves the R-paradox in both circuit contexts, and quantifies transmission-loss scaling, at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

The concept is one formula wearing three coats, and the whole game is the fixed-quantity question — drill 'what is pinned: I or V?' until it precedes every substitution. Stage the R-paradox deliberately (same resistors, series then parallel, predict the hottest) and let the contradiction force the resolution. Power versus energy deserves its own beat: bring a real electricity bill and translate it. The transmission calculation is the topic's showpiece — walk the 10×-voltage → 100×-less-loss arithmetic slowly, and flag the V²/R-with-supply-voltage trap explicitly (the voltage across the LINE is tiny). Bulb-rating questions (two 100 W bulbs in series) make excellent diagnostics.

**Student notes**

Three forms, one rule: know what's fixed. Series/known current → I²R; parallel/mains voltage → V²/R; both given → VI. Power is a rate (watts); energy is the total (joules; 1 kWh = 3.6 × 10⁶ J) — bills charge for energy. At fixed V, LOWER resistance means MORE power (kettle beats lamp); at fixed I it's the reverse. For transmission loss always use I²R_line with the LINE's current — never V²/R with the supply voltage. Appliance ratings hold only at rated voltage. Check answers against common sense: kettles ~10 A, bulbs well under 1 A.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.ohms-law
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): none yet mapped
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: Power fuses Ohm's law (phys.em.ohms-law) with the energy-rate concept from mechanics (phys.mech.power) — same watt, new plumbing. Joule heating is the collision story of resistivity (phys.em.resistivity) read as an energy ledger, and the ½QV charging loss met at capacitor energy (phys.em.energy-capacitor) is this concept's integral. EMF and internal resistance (phys.em.emf) split a source's power between load and self-heating; AC power and transformers (phys.em.ac-basics) industrialize the high-voltage transmission logic.

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.ohms-law

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one three-form computation with the fixed-quantity question answered aloud, one kWh cost calculation, one transmission two-voltage comparison. Monthly, restate the R-paradox resolution in two sentences (fixed I versus fixed V) — it audits every brightness and heating question in the domain.

---

### EMF, Internal Resistance, and Terminal Voltage

*Concept ID: `phys.em.emf` · Difficulty: proficient · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 4h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to define EMF as energy supplied per unit charge, distinguish it from terminal voltage via V = E − Ir, compute currents in circuits with internal resistance, determine EMF and r from V–I data, and analyze power sharing between the load and the source's interior.

EMF is the energy supplied per unit charge by a source; terminal voltage is reduced by the voltage drop across internal resistance.

A battery's label tells you its EMF; its terminals tell you less — and the gap between the two is this concept. EMF (electromotive force — a misnomer: it is not a force but ENERGY PER CHARGE, volts) is what the source's chemistry supplies to each coulomb it lifts through itself: E joules per coulomb, the full promise. But the source has an INTERNAL RESISTANCE r — its electrolyte and electrodes resist the very current they drive — so when a current I flows, Ir volts are spent inside before the charge ever reaches the terminals: the TERMINAL VOLTAGE is V = E − Ir. The equation is a short story with three chapters. Open circuit (I = 0): no internal drop, V = E — an ideal (zero-draw) voltmeter, or better a potentiometer, reads the true EMF. Loaded: V sags below E in proportion to the current — the headlights dim when the starter motor's huge I makes Ir bite. Short circuit (V = 0): the current maxes out at I = E/r, limited by nothing but the source's own innards — which is why shorting a car battery (tiny r, huge E/r) is a welding accident. Circuit analysis simply treats r as one more series resistor: I = E/(R + r), and every Kirchhoff loop through a real battery writes +E − Ir. Measurement inverts the story: plot terminal voltage against current drawn and V = E − Ir is a straight line — intercept E, slope −r — the standard experiment. Power bookkeeping completes it: the source converts EI per second, of which I²R lands in the load and I²r heats the battery (that is why hard-working batteries get warm). Maximizing LOAD power gives the maximum power transfer theorem — P_load peaks when R = r — but at only 50% efficiency, which is why power grids (efficiency first) run R ≫ r while signal electronics (transfer first) match impedances.

**Key ideas**

- EMF = energy per unit charge supplied by the source (volts, not a force) — the chemistry's full price paid to each coulomb.
- Internal resistance r: the source resists its own current; V_terminal = E − Ir — the terminals deliver the EMF minus the inside toll.
- Three regimes: open circuit V = E (measure EMF at zero draw); loaded V < E (sag ∝ I); short circuit I_max = E/r (the source's own limit).
- Circuit rule: treat r as a series resistor — I = E/(R + r); in Kirchhoff loops a real battery contributes +E and −Ir.
- The V–I line: terminal voltage versus current is straight with intercept E and slope −r — the standard determination experiment.
- Power split: EI total = I²R (load) + I²r (self-heating); load power peaks at R = r (maximum power transfer) but at 50% efficiency — grids choose efficiency (R ≫ r), signal circuits choose transfer.

**Vocabulary**

- **EMF (electromotive force) E** — Energy supplied per unit charge by a source (volts) — not a force; the source's full per-coulomb price.
- **internal resistance r** — The source's own opposition to the current it drives; drops Ir volts inside before the terminals.
- **terminal voltage V** — What the terminals actually deliver: V = E − Ir — equal to E only at zero current.
- **maximum power transfer** — Load power peaks when R = r — at exactly 50% efficiency; chosen for signals, shunned for power delivery.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* EMF is a force that pushes charges around the circuit.
  *Correction:* Despite the name, EMF is ENERGY PER CHARGE (volts, i.e., joules per coulomb) — the work the source does lifting each coulomb through itself. Nothing with units of newtons is involved; 'electromotive force' is a 200-year-old naming accident the equations have outgrown.
- *Misconception:* A battery's terminal voltage always equals its EMF — that's what the label means.
  *Correction:* Only at ZERO current. Under load, V = E − Ir: the terminals deliver less than the label by the internal drop. A '12 V' car battery cranking 200 A through r = 0.01 Ω shows only 10 V at its posts — same battery, different current, different terminal voltage.
- *Misconception:* A battery is a constant-current source — it always pushes the same current.
  *Correction:* A battery approximates a constant-VOLTAGE source (EMF fixed by chemistry); the CURRENT is set by the whole circuit: I = E/(R + r). Halve the load resistance and the current nearly doubles. Nothing in the battery meters out fixed amperes.
- *Misconception:* Maximum power transfer (R = r) is the goal of every circuit design.
  *Correction:* At R = r, half the power cooks the source — 50% efficiency, intolerable for energy delivery. Power systems run R ≫ r for efficiency near 100%; matching R = r is chosen only where extracting the most SIGNAL power matters more than waste (antennas, audio lines). Know which game you're playing.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Using V = E under load (forgetting the Ir sag).
- Dropping r from the series total (I = E/R instead of E/(R + r)).
- Reading the V–I slope as +r or the intercept as terminal voltage.
- Treating maximum power transfer as a universal design goal.
- Power-ledger errors: comparing I²R with EI instead of closing EI = I²R + I²r.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- A battery cutaway schematic: an ideal EMF cell in series with a small resistor r inside one casing, terminals outside — the model that makes every equation obvious.
- The three-regime animation: open circuit (V = E), increasing load (V sagging as Ir grows), short circuit (V = 0, I = E/r) — a terminal voltmeter and ammeter tracking throughout.
- The V–I experiment plotted live: data points falling on a straight line, intercept flagged E, slope flagged −r.
- An energy-ledger bar chart: EI total splitting into I²R (load, useful) and I²r (internal heat) as R is swept — with the R = r crossover and the efficiency curve overlaid.
- The car-cranking storyboard: headlights at full V, starter engaged (200 A), Ir bite, lights dimming — the everyday face of internal resistance.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A cell of EMF 12.0 V and internal resistance 0.50 Ω is connected to a 5.5 Ω load. (a) Find the current and the terminal voltage. (b) Find the power delivered to the load and the power wasted inside the cell. (c) What current would flow if the terminals were accidentally shorted? (d) In a V–I test this cell gave V = 11.0 V at I = 2.0 A and V = 10.0 V at I = 4.0 A — verify E and r from the data.

1. (a) I = E/(R + r) = 12.0/(5.5 + 0.50) = 12.0/6.0 = 2.0 A; V = E − Ir = 12.0 − 2.0 × 0.50 = 11.0 V (check: V = IR = 2.0 × 5.5 = 11.0 V ✓ — both routes must agree).
2. (b) Load: P_R = I²R = 4.0 × 5.5 = 22 W. Internal: P_r = I²r = 4.0 × 0.50 = 2.0 W. Total EI = 12.0 × 2.0 = 24 W ✓ — ledger closed; efficiency = 22/24 ≈ 92%.
3. (c) Short circuit: I_max = E/r = 12.0/0.50 = 24 A — five times the working current, limited only by the cell's interior; sustained, it cooks the cell (P_r = I²r = 288 W all inside).
4. (d) Two-point fit of V = E − Ir: slope = (10.0 − 11.0)/(4.0 − 2.0) = −0.50 → r = 0.50 Ω; intercept: E = V + Ir = 11.0 + 2.0 × 0.50 = 12.0 V ✓ — the graph method recovers both parameters.
5. Regime replay: open-circuit reading would be 12.0 V; the 11.0 V at 2.0 A is the loaded sag; 24 A is the short — one equation, three behaviors.
6. Design note: this cell wastes 8% at 2 A. Maximum LOAD power would need R = r = 0.5 Ω (I = 12 A, P_R = 72 W) — but then 72 W ALSO heats the cell: 50% efficiency, fine for a signal, ruinous for a power supply.

*Outcome:* The student computes I = 2.0 A and V = 11.0 V by two agreeing routes, closes the 24 W power ledger, finds the 24 A short-circuit limit, extracts E = 12.0 V and r = 0.50 Ω from V–I data, and distinguishes efficiency from maximum-transfer design goals.

**Real-world intuition**

- Car batteries are engineered for milliohm internal resistance so 200 A cranking only mildly sags the terminals — and the headlight dim during starting is V = E − Ir performed nightly.
- Phone battery health meters estimate r: as cells age, internal resistance climbs, voltage sags under load, and the phone dies at '30%' — the V–I line steepening with age.
- Battery-powered tools pick chemistry by r: high-drain devices need low-r cells (lithium) where high-r ones (alkaline) would waste their EMF internally.
- Impedance matching in audio and radio (R = r logic) maximizes signal power into speakers and antennas — the transfer theorem earning its keep where efficiency is secondary.
- Uninterruptible power supplies and grid batteries are specified by both E and r — the deliverable power at acceptable sag is E²-and-r arithmetic before anything else.

**Practice progression**

Item types: I = E/(R + r) and V = E − Ir computations across the three regimes, V–I graph interpretation (intercept/slope extraction of E and r), power-ledger problems (load versus internal, efficiency), design questions (battery choice by r, series cells, maximum transfer versus efficiency). Suggested item count: 10.

Begin with single-loop I and V computations, add the open/loaded/short regime contrasts, then graph-based determinations and power ledgers, ending with design trade-offs (R = r transfer versus R ≫ r efficiency) and multi-cell arrangements.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, graph-analysis tasks, design-reasoning problems. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must compute with internal resistance across regimes, extract parameters from data, and apply the power-split analysis to design choices, beyond defining EMF.

Mastery signal: The student solves loaded-circuit problems with agreeing V routes, reads E and r off V–I lines, closes power ledgers, and correctly assigns maximum-transfer versus efficiency criteria to contexts, at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Draw the battery model (ideal cell + series r inside one box) on day one and never let it leave the board — every equation in the topic is that picture read aloud. The three regimes make a strong lesson arc: measure a real cell open, loaded, and (safely, briefly, with a suitable cell) near-short. The V–I line experiment is the topic's assessment workhorse; have students extract E and r from their own data. Guard the two naming hazards: EMF is not a force, and a battery is not a constant-current source. The maximum-power-transfer theorem needs its efficiency caveat attached IMMEDIATELY — students who learn R = r as 'the goal' misdesign everything afterward. The car-cranking dim is the hook everyone recognizes.

**Student notes**

One picture: a perfect E in series with a small r, both inside the battery. Everything follows: I = E/(R + r), terminals show V = E − Ir = IR. Open circuit → V = E; loaded → V sags; short → I = E/r. On a V–I graph the intercept is E and the slope is −r — know this experiment. Power ledger: EI = I²R + I²r; efficiency = R/(R + r). Maximum LOAD power at R = r, but half the energy heats the battery — grids never do this on purpose. Always compute V both ways (E − Ir and IR) and make them agree.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.dc-circuits
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): none yet mapped
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: EMF completes the battery that dc-circuits (phys.em.dc-circuits) idealized — r is just one more series resistor, and Kirchhoff loops (phys.em.kirchhoffs-laws) write +E − Ir through every real source. The power split extends electrical power (phys.em.electrical-power) into the source itself, and the potentiometer (phys.em.potentiometer) exists precisely because V = E only at zero draw. Faraday's law (phys.em.faradays-law) will later mint EMF without any chemistry — same volts, new mechanism.

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.dc-circuits

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one loaded-circuit solve with the two-route V check, one E-and-r extraction from V–I data, one power ledger with efficiency. Monthly, recite the three regimes (open, loaded, short) with their equations — and the sentence 'EMF is energy per charge, not a force' until it is reflexive.

---

### RC Circuits: Charging and Discharging

*Concept ID: `phys.em.rc-circuits` · Difficulty: advanced · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 5h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to explain why capacitors charge and discharge exponentially, define and compute the time constant τ = RC, apply q(t), V(t), and I(t) for both charging and discharging, use the 63%/37% landmarks and half-life t½ = τ ln 2, and reason about capacitor behavior at t = 0 (acts like a wire) and t → ∞ (acts like a break).

In an RC circuit the capacitor charges and discharges exponentially with time constant τ = RC.

Put a resistor in series with a capacitor and the circuit acquires a MEMORY and a CLOCK. Close the switch on a battery E charging capacitor C through resistor R: Kirchhoff's loop law says E = IR + q/C at every instant — but as charge q accumulates, the capacitor's voltage rises and opposes the battery, shrinking the drive and hence the current that delivers further charge. Self-limiting growth is the signature of the EXPONENTIAL: q(t) = CE(1 − e^(−t/RC)), V_C(t) = E(1 − e^(−t/τ)), while the current starts at its maximum and dies away: I(t) = (E/R)e^(−t/τ). The governing scale is the TIME CONSTANT τ = RC (ohms × farads = seconds — a dimensional check worth performing once and believing forever): after one τ, charging reaches 63% of the way (1 − 1/e); after 2τ, 86%; 3τ, 95%; 5τ, over 99% — the engineer's 'fully charged'. Discharging is the mirror: q(t) = q₀e^(−t/τ), everything decaying by the factor 1/e ≈ 0.37 each τ, with the useful half-life t½ = τ ln 2 ≈ 0.693 RC (every half-life halves the charge — radioactive decay's exact mathematics, met here first in a circuit). Two limiting behaviors organize all qualitative reasoning: at t = 0 an uncharged capacitor is a WIRE (no voltage yet across it, current unimpeded, maximum I = E/R); as t → ∞ it is a BREAK (fully charged, opposing voltage equal to the source, zero current). Interrogate any RC circuit at these two instants and the exponential merely interpolates. The energy ledger from capacitance carries over with a twist: charging through ANY resistance banks ½CE² in the capacitor and burns an equal ½CE² in the resistor — the 50% tax is independent of R (R sets HOW LONG, not HOW MUCH). RC timing is everywhere: camera flashes recharging, intermittent wipers, the 555 timer, touchscreens sensing your finger's added C, and every heartbeat of a pacemaker.

**Key ideas**

- Self-limiting growth: charging obeys E = IR + q/C; accumulating charge opposes the source, so growth slows itself — the defining mechanism of the exponential.
- Time constant τ = RC (seconds): the circuit's natural clock — 63% charged after 1τ, 95% after 3τ, 'full' (>99%) after 5τ.
- Charging: q = CE(1 − e^(−t/τ)), V_C likewise; current I = (E/R)e^(−t/τ) starts at max and dies — charge grows, current decays, simultaneously.
- Discharging: pure decay q = q₀e^(−t/τ) — factor 0.37 per τ; half-life t½ = τ ln 2 ≈ 0.693RC halves everything each interval.
- Limit reasoning: at t = 0 an empty capacitor acts as a WIRE (I = E/R); at t → ∞ as a BREAK (I = 0) — solve circuits at both ends, let the exponential bridge.
- Energy: charging stores ½CE² and dissipates ½CE² in R regardless of R's value — resistance sets the timescale, never the split.

**Vocabulary**

- **time constant τ** — τ = RC (seconds): the RC circuit's natural time unit — 63% charged (or decayed to 37%) after one τ; ~full at 5τ.
- **exponential charging** — q = CE(1 − e^(−t/τ)): self-limiting growth as the capacitor's rising voltage opposes the source.
- **exponential decay** — q = q₀e^(−t/τ): discharge losing the same FRACTION per τ; half-life t½ = τ ln 2 ≈ 0.693RC.
- **wire/break limits** — An empty capacitor passes current like a wire (t = 0); a full one blocks like a break (t → ∞) — endpoint reasoning for any RC network.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* The capacitor charges at a steady rate until full, like filling a bucket from a steady tap.
  *Correction:* The tap throttles itself: as the capacitor's voltage rises it opposes the battery, the net drive (E − V_C) shrinks, and the charging current falls continuously — fast at first, ever slower. The approach is exponential, technically never complete; 5τ is the practical 'full'.
- *Misconception:* After one time constant the capacitor is fully charged.
  *Correction:* After 1τ it is 63% charged (1 − 1/e), not 100%. The τ landmarks are 63/86/95/99% at 1/2/3/5τ. One τ is the circuit's UNIT of time, not its finish line.
- *Misconception:* Current flows through the capacitor while it charges.
  *Correction:* No charge crosses the dielectric gap — ever. Current flows in the CIRCUIT: positive charge accumulates on one plate while an equal charge leaves the other, so the ammeter sees current 'through' the branch though nothing traverses the insulator. When accumulation stops, so does the current.
- *Misconception:* A bigger resistor means the capacitor stores less energy (since R eats more).
  *Correction:* R changes only the TIMESCALE. The final charge is CE and stored energy ½CE² whatever R is — and the resistor's total toll is ALSO fixed at ½CE² regardless of its value. Big R = same destination, slower trip, same total heat spread over more time.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Treating 1τ as 'fully charged' (it's 63%).
- Using the charging formula for discharge or vice versa.
- Unit slips in τ (kΩ and μF: 2 kΩ × 500 μF = 1 s, not 10⁶ s).
- Claiming current passes through the dielectric.
- Forgetting the current is maximum at t = 0, not zero — swap the curves and every answer inverts.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- The dual-curve plot: V_C(t) rising (1 − e^(−t/τ)) and I(t) falling (e^(−t/τ)) on shared axes, with the 1τ/63%, 3τ/95%, 5τ/99% landmarks flagged.
- A self-throttling animation: battery pushing charge onto plates, the capacitor's back-voltage growing, the net drive arrow (E − V_C) visibly shrinking, current arrows thinning.
- The two-limits diagram: the same circuit drawn at t = 0 (capacitor replaced by a wire) and t = ∞ (replaced by a break), each solved trivially.
- A discharge half-life staircase: q dropping 100 → 50 → 25 → 12.5% at equal t½ intervals — the halving rhythm that radioactivity will repeat.
- An RC timing gallery: intermittent wiper interval knob (varying R), camera-flash ready light, 555 timer — τ = RC as a designable product.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A 500 μF capacitor is charged through a 2.0 kΩ resistor from a 9.0 V battery, starting uncharged. (a) Find the time constant and the initial current. (b) Find the capacitor voltage at t = 1.0 s and t = 2.0 s. (c) How long until the capacitor is 95% charged, and what is the final stored charge and energy? (d) The battery is then removed and the capacitor discharges through the same resistor — find the half-life of the decay.

1. (a) τ = RC = 2.0 × 10³ × 500 × 10⁻⁶ = 1.0 s (Ω·F = s ✓). Initial current (empty capacitor = wire): I₀ = E/R = 9.0/2000 = 4.5 mA.
2. (b) V_C(t) = 9.0(1 − e^(−t/1.0)). At t = 1.0 s (= 1τ): V = 9.0(1 − e⁻¹) = 9.0 × 0.632 = 5.7 V. At t = 2.0 s: V = 9.0(1 − e⁻²) = 9.0 × 0.865 = 7.8 V — the second second added only 2.1 V where the first added 5.7 V: self-throttling in numbers.
3. (c) 95% ↔ 3τ = 3.0 s. Final charge Q = CE = 500 × 10⁻⁶ × 9.0 = 4.5 × 10⁻³ C = 4.5 mC; stored energy U = ½CE² = ½ × 500 × 10⁻⁶ × 81 = 2.0 × 10⁻² J ≈ 20 mJ.
4. (Ledger note: the resistor dissipated an equal ~20 mJ during charging — the R-independent 50% tax; the 2.0 kΩ only made it take seconds rather than microseconds.)
5. (d) Discharge: q = q₀e^(−t/τ) with the same τ = 1.0 s. Half-life: t½ = τ ln 2 = 0.693 s — every 0.69 s the charge (and voltage, and current) halves: 9.0 → 4.5 V at 0.69 s, → 2.25 V at 1.39 s.
6. Limits audit: t = 0 gave I = E/R (wire); t → ∞ gives I = 0 and V_C = 9.0 V (break) — the exponential answers agree with both endpoints, as they must.

*Outcome:* The student computes τ = 1.0 s and I₀ = 4.5 mA, evaluates the charging curve at 1τ and 2τ (5.7 V, 7.8 V), converts 95% to 3τ = 3.0 s with Q = 4.5 mC and U = 20 mJ, and finds the discharge half-life 0.69 s, using the wire/break limits as endpoint checks.

**Real-world intuition**

- Camera flashes are RC in both directions: slow charge through a big R (the whine and the ready light near 5τ), fast dump through the tube.
- Intermittent windscreen wipers time their pause with an adjustable R in an RC network — the interval knob is literally a τ dial.
- The 555 timer chip — billions made — clocks everything from LED blinkers to PWM motors by charging capacitors between fixed voltage thresholds.
- Touchscreens sense your fingertip as added capacitance: the changed RC response time at each grid point maps the touch.
- Cardiac pacemakers meter their life-keeping pulses with RC timing, and defibrillators are the discharge curve delivered clinically.

**Practice progression**

Item types: τ = RC computations with unit verification and landmark-percentage questions, charging/discharging curve evaluations (find V, q, or I at given t; find t for given fraction), limit-reasoning problems (circuit behavior at t = 0 and t → ∞, including multi-resistor networks), half-life and energy-ledger items (t½ = τ ln 2, the ½CE²/½CE² split). Suggested item count: 12.

Begin with τ computation and the 63/95/99 landmarks, add curve evaluations both directions (t → V and V → t via logarithms), then the two-limit analysis of richer circuits, ending with half-life chains and the R-independent energy-split argument.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, curve-analysis problems, limit-reasoning tasks. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must apply the exponential laws and time-constant landmarks to charging and discharging scenarios, and use the t = 0/t → ∞ limits to analyze circuits, beyond stating τ = RC.

Mastery signal: The student computes τ and evaluates both curves at arbitrary times (including inverse log solves), applies wire/break limit reasoning correctly, and reproduces the half-life and energy-split results, at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Derive the mechanism before the formula: the loop equation E = IR + q/C read aloud — 'the fuller it gets, the weaker the push' — makes the exponential inevitable rather than memorized. Establish the landmark table (1τ/63, 2τ/86, 3τ/95, 5τ/99) and the wire/break limits as the two reasoning tools; most exam questions fall to one or the other without touching e. Do the Ω × F = s dimension check once, ceremonially. A voltmeter across a charging capacitor with a stopwatch (τ of a few seconds) is a genuinely satisfying practical, and plotting ln V against t for discharge yields a straight line of slope −1/τ — a first taste of log-linearization. Flag the radioactivity connection explicitly: same mathematics, and students will meet it again in phys.mod.

**Student notes**

The clock is τ = RC — compute it FIRST in every problem (ohms × farads = seconds). Charging: V and q rise as (1 − e^(−t/τ)), current falls as e^(−t/τ); landmarks 63% at 1τ, 95% at 3τ, ~100% at 5τ. Discharging: everything decays by ×0.37 per τ, halving every 0.693τ. Reason at the ends: empty capacitor = wire (max current), full capacitor = break (zero current). To find a TIME from a fraction, take logs: t = τ ln(V₀/V). Energy: stored ½CE², and an equal amount is always lost in R — R sets speed, not the split. No charge ever crosses the gap.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.capacitance, phys.em.kirchhoffs-laws
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): none yet mapped
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: RC circuits animate capacitance (phys.em.capacitance) with Kirchhoff's loop law (phys.em.kirchhoffs-laws) as the engine, and the ½/½ energy split proves the charging tax first met at capacitor energy (phys.em.energy-capacitor). The exponential-with-time-constant pattern is physics' favorite tune: damped oscillations sang it (phys.wave.damped-oscillations), radioactive decay will sing it again (phys.mod), and LR/LC circuits (phys.em.self-inductance, phys.em.lc-circuits) transpose it to magnetism.

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.capacitance
- phys.em.kirchhoffs-laws

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one τ computation with landmark questions, one curve evaluation each way (t → V and V → t by logs), one wire/break limit analysis of a two-resistor RC network. Monthly, recite the landmark table and the half-life formula from memory — the same exponential grammar reappears in inductors and nuclear decay.

---

### Magnetic Field and Field Lines

*Concept ID: `phys.em.magnetic-field` · Difficulty: developing · Bloom level: understand · Mastery threshold: 0.75 · Estimated study time: 3h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to describe the magnetic field as the agent of magnetic force with symbol B and unit tesla, draw and interpret field-line patterns (bar magnet, straight wire, loop), apply the right-hand rule for the field around a current, state that field lines form closed loops with no magnetic monopoles, and connect currents — not stationary charges — to magnetism.

A magnetic field exerts forces on moving charges and current-carrying conductors; field lines indicate its direction and strength.

Magnetism gets its own field: B, the magnetic field, measured in tesla (T) — the agent that transmits magnetic influence through space exactly as E transmits electric influence. But B differs from E in its very source: while charges at REST make electric fields, magnetic fields are made by charges in MOTION — currents. That is the domain's hinge discovery (Oersted, 1820: a compass needle deflects beside a current-carrying wire — electricity and magnetism, separate sciences for centuries, unified in one lecture-hall accident). The field-line language carries over from electrostatics with the same grammar — direction along the tangent, strength in the crowding, lines never crossing — but with one profound structural change: magnetic field lines have NO ENDS. Electric lines run from + to −; magnetic lines form CLOSED LOOPS, because there are no magnetic charges — no monopoles. Cut a bar magnet in half and you get two smaller magnets, never a separated north and south; every north comes welded to a south, and 'poles' are just the places where loops crowd into or out of the material. The three canonical patterns are the topic's vocabulary: a BAR MAGNET's loops emerge from N, arc around, re-enter at S, and continue THROUGH the body back to N (the outside pattern matches a current loop's — a hint of magnetism's true source); a STRAIGHT WIRE wraps circles around itself, direction by the RIGHT-HAND RULE (thumb along the current, fingers curl along B), strength thinning with distance — quantitatively B = μ₀I/2πr, with μ₀ = 4π × 10⁻⁷ T·m/A the permeability of free space (formula quoted here; its derivation is Biot–Savart's job later); a LOOP of current concentrates field through its face like a little bar magnet. Scale calibrates intuition: Earth's field ~5 × 10⁻⁵ T (30 A of current 5 cm away rivals it — why wiring confuses compasses), fridge magnets ~10⁻² T, MRI machines 1.5–3 T, and the tesla is huge. What B DOES — the force on moving charges, F = qv × B — is the next concept's whole story; here the mission is the field's existence, sources, and geometry.

**Key ideas**

- B is the magnetic field: the space-filling agent of magnetic influence, unit the tesla (T = N/(A·m)) — a big unit; Earth's field is ~5 × 10⁻⁵ T.
- Source hinge: MOVING charges (currents) create magnetic fields — stationary charges make only E. Oersted's deflected compass unified electricity and magnetism.
- Field-line grammar carries over (tangent = direction, crowding = strength, no crossing) with one great exception: magnetic lines have no ends — they form closed loops.
- No monopoles: every N is welded to an S; halving a magnet yields two dipoles. Poles are where loops enter/exit the material, not point sources.
- Right-hand rule for a wire: thumb along the current, fingers curl the field circles; B = μ₀I/2πr around a straight wire (μ₀ = 4π × 10⁻⁷ T·m/A) — quoted here, derived at Biot–Savart.
- Canonical patterns: bar magnet (external loops N→S, closed through the body), straight wire (concentric circles), current loop (a face-threading field — a bar magnet in miniature).

**Vocabulary**

- **magnetic field B** — The space-filling agent of magnetic influence, sourced by moving charges; unit the tesla (T) — Earth's field is ~5 × 10⁻⁵ T.
- **field lines (magnetic)** — Tangent gives direction, crowding gives strength — and every line is a CLOSED LOOP; none begin or end.
- **magnetic monopole** — A hypothetical isolated N or S — never observed; every magnet is a dipole, and halving one makes two.
- **right-hand rule (wire)** — Thumb along the current, fingers curl in the sense of the circular field lines around it; B = μ₀I/2πr.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* Magnetic field lines start at the north pole and end at the south pole.
  *Correction:* They have no ends at all: outside the magnet they run N to S, but they continue THROUGH the magnet from S back to N, closing every loop. Unlike electric lines (which terminate on charges), magnetic lines cannot end — there are no magnetic charges to end on.
- *Misconception:* Cutting a bar magnet in half separates the north pole from the south pole.
  *Correction:* You get two complete magnets, each with its own N and S — and halving again just makes more dipoles, down to the atomic scale. No experiment has ever isolated a monopole; poles are features of the loop pattern, not detachable objects.
- *Misconception:* Any charge creates a magnetic field, just as it creates an electric field.
  *Correction:* A charge at rest creates ONLY an electric field. Magnetic fields require MOTION — currents. That asymmetry is the domain's architecture: electrostatics from charge, magnetism from charge in motion (and, deeper, the two are one phenomenon viewed from different frames — a relativity-flavored footnote for later).
- *Misconception:* The magnetic field around a wire points away from the wire, like E from a charged line.
  *Correction:* B wraps AROUND the wire in closed circles — no component toward or away from it. The right-hand rule gives the circulation sense: thumb with the current, fingers curl with B. Radial is electric grammar; circulating is magnetic grammar.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Drawing field lines that end at the poles (they must close through the magnet).
- Expecting to isolate a pole by cutting.
- Giving radial directions around a wire instead of circulating ones.
- Using the left hand, or curling from the field to find the current backward.
- Treating B ∝ 1/r² (single wire is 1/r; inverse square belongs to point sources).

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- The classic iron-filings triptych (real or simulated): bar magnet, straight wire, current loop — the three canonical patterns discovered rather than asserted.
- A closed-loop revelation diagram: the bar magnet's field drawn INCLUDING the interior segment S→N, every line a complete loop — beside an electric dipole's terminating lines for contrast.
- The magnet-cutting animation: halving yielding two dipoles, repeatedly, down to atomic current loops — the no-monopole rule made procedural.
- A right-hand-rule practice widget: wires at random orientations, student curls a rendered hand, field circles appear with the correct sense.
- A field-strength scale ladder (log): Earth 5 × 10⁻⁵ T, fridge magnet 10⁻² T, MRI 3 T, neutron star 10⁸ T — the tesla calibrated.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A long straight cable carries 30 A. (a) Using B = μ₀I/2πr, find the field strength 5.0 cm from the cable and compare it with Earth's field (~5.0 × 10⁻⁵ T). Would a compass held there be disturbed? (b) Sketch/describe the field pattern and give its direction on all sides for a current flowing north, using the right-hand rule. (c) At what distance from the cable does its field fall to one-tenth of Earth's?

1. (a) B = μ₀I/2πr = (4π × 10⁻⁷ × 30)/(2π × 0.050) = (2 × 10⁻⁷ × 30)/0.050 = 6.0 × 10⁻⁶/0.050 = 1.2 × 10⁻⁴ T.
2. Comparison: 1.2 × 10⁻⁴/5.0 × 10⁻⁵ = 2.4 — the cable's field is about 2.4 times Earth's, so a compass 5 cm away would swing far from north (badly disturbed); household wiring genuinely confuses nearby compasses.
3. (b) The field is concentric circles around the cable. Right-hand rule with the current flowing north (thumb north): fingers curl so B points WEST above the cable, EAST below it, UP on the east side, DOWN on the west side — circulation, never radial.
4. (c) Set B = 5.0 × 10⁻⁶ T: r = μ₀I/2πB = (2 × 10⁻⁷ × 30)/5.0 × 10⁻⁶ = 6.0 × 10⁻⁶/5.0 × 10⁻⁶ = 1.2 m.
5. Read the scaling: B ∝ 1/r (single power, not inverse square) — moving from 5 cm to 1.2 m (×24) cut the field ×24, from 2.4 Earths to a tenth of one.
6. Grammar audit: every direction answer was a TANGENT to a circle around the wire — any 'points away from the wire' answer fails the closed-loop rule on sight.

*Outcome:* The student computes 1.2 × 10⁻⁴ T and judges the compass badly disturbed, assigns circulation directions on all four sides via the right-hand rule, inverts the formula to r = 1.2 m, and articulates the 1/r (not 1/r²) falloff and the circulating (not radial) geometry.

**Real-world intuition**

- Navigation by compass — from ships to smartphones (magnetometers) — reads Earth's ~50 μT field, and must compensate for nearby currents and iron.
- MRI machines image the body inside a 1.5–3 T field from superconducting coils — the tesla scale put to clinical work.
- Electric motors and loudspeakers are current-and-field machines: every one is built on the field patterns of wires and loops drawn in this concept.
- Maglev trains ride engineered magnetic-field geometry — levitation and guidance by shaped B.
- Magnetic stripe readers, hard-disk heads, and security tags all write and read information as patterns of field — geometry of B as data.

**Practice progression**

Item types: field-pattern identification and sketching (bar magnet, wire, loop — with direction labels), right-hand-rule direction drills at varied orientations, B = μ₀I/2πr computations (solve for B, I, or r; compare with Earth's field), conceptual items on closed loops, no monopoles, and the moving-charge source rule. Suggested item count: 10.

Begin with pattern recognition and right-hand-rule drills, add single-wire field computations and Earth-field comparisons, then inverse solves and scaling questions, ending with conceptual contrasts against electric-field grammar (loops versus terminating lines, circulation versus radial).

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: diagram-labelling tasks, computation set, conceptual quiz. Bloom alignment: Understand — students must explain the field's sources, the closed-loop/no-monopole structure, and the canonical patterns with correct directions, beyond substituting into the wire formula.

Mastery signal: The student draws the three canonical patterns with correct closed-loop topology, applies the right-hand rule reliably, computes and compares field strengths at the 1/r scaling, and explains why motion is required to make B, at 75% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Let Oersted's accident be the opening act — a compass and a battery wire recreate the founding demo in thirty seconds and land the moving-charge source rule better than any assertion. Iron filings (or a good simulation) should generate the three canonical patterns before they are drawn formally. Push the closed-loop topology hard: have students complete the bar magnet's INTERIOR lines, and contrast with an electric dipole's terminating lines side by side — that contrast is the deep content. Quote B = μ₀I/2πr as an experimental result with its derivation promissory-noted to Biot–Savart; resist deriving early. Drill the right-hand rule physically (everyone's actual right hand, wires in several orientations) — kinesthetic beats diagrammatic. Keep force questions parked: 'what B does' is the next lesson, and mixing the two rules confuses both.

**Student notes**

New field, new source: B comes from MOVING charge — currents — never from charge at rest. Its unit is the tesla (huge; Earth's field is ~5 × 10⁻⁵ T). Field-line reading works as before (tangent, crowding) with one big change: magnetic lines are CLOSED LOOPS — they run through the magnet too, and no line ever ends, because monopoles don't exist (cut a magnet: two magnets). Know the three patterns cold: bar magnet, wire (concentric circles, right-hand rule: thumb = current, curl = B), loop (a mini bar magnet). Around a wire B = μ₀I/2πr — falls as 1/r, and points along circles, never toward/away. Force rules come next; don't mix rules yet.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.electric-current
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): phys.em.magnetic-flux, phys.em.magnetic-force, phys.em.magnetic-materials
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: The field concept transplants from electric fields (phys.em.electric-field) — same mapping language, new source rule (currents, from phys.em.electric-current) and new topology (closed loops). What B does to moving charges is the Lorentz force (phys.em.magnetic-force, next); where each piece of a circuit's B comes from is Biot–Savart (phys.em.biot-savart); flux through surfaces (phys.em.magnetic-flux) and materials' response (phys.em.magnetic-materials) build directly on today's geometry. The no-monopole rule will be enshrined as one of Maxwell's equations (phys.em.maxwells-equations).

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.electric-current

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one three-pattern sketch set with directions, one right-hand-rule drill batch, one B = μ₀I/2πr computation with an Earth-field comparison. Monthly, restate the two structural laws — moving charges make B; magnetic lines close on themselves (no monopoles) — they are two of Maxwell's four, met early.

---

### Magnetic Force on Moving Charges — Lorentz Force

*Concept ID: `phys.em.magnetic-force` · Difficulty: proficient · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 5h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to apply the Lorentz force F = q(E + v × B), compute the magnetic force magnitude qvB sin θ with direction from the right-hand rule, explain why magnetic forces do no work, analyze circular motion of charges in uniform fields (r = mv/qB, period independent of speed), and compute the force F = BIL on a current-carrying conductor.

The Lorentz force F = q(E + v × B) acts on a charge q moving with velocity v in electric and magnetic fields.

Now the magnetic field acts. The complete electromagnetic force on a charge is the LORENTZ FORCE, F = q(E + v × B): the electric part qE pushes along the field regardless of motion; the magnetic part qv × B is stranger in every way. Its magnitude is F = qvB sin θ — zero for a charge at rest (v = 0) and zero for motion parallel to the field (θ = 0), maximal when charge cuts across the field at right angles. Its direction is the cross product's: perpendicular to BOTH v and B, given by the right-hand rule (fingers point along v, curl toward B, thumb gives F for positive charge — reverse for negative). That perpendicularity has a profound consequence: the magnetic force NEVER does work. Always at right angles to the velocity, it can bend a trajectory but never speed it up or slow it down — kinetic energy is untouchable by B alone.
