The signature motion follows: a charge moving perpendicular to a uniform field is bent into a CIRCLE, the magnetic force playing centripetal: qvB = mv²/r gives radius r = mv/qB (fast or heavy → wide circle; strong field or big charge → tight one), and the period T = 2πm/qB is INDEPENDENT of speed — faster particles run bigger circles in exactly the same time, the resonance secret of the cyclotron. Velocity with a component along B adds an unbent drift: the path becomes a HELIX winding along the field line — the mechanism that funnels solar-wind particles along Earth's field toward the poles (auroras). Since currents are marching charges, a wire in a field feels the summed force: F = BIL sin θ on length L, direction again by the right-hand rule (fingers along conventional I) — the seed of every motor. Crossed E and B fields make a velocity selector (qE = qvB passes only v = E/B), and the same force physics runs mass spectrometers, cyclotrons, and the Hall effect.

**Key ideas**

- Lorentz force: F = q(E + v × B) — the electric part acts always; the magnetic part only on MOVING charge, F = qvB sin θ.
- Direction by right-hand rule (v curled into B, thumb = F for + charge; flip for −): always perpendicular to both v and B.
- Magnetic forces do NO WORK: forever perpendicular to velocity, they bend paths but never change speed or kinetic energy.
- Perpendicular entry → circular motion: qvB = mv²/r, so r = mv/qB; period T = 2πm/qB is speed-independent — the cyclotron's resonance principle.
- Velocity along B is unaffected → helical paths winding along field lines (aurora funnel); crossed fields select speed v = E/B.
- Current version: F = BIL sin θ on a straight wire (fingers along I) — charges' forces summed, and the working principle of motors.

**Vocabulary**

- **Lorentz force** — The full electromagnetic force on a charge: F = q(E + v × B) — electric part always, magnetic part only in motion.
- **right-hand rule (force)** — Fingers along v, curl toward B, thumb gives F on a POSITIVE charge; reverse for negative. F ⊥ v and F ⊥ B always.
- **cyclotron radius & period** — r = mv/qB and T = 2πm/qB — the period contains no v: all speeds lap in equal time.
- **force on a wire** — F = BIL sin θ on length L carrying current I — the per-charge Lorentz force summed into a macroscopic push; motor principle.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* The magnetic force pushes charges along the field lines, like the electric force does.
  *Correction:* The magnetic force is PERPENDICULAR to the field (and to the velocity) — a charge is never pushed along B by the magnetic force. Motion along B feels no force at all; the along-the-line picture belongs to E. Confusing the two grammars is the domain's most common wrong turn.
- *Misconception:* A magnetic field speeds particles up — that's how accelerators work.
  *Correction:* B alone cannot change a particle's speed: the force is always at right angles to the motion, so it does zero work. Cyclotrons BEND with the magnetic field and ACCELERATE with an electric field in the gaps — B steers, E feeds energy. Steering and speeding are different jobs.
- *Misconception:* A stationary charge near a magnet feels a magnetic force.
  *Correction:* No motion, no magnetic force: F = qvB sin θ vanishes at v = 0. A charge sitting beside the strongest MRI magnet feels nothing magnetic. (A moving OBSERVER complicates the story — a hint that E and B are frame-dependent faces of one field — but in your frame, static charge means zero magnetic force.)
- *Misconception:* Faster particles in a cyclotron take longer per revolution, since the circle is bigger.
  *Correction:* The period T = 2πm/qB contains no v: faster particles ride proportionally larger circles and complete them in exactly the same time. That speed-independence is precisely why a fixed-frequency alternating voltage stays synchronized — it is the design principle, not a coincidence (until relativity spoils it at near-light speeds).

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Pushing charges along field lines (electric grammar applied to B).
- Forgetting the negative-charge flip of the right-hand rule.
- Claiming the field does work or changes speed in circular motion.
- Putting v into the period (T = 2πm/qB has none).
- Using sin θ = 1 blindly when v or I is not perpendicular to B.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- A right-hand-rule trainer: randomized v and B arrows, student orients a rendered hand, F appears — with negative-charge reversal drills.
- The no-work animation: a charge circling in uniform B with speed and kinetic-energy meters pinned constant while the velocity ARROW rotates.
- A circular-to-helical morph: perpendicular entry giving a circle, then a growing parallel velocity component stretching it into a helix along the field line — ending with the aurora funnel at Earth's poles.
- A cyclotron schematic in motion: two dees, B bending the path, E kicking at each gap crossing, radius growing while the lap time stays fixed.
- The F = BIL jumping-wire demo (or video): a rod on rails in a magnet gap leaping when current flows, direction flipping with current or field reversal.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A proton (m = 1.67 × 10⁻²⁷ kg, q = 1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹ C) enters a uniform field B = 0.50 T at v = 2.0 × 10⁶ m/s, perpendicular to B. (a) Find the magnetic force on it. (b) Find the radius of its circular path and its period. (c) How much work does the field do per revolution? (d) A wire 0.20 m long carrying 15 A sits perpendicular to the same field — find the force on it.

1. (a) F = qvB sin 90° = 1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹ × 2.0 × 10⁶ × 0.50 = 1.6 × 10⁻¹³ N — tiny in newtons, but colossal against the proton's weight (~1.6 × 10⁻²⁶ N): thirteen orders of magnitude; gravity is irrelevant to particle optics.
2. (b) The force is centripetal: r = mv/qB = (1.67 × 10⁻²⁷ × 2.0 × 10⁶)/(1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹ × 0.50) = 3.34 × 10⁻²¹/8.0 × 10⁻²⁰ = 4.2 × 10⁻² m ≈ 4.2 cm.
3. Period: T = 2πm/qB = (2π × 1.67 × 10⁻²⁷)/8.0 × 10⁻²⁰ = 1.05 × 10⁻²⁶/8.0 × 10⁻²⁰ ≈ 1.3 × 10⁻⁷ s — and note v appears nowhere: a proton at twice the speed rides a 8.4 cm circle in the same 0.13 μs.
4. (c) Zero. The force is perpendicular to the velocity at every instant, so W = 0 over any path segment and any number of revolutions — the speed stays 2.0 × 10⁶ m/s forever (in B alone).
5. (d) F = BIL sin 90° = 0.50 × 15 × 0.20 = 1.5 N — a full, hand-feelable newton and a half: summed over ~10²³ drifting electrons, the same force that was 10⁻¹³ N per particle runs motors.
6. Direction discipline: each force answer requires the right-hand rule with the actual geometry; for the wire, fingers follow CONVENTIONAL current. Every F must come out perpendicular to both its v (or I) and B — any answer along B fails on sight.

*Outcome:* The student computes F = 1.6 × 10⁻¹³ N, r ≈ 4.2 cm, and T ≈ 1.3 × 10⁻⁷ s, states the speed-independence of T, answers zero work with the perpendicularity argument, and finds the 1.5 N wire force — applying the right-hand rule consistently throughout.

**Real-world intuition**

- Mass spectrometers identify molecules by r = mv/qB: same speed in, each mass bends onto its own radius — chemistry, forensics, and Mars rovers all carry one.
- Cyclotrons and synchrotrons steer particle beams with B and feed them energy with E — medical isotope production and the LHC alike.
- Every electric motor is F = BIL harnessed: current-carrying loops forced to turn in a field.
- The aurora is Lorentz-force choreography: solar-wind charges spiral helically along Earth's field lines into the polar atmosphere.
- Hall-effect sensors (in phones, cars, brushless motors) read the sideways qv × B push on carriers as a voltage — the force turned into a measurement.

**Practice progression**

Item types: force computations F = qvB sin θ and F = BIL sin θ with right-hand-rule directions, circular-motion problems (r, T, frequency; solve for m, q, or B — mass-spectrometer style), no-work and energy-invariance reasoning items, combined-field problems (velocity selector qE = qvB, helical paths). Suggested item count: 12.

Begin with magnitude computations and direction drills, add circular-motion solves in both directions, then work/energy reasoning and speed-independent-period questions, ending with velocity selectors, helices, and mass-spectrometer compositions.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, direction-drill batteries, trajectory-analysis problems. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must apply the Lorentz force in magnitude and direction to particle and wire scenarios, and use the circular-motion relations quantitatively, beyond stating F = qv × B.

Mastery signal: The student computes forces with correct right-hand-rule directions (including negative charges), solves r = mv/qB problems both ways, defends the zero-work property, and handles selector/helix compositions, at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Two grammars must be kept apart from the first minute: E pushes ALONG its field, B pushes PERPENDICULAR to everything — run a compare-and-contrast table early. Drill the right-hand rule kinesthetically and include negative charges from the start (electrons dominate real devices). The no-work theorem deserves a full-class proof moment: perpendicular force, zero work, constant speed — then immediately ask 'so how does a cyclotron accelerate anything?' and let the E-in-the-gaps answer land. Derive r = mv/qB from qvB = mv²/r as circular-motion revision, and make students discover the v-free period themselves — it's the topic's best 'aha'. The jumping-wire demo makes F = BIL visceral in ten seconds. Defer torque on loops to the magnetic-dipole concept.

**Student notes**

Magnetic force: F = qvB sin θ, direction from the right-hand rule (flip it for electrons), ALWAYS perpendicular to both v and B. Consequences to own: no force on static charge or on motion along B; no work EVER (B bends, never speeds); perpendicular entry gives a circle with r = mv/qB and a period T = 2πm/qB that ignores speed. Helix = circle + drift along B. Velocity selector: v = E/B passes straight. Wire version: F = BIL sin θ, fingers along conventional current. Check every direction answer: if your F has a component along B, it's wrong.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.magnetic-field, phys.em.electric-field
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): phys.em.biot-savart
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: The Lorentz force unites the two fields built so far (phys.em.electric-field, phys.em.magnetic-field) into one law, and its circular motion is centripetal dynamics (phys.mech.circular-motion) with a new force in the role. F = BIL seeds the motor torque of magnetic dipoles (phys.em.magnetic-dipole), and moving-charge-feels-force pairs with moving-charge-makes-field (phys.em.biot-savart, next) to close the interaction loop. The frame-dependence hint (whose v?) foreshadows special relativity (phys.rel).

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.magnetic-field
- phys.em.electric-field

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one force computation with direction, one r-and-T solve with the speed-independence stated, one no-work explanation. Monthly, redo a velocity-selector or spectrometer composite — the Lorentz force is the working tool of every charged-particle device in modern physics.

---

### Biot-Savart Law

*Concept ID: `phys.em.biot-savart` · Difficulty: advanced · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 6h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to state the Biot–Savart law dB = (μ₀/4π)(I dl × r̂)/r² as the field contribution of a current element, explain its structure (inverse-square, perpendicular geometry, superposition), apply its standard results — the circular-loop center B = μ₀I/2R (and N-turn coils), the long straight wire B = μ₀I/2πr — and choose between them by geometry.

The Biot-Savart law gives the infinitesimal magnetic field contribution dB from a current element Id𝑙.

Biot–Savart is magnetism's Coulomb law: it prices the field made by each infinitesimal piece of a current. A current element I dl (a short arrow of wire carrying current I) contributes dB = (μ₀/4π)(I dl × r̂)/r² at a point distance r away — and every clause of the formula teaches. The 1/r² is the familiar inverse square of point sources. The current element I dl replaces the point charge q — magnetism's source is not charge but charge IN MOTION, dealt out along the wire. The CROSS PRODUCT dl × r̂ encodes the strange geometry: the contribution is perpendicular to both the element and the line to the field point, strongest broadside to the element (sin θ = 1) and exactly ZERO straight ahead of or behind it (a current element throws no field along its own axis). The constant μ₀/4π = 10⁻⁷ T·m/A makes the numbers honest. No lone current element exists — currents flow in closed circuits — so the law is used by SUPERPOSITION: integrate the contributions around the actual wire. Two integrals do most of physics' daily work, and both should be owned as results with their logic. CENTER OF A CIRCULAR LOOP: every element is perpendicular to the line to the center at the same distance R, so contributions simply pile up: B = μ₀I/2R, multiplied to B = μ₀NI/2R for an N-turn coil — the field-concentration trick behind every electromagnet coil. LONG STRAIGHT WIRE: the full integral delivers B = μ₀I/2πr — the concentric-circle field quoted back at magnetic-field, now derived, its single power of 1/r explained (contributions from an infinite line accumulate, softening the point-source 1/r² to 1/r — the same geometry-driven softening seen for the infinite charged line in electrostatics). Comparing the two results at equal distance (loop center versus wire at r = R) gives B_loop/B_wire = π: curling the wire around the point concentrates its field π-fold. Biot–Savart handles ANY geometry by honest integration (arcs contribute in proportion to their angle; straight segments need the full treatment), but for high-symmetry cases a shortcut is coming — Ampère's law is to Biot–Savart what Gauss was to Coulomb.

**Key ideas**

- dB = (μ₀/4π)(I dl × r̂)/r²: each current element contributes an inverse-square, cross-product field — magnetism's Coulomb law, sourced by moving charge.
- Geometry clause: dB ⊥ both dl and r̂, maximal broadside (sin θ = 1), ZERO along the element's own axis — the cross product is the physics, not decoration.
- μ₀/4π = 10⁻⁷ T·m/A exactly — the magnetic constant in its most computable form.
- Fields of real circuits come by superposition: integrate dB around the closed wire — no isolated current element exists.
- Standard result 1 — loop center: B = μ₀I/2R (all elements broadside and equidistant, contributions aligned); N turns multiply: B = μ₀NI/2R.
- Standard result 2 — long straight wire: B = μ₀I/2πr derived; the 1/r (not 1/r²) falloff is the infinite-line accumulation, and the loop-versus-wire comparison gives the π-fold concentration of curling the current around the point.

**Vocabulary**

- **Biot–Savart law** — dB = (μ₀/4π)(I dl × r̂)/r² — the inverse-square, cross-product field of a current element; circuit fields follow by integration.
- **current element I dl** — An infinitesimal directed piece of current-carrying wire — magnetism's source unit, replacing the point charge.
- **loop-center field** — B = μ₀I/2R for one turn, μ₀NI/2R for N — all elements broadside and equidistant, the coil designer's formula.
- **axis dead zone** — dl × r̂ = 0 along a current element's own line: no field contribution straight ahead or behind — radial feed wires vanish at a loop's center.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* A current element creates field all around it equally, like a point charge does.
  *Correction:* The cross product kills the field along the element's axis: dB ∝ sin θ, zero straight ahead and behind, maximal broadside. A current element is a directional lamp, not a bare bulb — and summing these directional contributions is why circuit geometry matters so much.
- *Misconception:* The straight wire's B = μ₀I/2πr falls as 1/r, so Biot–Savart must not really be inverse-square.
  *Correction:* Each ELEMENT contributes as 1/r² exactly; the 1/r of the whole wire is what an infinite line of inverse-square contributions integrates to — nearer the wire, more of it matters. Same resolution as the charged line in electrostatics: the law is about elements, the falloff of a TOTAL depends on the source's shape.
- *Misconception:* Since the loop-center formula is B = μ₀I/2R, a bigger loop gives a bigger field.
  *Correction:* R is in the DENOMINATOR: a bigger loop puts every element farther from the center and the field is WEAKER. Field concentration wants small, many-turned coils (B = μ₀NI/2R) — small R and large N is the electromagnet recipe.
- *Misconception:* You must redo the full integral for every problem.
  *Correction:* The standard results are reusable tools: loop center μ₀I/2R (N turns: ×N), straight wire μ₀I/2πr, arcs in proportion to their angle (a half-loop gives half of μ₀I/2R). Recognize the geometry, deploy the result, and reserve honest integration for genuinely new shapes.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Granting a current element field along its own axis (the cross product forbids it).
- Forgetting the N in multi-turn coils, or putting R upstairs in the loop formula.
- Using the loop formula off-center or the wire formula for short segments.
- Missing the arc-fraction shortcut and mis-integrating quarter/half loops.
- Treating the wire's 1/r as contradicting the element's 1/r².

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- A current-element 'directional lamp' rendering: dB magnitude drawn over all directions around one element — broadside maximum, dead zones fore and aft along its axis.
- The loop-center superposition animation: contributions from elements around the ring, each perpendicular and equal, stacking into the axial B = μ₀I/2R.
- The straight-wire integral pictured: elements along the line, near ones contributing strongly, far ones weakly, the running sum converging to μ₀I/2πr.
- A loop-versus-wire comparison graphic at the same distance: the π-fold concentration of curling the current around the point, with both numbers computed.
- An arc calculator: fields at the center of quarter-, half-, and full loops (proportional to angle), composed with straight-segment feeds — the exam-problem toolkit visualized.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A circular coil of radius R = 0.10 m carries I = 5.0 A. (a) Find the field at its center for a single turn. (b) Compare with the field 0.10 m from a long straight wire carrying the same current, and explain the ratio. (c) The coil is rewound with N = 100 turns at the same radius — find the new central field. (d) What field would a half-loop (semicircle) of the same radius and current produce at the center?

1. (a) B = μ₀I/2R = (4π × 10⁻⁷ × 5.0)/(2 × 0.10) = (6.28 × 10⁻⁶)/0.20 = 3.1 × 10⁻⁵ T — a single turn at 10 cm rivals Earth's field.
2. (b) Straight wire at r = 0.10 m: B = μ₀I/2πr = (2 × 10⁻⁷ × 5.0)/0.10 = 1.0 × 10⁻⁵ T. Ratio: 3.14 × 10⁻⁵/1.0 × 10⁻⁵ = π.
3. Why π: curling the wire into a loop wraps the current around the point so every element sits broadside (sin θ = 1) at the minimum distance — the circle concentrates what the straight line spreads; the ratio is exactly (μ₀I/2R)/(μ₀I/2πR) = π.
4. (c) N aligned turns superpose: B = μ₀NI/2R = 100 × 3.1 × 10⁻⁵ = 3.1 × 10⁻³ T — a hundredfold for the cost of winding: the electromagnet's core trick.
5. (d) An arc contributes in proportion to its angle: a semicircle gives half the full loop, B = ½ × μ₀I/2R = μ₀I/4R = 1.6 × 10⁻⁵ T. (The straight feed wires, if they run radially through the center, contribute ZERO there — dl × r̂ vanishes along their own line: the dead-zone clause earning its keep.)
6. Toolkit audit: three reusable results (loop, wire, arc-fraction) plus one structural fact (no field along an element's axis) solved every part — no integral was recomputed.

*Outcome:* The student computes 3.1 × 10⁻⁵ T for the loop center, explains the π ratio against the wire's 1.0 × 10⁻⁵ T geometrically, scales to 3.1 mT for 100 turns, and halves the loop result for the semicircle while zeroing the radial feeds by the axis dead-zone rule.

**Real-world intuition**

- Electromagnet and inductor design starts at B = μ₀NI/2R — turn count and radius are the engineer's levers for field per ampere.
- MRI gradient coils are Biot–Savart engineering: precisely shaped windings sculpt the spatial field profile that encodes position in the image.
- Magnetic stimulation therapy (TMS) computes cortical fields from coil geometry by Biot–Savart before any pulse is delivered.
- Beam-steering magnets in accelerators and electron microscopes are designed by integrating current geometries to shape the bending field.
- Compass deviation compensation on ships and aircraft models the fields of onboard wiring runs — Biot–Savart audits of every current path near the sensor.

**Practice progression**

Item types: standard-result computations (loop center, N-turn coils, straight wire) solving for B, I, R, or N, composite-geometry problems (arcs + straight segments, concentric loops, opposed currents), structure-of-the-law items (dead zones, broadside maxima, why 1/r for the wire), comparison and scaling questions (loop versus wire, radius and turn-count scaling). Suggested item count: 10.

Begin with single-formula plug-ins, add N-turn and arc-fraction variations, then composite center-field problems with zero-contribution feeds, ending with structural explanations (cross-product geometry, integral origins of 1/r) and multi-source superpositions.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, composite-geometry problems, structural-explanation prompts. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must deploy the Biot–Savart standard results across geometries and compose arc/segment fields, beyond quoting the differential law.

Mastery signal: The student computes loop, coil, and wire fields with correct scaling, composes arc-plus-segment geometries including zero-axis contributions, and explains the law's cross-product structure and the wire's 1/r origin, at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Sell the law clause by clause — inverse square (old friend), current element (new source), cross product (new geometry) — before any integral. The 'directional lamp' picture of an element's field is worth the drawing time: broadside maximum and axial dead zones explain half the exam tricks (radial feeds contributing zero is the perennial one). Derive the loop center honestly (it's three lines: all elements broadside, equidistant, aligned) and QUOTE the straight-wire integral result unless your cohort has the calculus — but do explain the 1/r softening by the infinite-line accumulation argument, mirroring the charged-line story from electrostatics. Build the arc-fraction rule and the standard-results toolkit explicitly; students who recognize geometry outperform those who re-derive. Trail Ampère's law as 'Gauss for magnetism — the symmetry shortcut is next.'

**Student notes**

The law prices one current element's field: (μ₀/4π)(I dl sin θ)/r², perpendicular to both the element and the sightline, ZERO along the element's own axis, maximal broadside. μ₀/4π = 10⁻⁷ exactly. Own the toolkit: loop center B = μ₀I/2R; N turns multiply by N; straight long wire B = μ₀I/2πr; an arc gives its angle's fraction of the loop. Composite problems: add contributions as vectors and remember radial feed wires give ZERO at the center. Bigger loop = WEAKER center field (R is downstairs). The wire's 1/r is the integrated total, not a new law — each element is still 1/r².

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.magnetic-force
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): phys.em.amperes-law
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: Biot–Savart is Coulomb's law (phys.em.coulombs-law) with moving charge as source and a cross product as geometry — and it completes the interaction loop with the Lorentz force (phys.em.magnetic-force): currents both make fields and feel them. It certifies the wire field quoted at phys.em.magnetic-field, and its symmetry shortcut is Ampère's law (phys.em.amperes-law, next) exactly as Gauss (phys.em.gauss-law) shortcut Coulomb. Coil results feed the solenoid (phys.em.solenoid) and every inductor after.

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.magnetic-force

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one loop/coil computation with scaling, one composite arc-plus-feeds problem, one structural explanation (dead zones, or the wire's 1/r origin). Monthly, rebuild the standard-results toolkit from memory (element law → loop, wire, arc rules) — Ampère, solenoids, and inductance all draw on it.

---

### Ampere's Circuital Law

*Concept ID: `phys.em.amperes-law` · Difficulty: advanced · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 6h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to state Ampère's circuital law ∮B·dl = μ₀I_enc, explain circulation and enclosed current, apply it with symmetry-matched Amperian loops to derive the fields of a long straight wire (inside and outside a thick conductor), recognize when the law applies usefully, and connect it to Gauss's-law-style reasoning.

Ampere's law relates the line integral of magnetic field around a closed loop to the enclosed current.

Ampère's law is to Biot–Savart what Gauss's law was to Coulomb: the same physics repackaged as a global statement that turns symmetric problems into one-line derivations. The statement: the CIRCULATION of the magnetic field around any closed loop — ∮B·dl, the running sum of B's along-the-path component times path length — equals μ₀ times the total current THREADING the loop: ∮B·dl = μ₀I_enc. Every structural clause echoes Gauss with the geometry rotated: where Gauss used FLUX through a closed SURFACE counting enclosed CHARGE, Ampère uses CIRCULATION around a closed CURVE counting enclosed (threading) CURRENT — the swap reflects the fields' natures, E diverging from charges, B circulating around currents. Only currents piercing the loop's spanned surface count; currents outside contribute circulation zero (their positive and negative path segments cancel exactly), and the law holds for ANY loop — but is USEFUL only when symmetry lets B be pulled out of the integral. The working method mirrors Gauss's three steps: (1) read the symmetry to know B's shape (circles around a straight wire, uniform along a solenoid's axis); (2) choose an Amperian loop on which B is constant-and-parallel (a concentric circle; a rectangle half-in half-out); (3) then ∮B·dl collapses to B × (path length) and B follows arithmetically. The showcase is the THICK WIRE of radius a carrying uniform current I: outside (r > a), a concentric circular loop encloses all of I, so B·2πr = μ₀I gives B = μ₀I/2πr — the whole wire acts as if concentrated on its axis (shell-theorem flavor, magnetically); inside (r < a), the loop encloses only the fraction I(r²/a²) that threads it, so B = μ₀Ir/2πa² — rising LINEARLY from zero at the center, peaking at the surface, then handing over to the 1/r exterior. One asterisk belongs in every honest statement: the law as written holds for STEADY currents; changing electric fields add a term (Maxwell's displacement current) — the amendment that completes the law is deferred to Maxwell's equations, where it becomes the key to light itself.

**Key ideas**

- ∮B·dl = μ₀I_enc: the circulation of B around any closed loop equals μ₀ times the current threading it — Ampère's circuital law.
- Gauss-with-geometry-rotated: flux/surface/charge becomes circulation/curve/current — E diverges from its sources, B circulates around them.
- Only THREADING currents count: currents outside the loop circulate net zero; the law is always true, but only USEFUL when symmetry tames the integral.
- Method: read symmetry → choose an Amperian loop where B is constant and parallel to the path → circulation collapses to B × length → solve.
- Showcase (thick wire, radius a): outside B = μ₀I/2πr (all current enclosed — axis-equivalent); inside B = μ₀Ir/2πa² (only the r²/a² fraction threads) — linear rise, surface peak, 1/r fall.
- Fine print: valid for steady currents; changing E-fields add Maxwell's displacement-current term — the completion deferred to Maxwell's equations.

**Vocabulary**

- **Ampère's circuital law** — ∮B·dl = μ₀I_enc: the circulation of B around any closed loop equals μ₀ times the current threading it (steady currents).
- **circulation** — The line integral of B's along-path component around a closed curve — Ampère's counterpart to Gauss's flux.
- **Amperian loop** — The chosen closed path; picked to match symmetry so B is constant and parallel and the integral collapses to B × length.
- **enclosed (threading) current** — The net signed current piercing any surface spanning the loop — the only current the circulation counts.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* If a current sits outside the Amperian loop, the field on the loop is zero.
  *Correction:* The FIELD on the loop is generally nonzero — the outside current's B is right there. What vanishes is its net CIRCULATION: along-the-path contributions cancel around the closed curve. Ampère's law counts enclosed current only, but says nothing about B being zero pointwise; conflating circulation with field is the central trap.
- *Misconception:* Ampère's law only works for special loops, so it must be an approximation.
  *Correction:* The law is EXACT for every closed loop (steady currents) — as exact as Gauss's law. What's special about symmetric loops is only that they make the integral SOLVABLE by hand: B constant-and-parallel collapses ∮B·dl to B × length. Loop choice is strategy, not validity.
- *Misconception:* Inside a current-carrying wire the field is zero, like inside the charged conductor of electrostatics.
  *Correction:* The electrostatic zero was for STATIC charge on a conductor. A wire CARRYING current is not electrostatic — current fills its cross-section, and the interior field grows linearly, B = μ₀Ir/2πa², from zero at the axis to a maximum at the surface. Enclosed current, not conductor-ness, decides.
- *Misconception:* Ampère's law and Biot–Savart can disagree, since they look so different.
  *Correction:* They are the same physics: Ampère's law follows from Biot–Savart for steady currents, exactly as Gauss follows from Coulomb. Any geometry solvable by both gives identical answers (the straight wire's μ₀I/2πr checks). Choose by convenience — symmetry favors Ampère; arbitrary shapes need Biot–Savart.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Setting B = 0 on a loop because the current is outside (only the CIRCULATION is zero).
- Enclosing the full current inside a thick wire (use the r²/a² fraction).
- Choosing loops where B varies or tilts, then pulling B out of the integral anyway.
- Sign errors with multiple threading currents (direction sets the sign).
- Forgetting the steady-current condition when fields or currents change in time.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- A side-by-side Gauss/Ampère dictionary table-graphic: closed surface ↔ closed loop, flux ↔ circulation, Q_enc ↔ I_enc, 1/ε₀ ↔ μ₀ — the analogy as one picture.
- A circulation-meter animation: a bead walked around loops enclosing and not enclosing a wire, the running ∮B·dl accumulating to μ₀I or exactly zero.
- The thick-wire field profile: B(r) plotted rising linearly to the surface then falling as 1/r, with the two Amperian circles (inside/outside) drawn beneath their segments of the curve.
- An enclosed-current counter: loops threading one, two, or zero of several wires (with signs by direction), the circulation updating with each.
- The loop-choice gallery: good Amperian loops (concentric circle, solenoid rectangle) versus useless ones (tilted ellipse) — validity everywhere, usefulness only with symmetry.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A long straight conductor of radius a = 2.0 mm carries I = 10 A distributed uniformly over its cross-section. Find the magnetic field (a) at r = 4.0 mm from the axis, (b) at r = 1.0 mm (inside), and (c) at the surface r = a. (d) Sketch B(r) and state where the field is maximum.

1. (a) Outside: a concentric Amperian circle of radius 4.0 mm encloses the full 10 A. Symmetry: B is constant and tangent on the circle, so ∮B·dl = B·2πr = μ₀I → B = μ₀I/2πr = (2 × 10⁻⁷ × 10)/0.0040 = 5.0 × 10⁻⁴ T.
2. (b) Inside: the loop at r = 1.0 mm encloses only the current threading it: I_enc = I(r²/a²) = 10 × (1.0/2.0)² = 2.5 A.
3. B·2πr = μ₀I_enc → B = (2 × 10⁻⁷ × 2.5)/0.0010 = 5.0 × 10⁻⁴ T — numerically the same as at 4.0 mm, a neat coincidence of this problem (r = a/2 and r = 2a give equal B, since linear rise and 1/r fall mirror about the peak).
4. (c) At the surface both formulas agree (continuity check): B = μ₀I/2πa = (2 × 10⁻⁷ × 10)/0.0020 = 1.0 × 10⁻³ T — the maximum.
5. (d) B(r) rises linearly from 0 at the axis to 1.0 mT at r = a, then falls as 1/r outside — the tent-shaped profile with its peak at the surface; the interior linearity comes directly from I_enc ∝ r².
6. Method audit: every part was three lines — symmetry (circles), loop choice (concentric), collapse (B·2πr = μ₀I_enc). Biot–Savart would integrate for a page to say the same; that economy is the law's entire job.

*Outcome:* The student computes 0.50 mT at both 4.0 mm and 1.0 mm (recognizing the enclosed-fraction logic inside), finds the 1.0 mT surface maximum with both formulas agreeing, and sketches the linear-rise/1-over-r-fall profile, crediting the symmetry method for the brevity.

**Real-world intuition**

- Coaxial cables are Ampère's law engineered: equal and opposite currents on core and shield make the external circulation — and field — zero, which is why coax doesn't radiate into (or hear) its neighbors.
- Current transformers and clamp meters measure a wire's current from the circulation around it without touching it — Ampère's law as an instrument.
- Busbar and conductor design uses the interior linear field profile to compute forces and inductance within thick conductors.
- Tokamak and toroidal-confinement physics rests on Ampère's-law field bookkeeping of plasma and coil currents.
- EMC (electromagnetic compatibility) practice — twisted pairs, return-path routing — is applied enclosed-current cancellation: minimize net threading current, minimize stray field.

**Practice progression**

Item types: Amperian-loop applications (thick wires inside/outside, coaxial cables, current sheets), enclosed-current bookkeeping (multiple wires, signed threading, partial enclosure), circulation-versus-field conceptual items (outside currents, loop validity versus usefulness), profile-sketching and continuity checks (B(r) across boundaries). Suggested item count: 10.

Begin with single-wire outside/inside computations, add enclosed-fraction and multi-wire sign bookkeeping, then coaxial-cable regional analyses, ending with conceptual circulation/field distinctions and the steady-current fine print.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, regional-analysis problems, conceptual quiz. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must apply the circulation law with symmetry-matched loops across regions and count threading currents correctly, beyond stating ∮B·dl = μ₀I_enc.

Mastery signal: The student derives inside/outside fields with correct enclosed-current fractions, maintains continuity at boundaries, distinguishes circulation from pointwise field for external currents, and states the steady-current limitation, at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Teach it AS Gauss's sibling — students who internalized the Gauss three-step (symmetry, surface, collapse) transfer it wholesale once the dictionary (flux→circulation, charge→current) is on the board. The circulation-versus-field distinction needs explicit confrontation: walk a loop beside (not around) a wire and show the cancelling contributions — 'zero circulation' with obviously nonzero field. The thick-wire interior is the set piece; the enclosed-fraction argument (I r²/a²) rewards a slow, clear treatment, and the tent-shaped B(r) profile with its continuity check makes a fine board summary. State the steady-current fine print honestly and park displacement current with a one-line trailer for Maxwell. Keep solenoid/toroid applications for the next concept — one new law at a time.

**Student notes**

The law: circulation of B around a closed loop = μ₀ × (current THREADING the loop). Outside currents make field but ZERO net circulation — don't conflate the two. Use the Gauss method transplanted: symmetry tells you B's shape, pick a loop where B is constant-and-parallel (concentric circles for wires), collapse to B × 2πr = μ₀I_enc. Thick wire: inside, only the r²/a² fraction is enclosed → B ∝ r; outside, everything → B ∝ 1/r; peak at the surface, values matching at r = a. The law is exact for steady currents (a changing-E correction comes with Maxwell). Choose Ampère for symmetry, Biot–Savart for arbitrary shapes — they never disagree.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.biot-savart
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): phys.em.maxwells-equations, phys.em.solenoid
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: Ampère's law completes the Gauss analogy (phys.em.gauss-law) — the domain's two great symmetry shortcuts, one for each field — and repackages Biot–Savart (phys.em.biot-savart) exactly as Gauss repackaged Coulomb. Its immediate harvest is the solenoid and toroid (phys.em.solenoid, next); its displacement-current amendment is Maxwell's decisive edit (phys.em.maxwells-equations), the step that turns circuit law into light.

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.biot-savart

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one outside/inside thick-wire derivation with the B(r) sketch, one enclosed-current bookkeeping problem with signs, one circulation-versus-field explanation. Monthly, recite the Gauss↔Ampère dictionary and the steady-current asterisk — the missing term returns as the hero of Maxwell's equations.

---

### Magnetic Field of a Solenoid and Toroid

*Concept ID: `phys.em.solenoid` · Difficulty: proficient · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 4h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to derive the solenoid's interior field B = μ₀nI by Ampère's law, distinguish turn density n = N/L from total turns, describe the ideal solenoid's uniform-inside/zero-outside field, apply the toroid result B = μ₀NI/2πr, and reason about real-world deviations (end effects, finite length).

The solenoid produces a uniform axial magnetic field B = μ₀nI inside; a toroid confines the field within its core.

The solenoid is the answer to a design brief: build a UNIFORM magnetic field on demand. Wind a long wire into a tight helix of N turns over length L (turn density n = N/L, turns per metre) and the geometry performs a beautiful cancellation-and-reinforcement: between neighboring turns the fields oppose and cancel; inside the bore every turn's contribution aligns AXIALLY, stacking into a strong, straight, uniform field; outside, the return field spreads over so much space that (for the ideal infinite solenoid) it vanishes. Ampère's law harvests the result in three lines: take a rectangular Amperian loop straddling the winding, one side inside (parallel to the axis, length ℓ), one side outside (B = 0), two sides perpendicular (no along-path contribution) — circulation = Bℓ; the threading current is nℓ turns each carrying I, so Bℓ = μ₀nℓI, and B = μ₀nI. The formula's austerity is the lesson: the interior field depends ONLY on turn density and current — not on the solenoid's radius, not on position inside (uniform!), not on total length. Doubling the turns per metre or the current doubles B; a fatter bore gives the same field over more volume. Real solenoids honor the ideal in the middle and betray it at the ends: the field sags to about HALF the central value at each opening (end effect), and 'long compared to its radius' is the fidelity condition — a length-to-radius ratio of 10 keeps the central region uniform to fine precision. The TOROID is the solenoid bent into a doughnut, swallowing its own ends: now the field is confined ENTIRELY within the core, circulating along it with B = μ₀NI/2πr (from a circular Amperian loop of radius r inside the core — note it thins slightly toward the outer edge as 1/r, and is exactly zero both in the hole and outside the body). No ends, no leakage: the toroid is the choice where stray field is intolerable (transformers, tokamaks). Add a ferromagnetic core to either and the field multiplies enormously — the electromagnet — but that story belongs to magnetic materials.

**Key ideas**

- Solenoid interior: B = μ₀nI with n = N/L turns per metre — uniform, axial, and independent of radius and of position inside.
- The geometry's trick: between-turn fields cancel, axial contributions stack — uniform inside, (ideally) zero outside; the return flux spreads to nothing.
- Ampère derivation in three lines: rectangular loop half-in half-out → circulation Bℓ, threading current nℓI → B = μ₀nI.
- Turn DENSITY, not turn count: 1000 turns on 10 cm beats 1000 turns on 1 m tenfold — n is the design lever, with I.
- Real solenoids: field ≈ half the central value at the ends; ideality needs length ≫ radius (L/R ≳ 10 for a clean central region).
- Toroid — the solenoid that ate its ends: B = μ₀NI/2πr inside the core only (thinning as 1/r across it), zero in the hole and outside: total field confinement.

**Vocabulary**

- **solenoid** — A long tight helix of current-carrying wire: interior field B = μ₀nI — uniform, axial, radius-independent; (ideally) zero outside.
- **turn density n** — Turns per unit length, n = N/L — the design lever: field follows density and current, never total count alone.
- **end effect** — The field at a real solenoid's opening falls to ≈ half the central value; uniformity lives in the deep interior (L ≫ R).
- **toroid** — A solenoid closed into a ring: B = μ₀NI/2πr confined entirely within the core — zero in the hole and outside.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* A wider solenoid produces a weaker field, since the same flux spreads over more area.
  *Correction:* B = μ₀nI contains no radius: the interior field is set by turn density and current alone. A fatter bore delivers the SAME field strength filling MORE volume (more flux, same B). Radius costs wire and power, not field strength.
- *Misconception:* More total turns always means a stronger field.
  *Correction:* Only turns per METRE matter: n = N/L. Stretching 1000 turns from 10 cm to 1 m dilutes n tenfold and the field with it. Winding density — turns packed per unit length — is the lever; total count without length is meaningless.
- *Misconception:* The field just inside the end of a solenoid equals the central value μ₀nI.
  *Correction:* At the ends the field drops to about HALF the central value — the missing 'other half' of the infinite solenoid no longer contributes. Uniformity is a deep-interior property; instruments needing clean fields work in the middle third and treat the ends as fringe country.
- *Misconception:* A toroid's field fills the doughnut hole and leaks outside, like a bar magnet's.
  *Correction:* An ideal toroid confines its field ENTIRELY within the winding's core: Amperian loops in the hole enclose no current, and loops outside enclose equal and opposite threading (net zero). Inside the core B = μ₀NI/2πr; everywhere else, zero — that confinement is the toroid's whole point.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Using total turns N where turn density n belongs (or forgetting to divide by length).
- Expecting the field to depend on the solenoid's radius or to vary across the bore.
- Assuming full field strength at the ends (it's ≈ half).
- Placing toroid field in the hole or outside the body.
- Dropping the 2πr in the toroid formula or using the solenoid formula with the toroid's N directly.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- A turn-stacking animation: single loop's field, then 5, 20, 100 turns compounding — between-turn cancellation and axial reinforcement building the uniform bore field.
- The Ampère rectangle diagram: the half-in half-out loop over a cutaway solenoid, each side's contribution labelled (Bℓ, 0, 0, 0), the three-line derivation beside it.
- A field-map comparison: ideal solenoid (uniform inside, zero outside) versus a real finite one (bulging fringe fields, half-strength ends) — same colormap.
- The solenoid-to-toroid morph: bending the cylinder until its ends meet, the fringe fields swallowed, the field now circulating confined in the core with the 1/r shading across it.
- A design-lever dashboard: sliders for N, L, I (and radius, showing NO effect on B) with the bore field and required wire length updating — n as the real variable.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A solenoid 0.50 m long is wound with 2000 turns and carries 3.0 A. (a) Find the interior field. (b) The same 2000 turns are rewound over 1.0 m — what is the field now? (c) What current would restore the original field in the stretched winding? (d) A toroid with 2000 turns and mean radius 0.25 m carries the same 3.0 A — find the field at the mean radius and state the field in the doughnut's hole.

1. (a) n = N/L = 2000/0.50 = 4000 turns/m. B = μ₀nI = 4π × 10⁻⁷ × 4000 × 3.0 = 1.5 × 10⁻² T = 15 mT — a respectable laboratory field from bench-top numbers.
2. (b) Stretched: n = 2000/1.0 = 2000 /m → B = μ₀nI = 4π × 10⁻⁷ × 2000 × 3.0 = 7.5 mT — HALVED, though not one turn was removed: density, not count, is the lever.
3. (c) Restore 15 mT: with n halved, I must double → I = 6.0 A. (Cost note: doubling I quadruples the winding's I²R heat — density bought with copper beats field bought with current.)
4. (d) Toroid: B = μ₀NI/2πr = (4π × 10⁻⁷ × 2000 × 3.0)/(2π × 0.25) = (2 × 10⁻⁷ × 2000 × 3.0)/0.25 = 4.8 × 10⁻³ T at the mean radius.
5. In the hole (and outside the body): exactly ZERO — Amperian loops there enclose no net current; the toroid's field lives only within its core.
6. Consistency check: the toroid formula is the solenoid's in disguise — its effective turn density is n = N/2πr = 2000/1.571 ≈ 1273 /m, and μ₀nI = 4π × 10⁻⁷ × 1273 × 3.0 ≈ 4.8 mT ✓ — the bent solenoid remembers its origin.

*Outcome:* The student computes 15 mT, halves it to 7.5 mT under stretching (articulating density-not-count), restores it with 6.0 A noting the I²R cost, and finds 4.8 mT in the toroid core with zero in the hole — verifying the toroid as a closed solenoid.

**Real-world intuition**

- MRI magnets are superconducting solenoids: a human-bore helix carrying persistent current makes the 1.5–3 T uniform field the imaging depends on.
- Every relay, contactor, and car-starter solenoid is B = μ₀nI put to work — current in, plunger pulled, circuit switched.
- Inductors and transformer windings are solenoids and toroids by trade; toroidal cores are chosen exactly for their field confinement (low interference).
- Tokamak fusion reactors confine plasma in toroidal fields — the zero-leakage doughnut scaled to megamperes.
- Particle-beam and electron-microscope focusing coils use solenoid fields as magnetic lenses — uniformity by winding density, aim by geometry.

**Practice progression**

Item types: B = μ₀nI computations (solve for B, n, I, or N/L; rewinding variations), toroid problems (mean-radius fields, inner/outer variation, hole and exterior zeros), derivation and justification items (the Ampère rectangle, why radius is absent), design problems (specify n and I for a target field; end-effect and L/R fidelity reasoning). Suggested item count: 10.

Begin with direct B = μ₀nI plug-ins and rewinding contrasts, add toroid computations with region reasoning, then the Ampère-loop derivation reproduced, ending with design-to-specification and end-effect/fidelity questions.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, derivation task, design problems. Bloom alignment: Apply — students must apply B = μ₀nI and the toroid formula across rewinding, design, and regional-field scenarios, and reproduce the Amperian derivation, beyond memorizing the formulas.

Mastery signal: The student computes solenoid and toroid fields with the density-versus-count distinction intact, derives B = μ₀nI from the rectangular loop, assigns zero field correctly to toroid hole/exterior, and designs windings to specification, at 80% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

The derivation IS the lesson: the rectangular Amperian loop with three dead sides is Ampère's law at its most elegant — build it slowly and let B = μ₀nI fall out. Then interrogate the formula's silences: no radius, no position, no total length — each absence is a physics statement worth a minute. The rewinding problem (same turns, double length, half field) is the cleanest diagnostic for density-versus-count confusion. Demonstrate with a real coil and compass if possible; iron filings through a slotted solenoid model show the uniform bore beautifully. Introduce the toroid as 'the solenoid that solved its own end problem' and verify the formulas agree via effective density — students enjoy the consistency check. Park iron cores explicitly for magnetic-materials: 'this formula is the air-core truth; iron multiplies it, next concept but two.'

**Student notes**

One formula, austere and powerful: B = μ₀nI inside a solenoid — n is turns PER METRE (N/L), and neither radius nor position inside appears: the bore field is uniform. Rewinding the same turns over twice the length HALVES the field. Ends run at about half strength; trust the middle. Know the three-line Ampère derivation (rectangle half-in, half-out). Toroid: B = μ₀NI/2πr inside the core ONLY — zero in the hole, zero outside; it's a solenoid bent shut, and the formulas agree if you use n = N/2πr. Iron cores multiply everything — but that's the next story.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.amperes-law
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): none yet mapped
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: The solenoid is Ampère's law's (phys.em.amperes-law) finest harvest and the coil results of Biot–Savart (phys.em.biot-savart) taken to their engineered conclusion. Its uniform bore is the stage for flux and induction (phys.em.magnetic-flux, phys.em.faradays-law) and the geometry behind self-inductance (phys.em.self-inductance); iron-core multiplication arrives with magnetic materials (phys.em.magnetic-materials). The toroid's confinement returns at transformers (phys.em.mutual-inductance) and fusion machines.

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.amperes-law

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one B = μ₀nI computation with a rewinding twist, one three-line Ampère derivation from memory, one toroid problem with the three-region field map (core/hole/outside). Monthly, recite the formula's absences (radius, position, total length) and why each is absent — the solenoid is the domain's uniform-field workhorse from here to induction.

---

### Dia-, Para-, and Ferromagnetism

*Concept ID: `phys.em.magnetic-materials` · Difficulty: proficient · Bloom level: understand · Mastery threshold: 0.75 · Estimated study time: 4h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to classify materials as diamagnetic, paramagnetic, or ferromagnetic by their response to external fields, explain each behavior via atomic magnetic moments and domains, use relative permeability μr to quantify the response, describe hysteresis and the Curie temperature, and predict core effects on solenoid fields.

Materials are classified as diamagnetic, paramagnetic, or ferromagnetic based on their response to external magnetic fields.

Put matter into a magnetic field and the matter answers back — in one of three voices. The atomic origin is current at the smallest scale: orbiting and spinning electrons are microscopic current loops, each a tiny magnetic moment; how a material's electron moments are arranged (or absent) and how they respond decides its class. DIAMAGNETIC materials (bismuth, copper, water, most organic matter) have NO permanent atomic moments: the applied field induces feeble opposing loops (an atomic-scale Lenz effect), so they are WEAKLY REPELLED — relative permeability μr a hair below 1 (bismuth 0.99983), effect so faint it hides beneath anything stronger, yet universal (every substance is diamagnetic underneath) and, in extremis, capable of levitating a frog in a 16 T bore. PARAMAGNETIC materials (aluminium, sodium, oxygen) have permanent atomic moments that are randomized by thermal jostling: an applied field partially aligns them, giving weak ATTRACTION — μr a hair above 1 (aluminium 1.00002), strengthening as temperature falls (less jostling — Curie's law) . FERROMAGNETIC materials — iron, nickel, cobalt, and their alloys — are the loud exception: quantum exchange coupling locks NEIGHBORING moments parallel across whole regions called DOMAINS (each a microscopic magnet, ordinarily pointing every which way so the bulk shows nothing); an applied field grows favorably-aligned domains and swings others into line, multiplying the field THOUSANDS-fold — μr of order 10²–10⁵, the reason iron cores turn modest solenoids into industrial electromagnets (B = μrμ₀nI). Ferromagnetism carries two signature behaviors: HYSTERESIS — the domain realignment doesn't fully undo when the field is removed, leaving remanent magnetization (memory: permanent magnets, tape, hard disks; the B–H loop's area is the energy lost per cycle, so transformer cores want narrow loops, magnets fat ones) — and the CURIE TEMPERATURE, above which thermal agitation defeats the exchange coupling and iron abruptly resigns to mere paramagnetism (770 °C for iron; a heated magnet drops its keys). Three voices, one physics: atomic moments, and whether they exist, merely lean, or lock arms.

**Key ideas**

- Atomic origin: electron orbits and spins are microscopic current loops with magnetic moments — a material's class is set by whether moments exist and how they respond.
- Diamagnetic: no permanent moments; induced opposing loops → weak REPULSION, μr slightly < 1 — universal, faint, the substrate beneath all other magnetism.
- Paramagnetic: permanent moments randomized by heat, partially aligned by the field → weak ATTRACTION, μr slightly > 1, stronger when cold (Curie's law).
- Ferromagnetic: exchange coupling locks neighboring moments into DOMAINS; the field grows and aligns domains → μr ~ 10²–10⁵, thousands-fold amplification (B = μrμ₀nI with a core).
- Hysteresis: domain alignment persists after the field is gone — remanence makes permanent magnets and data storage; B–H loop area = energy lost per cycle (narrow loops for transformers, fat for magnets).
- Curie temperature: above it thermal agitation breaks the exchange lock and ferromagnets fall to paramagnetism (iron: 770 °C) — magnetic order melts before the metal does.

**Vocabulary**

- **diamagnetism** — Universal weak repulsion from fields: induced opposing atomic loops; μr slightly below 1 (bismuth, water, copper).
- **paramagnetism** — Weak attraction: permanent atomic moments partially aligned against thermal disorder; μr slightly above 1, stronger when cold.
- **ferromagnetism** — Domain-locked cooperative alignment (Fe, Ni, Co): μr ~ 10²–10⁵, hysteresis, and a Curie temperature above which order collapses.
- **hysteresis / remanence** — Magnetization lagging the applied field; what remains at zero field is remanence — the physics of permanent magnets and magnetic memory.
- **Curie temperature** — The temperature at which thermal agitation defeats exchange coupling and a ferromagnet becomes paramagnetic (iron: 770 °C).

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* All materials are attracted to magnets — some just too weakly to notice.
  *Correction:* Diamagnetic materials are REPELLED — water, copper, bismuth, and you. The effect is feeble but real (strong fields levitate frogs and graphite). The three classes differ in SIGN as well as size: repelled (dia), weakly attracted (para), fiercely attracted (ferro).
- *Misconception:* Iron is magnetic because its atoms are magnetic, and other metals' atoms aren't.
  *Correction:* Aluminium atoms carry magnetic moments too — iron's difference is COOPERATION: exchange coupling locks neighboring moments parallel into domains. Ferromagnetism is a collective, solid-state phenomenon, not an atomic one — the same iron atoms above 770 °C (Curie point) are merely paramagnetic.
- *Misconception:* An unmagnetized iron nail contains no magnetism until a magnet 'puts some in'.
  *Correction:* The nail is FULL of magnetism — countless domains, each saturated — but they point randomly and cancel. Magnetizing is reorganization, not injection: the external field grows aligned domains at their neighbors' expense. That's also why hammering or heating (disordering the domains) demagnetizes.
- *Misconception:* Hysteresis is a defect that better materials avoid entirely.
  *Correction:* Hysteresis is a TOOL as much as a tax: remanence IS the permanent magnet and IS magnetic data storage. Engineers choose the loop for the job — soft magnetic materials (narrow loop, low loss) for transformer cores, hard ones (fat loop, stubborn remanence) for magnets and memory.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Treating diamagnetic repulsion as 'weak attraction' (the sign differs, not just the size).
- Explaining ferromagnetism as 'atoms are magnets' without the domain/exchange cooperation.
- Thinking an unmagnetized nail contains no magnetic moments.
- Forgetting μr multiplies the ENTIRE solenoid formula (B = μrμ₀nI).
- Confusing Curie temperature (order collapse) with melting, or expecting gradual rather than sharp loss.

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- A three-panel response chart: dia/para/ferro samples near a strong magnet — feeble repulsion, feeble attraction, violent attraction — with μr values (0.99983, 1.00002, ~5000) captioned.
- A domain animation: randomly oriented domains in unmagnetized iron, an applied field growing aligned domains and rotating others, then partial persistence after field removal (remanence).
- The B–H hysteresis loop, annotated: initial magnetization curve, saturation, remanence, coercivity — with a narrow (transformer) and fat (permanent-magnet) loop overlaid.
- A Curie-point demonstration storyboard: iron pendant clinging to a magnet, heated past 770 °C, dropping — order melting on camera.
- A solenoid-core comparison: the same coil's field with air, then iron core, the B readout multiplying by μr — the electromagnet born in one substitution.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A solenoid with 1000 turns per metre carries 2.0 A. (a) Find its interior field with an air core. (b) An iron core of relative permeability μr = 400 is inserted — find the new field. (c) Classify these observations: bismuth (μr = 0.99983) is pushed weakly out of a strong field; aluminium (μr = 1.00002) is drawn weakly in; the iron core multiplied the field 400-fold. (d) The iron is heated above 770 °C — what happens to the field in the solenoid, and why?

1. (a) Air core: B₀ = μ₀nI = 4π × 10⁻⁷ × 1000 × 2.0 = 2.5 × 10⁻³ T = 2.5 mT.
2. (b) Iron core: B = μrμ₀nI = 400 × 2.5 × 10⁻³ = 1.0 T — the same coil and current now rival an MRI magnet: the multiplication is entirely the core's domain alignment.
3. (c) Bismuth: repelled, μr < 1 → DIAMAGNETIC (induced opposing moments, no permanent ones). Aluminium: weakly attracted, μr barely > 1 → PARAMAGNETIC (permanent moments, thermally randomized, faintly aligned). Iron: μr ~ 10² → FERROMAGNETIC (domain cooperation).
4. Scale reading: dia and para deviate from μr = 1 in the 4th–5th decimal; ferro deviates by a factor of hundreds — the classes are separated by SIX orders of magnitude of response, not shades of the same effect.
5. (d) Above the Curie temperature the exchange coupling loses to thermal agitation: domains dissolve, iron becomes merely paramagnetic (μr ≈ 1), and the field collapses from 1.0 T back to essentially the air-core 2.5 mT.
6. Moral audit: the coil supplied 2.5 mT of 'seed' field throughout; the missing 0.9975 T was matter's contribution — magnetism borrowed from electron alignment, repayable on heating.

*Outcome:* The student computes 2.5 mT air-core and 1.0 T iron-core fields, classifies the three materials from sign and size of response, and predicts the collapse to ~2.5 mT above the Curie point with the domain-dissolution mechanism stated.

**Real-world intuition**

- Transformer and motor cores are soft ferromagnets (silicon steel, ferrites): μr in the thousands multiplies flux, narrow hysteresis loops keep the per-cycle tax small.
- Permanent magnets (neodymium, alnico) are engineered remanence — fat hysteresis loops that hold their alignment against everything short of heat and hammer.
- Magnetic data storage — hard disks, tape, stripe cards — writes bits as local remanent magnetization: hysteresis as memory.
- MRI exploits paramagnetic contrast agents (gadolinium) and the diamagnetism of tissue water — all three classes appear in one scan.
- Induction cooktops need ferromagnetic pans: the pan's domains and eddy losses turn field oscillations into dinner — copper and aluminium pans stay cold.

**Practice progression**

Item types: classification items (from μr values, force sign, or described behavior), core-multiplication computations (B = μrμ₀nI, solve for any factor), mechanism explanations (induced loops, thermal randomization, domains, Curie transition), hysteresis-loop reading and material-selection questions (soft versus hard). Suggested item count: 10.

Begin with three-way classification from data, add core-field computations, then mechanism explanations at the atomic/domain level, ending with hysteresis-loop interpretation and application-matched material selection.

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: classification quiz, computation set, explanation prompts. Bloom alignment: Understand — students must explain the three response mechanisms and their signatures (μr, hysteresis, Curie point), beyond labelling materials.

Mastery signal: The student classifies materials from behavior or μr, computes core-multiplied fields, explains domains/remanence/Curie transition mechanistically, and matches loop shapes to applications, at 75% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Anchor the taxonomy in NUMBERS early — μr = 0.99983 / 1.00002 / ~5000 — so students grasp that ferro is not 'stronger para' but a different phenomenon (six orders of magnitude, plus memory and a phase transition). The domain model does the heavy lifting: unmagnetized-yet-full-of-magnetism, magnetizing-as-reorganizing, demagnetizing by hammer and heat all follow from one picture. A Curie-point demo (nickel wire or iron washer on a string near a flame and magnet) is theatrical gold. Present hysteresis as engineering choice (soft/narrow versus hard/fat loops) rather than nuisance. The frog-levitation anecdote earns diamagnetism more respect than its decimal places suggest. Defer m = NIA and torque to the next concept — keep this one qualitative-plus-μr.

**Student notes**

Three responses, three mechanisms: DIA (repelled, μr < 1 — induced opposing loops; everything has it), PARA (weakly attracted, μr > 1 — permanent moments vs thermal jostling; stronger cold), FERRO (fiercely attracted, μr ~ hundreds-plus — domains locked by exchange coupling; Fe, Ni, Co). Iron cores multiply a solenoid's field by μr: B = μrμ₀nI. Unmagnetized iron = randomly aimed domains, not absent magnetism; magnetize by aligning, demagnetize by heat/hammer/opposing field. Hysteresis = the alignment's memory (remanence → permanent magnets, storage; loop area = per-cycle loss). Above the Curie point (Fe: 770 °C) ferromagnetism switches off.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.magnetic-field
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): phys.em.magnetic-dipole
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: Matter's response completes the field story of phys.em.magnetic-field, and the induced-opposing-loop mechanism of diamagnetism is a preview of Lenz's law (phys.em.lenzs-law) at atomic scale. Core multiplication supercharges the solenoid (phys.em.solenoid) into transformers (phys.em.mutual-inductance); domain moments are the microscopic m = NIA of magnetic dipoles (phys.em.magnetic-dipole, next). The Curie transition is a phase change in the thermodynamic sense (phys.therm.phase-transitions) — order versus temperature, fought and lost.

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.magnetic-field

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one three-way classification from μr/behavior data, one core-field computation, one domain-model explanation (magnetize/demagnetize/Curie). Monthly, redraw the hysteresis loop with remanence and coercivity labelled and name one soft and one hard application — the loop returns with transformer losses.

---

### Magnetic Dipole and Earth's Magnetism

*Concept ID: `phys.em.magnetic-dipole` · Difficulty: proficient · Bloom level: understand · Mastery threshold: 0.75 · Estimated study time: 4h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to define the magnetic dipole moment m = NIA (direction by right-hand rule), compute the torque τ = mB sin θ and potential energy U = −mB cos θ of a dipole in a uniform field, explain compass alignment and motor action, and describe Earth's magnetism as a giant tilted dipole with declination and inclination.

A magnetic dipole has a moment m = NIA; Earth behaves as a giant magnetic dipole with geographic and magnetic poles.

The magnetic dipole is the domain's atom of magnetism — the smallest complete magnetic identity, and the exact twin of the electric dipole met in electrostatics. A flat coil of N turns, area A, carrying current I has magnetic dipole moment m = NIA (units A·m²), directed along the coil's axis by the right-hand rule (curl fingers with the current; thumb is m). The moment is the coil's handle: a uniform field grips it with TORQUE τ = mB sin θ — zero when m aligns with B, maximal broadside — twisting the dipole toward alignment exactly as the electric field twisted p; and the associated potential energy U = −mB cos θ runs from the aligned minimum (−mB, stable equilibrium) through zero (perpendicular) to the anti-aligned maximum (+mB, unstable). This torque IS rotating machinery: a compass needle is a dipole seeking alignment with Earth's field; a galvanometer reads current as deflection because τ ∝ I; and an electric MOTOR is a coil-dipole whose alignment torque is never allowed to finish — a commutator reverses the current every half-turn so the 'seek' continues forever. Everything magnetic is dipoles all the way down: bar magnets are domain dipoles summed, and the electron's spin is nature's elementary moment — which is why (echoing the no-monopole law) the dipole, not any magnetic charge, is magnetism's fundamental actor. EARTH itself is the grandest example: the planet behaves as a colossal dipole (moment ~8 × 10²² A·m²) generated not by buried iron bars (the core is above its Curie point!) but by the GEODYNAMO — convecting, rotating conductive liquid iron in the outer core sustaining currents. The dipole is tilted ~11° from the spin axis, so compasses point to magnetic (not geographic) north — the angular difference at your location is the DECLINATION; the field also dips into the ground at an angle (INCLINATION: 0° at the magnetic equator, 90° at the magnetic poles — the reason compass needles are counterweighted by latitude). Two footnotes with consequences: the pole in the Arctic is, magnetically speaking, a SOUTH pole (it must attract compass norths), and the geodynamo's record — striped into oceanic crust — shows the dipole has reversed polarity hundreds of times.

**Key ideas**

- m = NIA (A·m²), direction along the coil axis by the right-hand rule — the dipole moment is a current loop's complete magnetic identity.
- Torque τ = mB sin θ twists the dipole toward alignment; energy U = −mB cos θ — aligned is the stable minimum, anti-aligned the unstable maximum: the electric dipole's mathematics transplanted.
- The torque is machinery: compass (alignment sought), galvanometer (deflection ∝ I), motor (alignment perpetually re-denied by a commutator flipping I each half-turn).
- Dipoles all the way down: domains, atoms, and electron spin are moments — with no monopoles, the dipole is magnetism's elementary actor.
- Earth is a tilted dipole (~8 × 10²² A·m², ~11° off the spin axis) powered by the geodynamo — convecting conductive liquid iron, NOT a buried magnet (the core exceeds iron's Curie point).
- Navigation fine print: declination (magnetic vs geographic north), inclination (dip angle: 0° at the magnetic equator, 90° at the poles), the Arctic pole is magnetically a SOUTH pole, and the dipole has reversed polarity repeatedly through geological time.

**Vocabulary**

- **magnetic dipole moment m** — m = NIA for a coil (A·m²), along the axis by the right-hand rule — a loop's complete magnetic identity.
- **dipole torque / energy** — τ = mB sin θ toward alignment; U = −mB cos θ — aligned minimum (stable), anti-aligned maximum (unstable).
- **geodynamo** — Earth's field source: convecting, rotating conductive liquid iron in the outer core sustaining currents — not a permanent magnet (too hot).
- **declination / inclination** — Declination: angle between magnetic and geographic north at your location. Inclination: the field's dip below horizontal — 0° at the magnetic equator, 90° at the poles.

**Common misconceptions**

- *Misconception:* Earth's magnetism comes from a giant permanent magnet of iron in the core.
  *Correction:* The core is far hotter than iron's Curie temperature (770 °C) — permanent magnetization is impossible there. The field is generated DYNAMICALLY: convection and rotation of conductive liquid iron in the outer core drive electric currents (the geodynamo), a self-sustaining electromagnetic engine, not a buried bar magnet.
- *Misconception:* The magnetic pole in the Arctic is a magnetic north pole.
  *Correction:* It is magnetically a SOUTH pole: compass needles' NORTH ends point toward it, and unlike poles attract. 'Magnetic North Pole' is a geographic naming convention. Getting this right is the cleanest test of whether pole attraction is actually understood.
- *Misconception:* A compass needle points exactly at geographic north.
  *Correction:* It points along the LOCAL field toward magnetic north, which is offset from geographic north by the declination — several degrees in most places, and mapmakers print it on every serious chart. The needle also tries to DIP into the ground (inclination), which is why needles are counterweighted by hemisphere.
- *Misconception:* In a uniform field, a current loop experiences a net force pulling it toward the field source.
  *Correction:* In a UNIFORM field the net FORCE on a dipole is zero — opposite sides of the loop feel equal-and-opposite forces. What it feels is pure TORQUE (τ = mB sin θ). Net forces on dipoles require field GRADIENTS — which is why magnets attract iron strongly only up close, where the field is non-uniform.

**Common mistakes in practice**

- Using cos θ for torque or sin θ for energy (they pair the other way).
- Expecting a net force on a dipole in a UNIFORM field (torque only; force needs a gradient).
- Forgetting N in m = NIA.
- Calling the Arctic pole a magnetic north pole, or expecting compasses to point at true north.
- Explaining Earth's field with a buried permanent magnet (Curie point forbids it).

**Visual teaching opportunities**

- The m = NIA builder: a current loop with the right-hand rule curling to the moment arrow, then N stacked turns scaling the arrow — the dipole as a vector you construct.
- A torque-and-energy dial: the dipole at angle θ in a uniform field with τ = mB sin θ and U = −mB cos θ plotted together — equilibria (stable/unstable) flagged where torque crosses zero.
- The motor half-turn storyboard: coil twisting toward alignment, commutator flipping the current at the crossing, torque renewed — alignment forever denied, rotation forever sustained.
- Earth's dipole cutaway: tilted moment arrow, field lines pole to pole, the geodynamo's convecting outer core sketched, the Arctic pole labelled 'magnetically S'.
- A declination/inclination pair of maps: compass offset angles across the globe, and dip angle from 0° (magnetic equator) to 90° (poles), with a counterweighted needle inset.

**Worked example**

*Setup:* A rectangular coil of 50 turns, 10 cm × 10 cm, carries 2.0 A in a uniform field B = 0.20 T. (a) Find its magnetic moment. (b) Find the torque when the moment makes 30° with the field, and at what angle torque is maximal. (c) Find the work needed to rotate the coil from alignment (θ = 0°) to anti-alignment (θ = 180°). (d) Explain, in dipole terms, why a compass needle both points north and refuses to spin continuously like a motor.

1. (a) A = 0.10 × 0.10 = 1.0 × 10⁻² m². m = NIA = 50 × 2.0 × 1.0 × 10⁻² = 1.0 A·m² — direction along the coil axis by the right-hand rule.
2. (b) τ = mB sin θ = 1.0 × 0.20 × sin 30° = 0.20 × 0.50 = 0.10 N·m. Torque is maximal at θ = 90° (moment broadside to the field): τ_max = mB = 0.20 N·m; it vanishes at alignment — equilibrium.
3. (c) W = U(180°) − U(0°) = (+mB) − (−mB) = 2mB = 2 × 1.0 × 0.20 = 0.40 J — the full climb from the stable energy valley to the unstable hilltop.
4. (d) The compass needle is a dipole in Earth's field: torque swings it toward alignment (pointing along the local field ≈ north), overshoots damp out, and it settles in the U = −mB valley. It cannot rotate continuously because torque REVERSES past alignment, always restoring — a motor spins only because its commutator flips the current every half-turn, re-denying alignment; the compass has no commutator and so finds rest.
5. Scale note: Earth's field (~5 × 10⁻⁵ T) would grip this same coil with at most mB = 5 × 10⁻⁵ N·m — why compass needles are light, nearly frictionless, and finely pivoted.
6. Direction audit: every torque answer twists m TOWARD B (check with the right-hand rule); energy answers must match — negative and deepest when aligned, positive and highest when opposed.

*Outcome:* The student computes m = 1.0 A·m², τ = 0.10 N·m at 30° with the 90° maximum identified, W = 2mB = 0.40 J for full inversion, and explains compass settling versus motor spinning via torque reversal and the commutator's role.

**Real-world intuition**

- Every electric motor — fans, drills, EVs — is the dipole alignment torque with a commutator (or electronic switching) denying it rest.
- Moving-coil galvanometers and analogue meters read current as dipole deflection against a hairspring: τ = NIAB linear in I.
- Compasses and smartphone magnetometers navigate by Earth's dipole — with declination corrections built into mapping software.
- MRI's signal source is nuclear spin dipoles (protons) aligning in the scanner field and precessing — dipole energy levels put to imaging.
- Paleomagnetism reads the geodynamo's diary: seafloor stripes of reversed remanent magnetization dated the ocean floor and clinched plate tectonics.

**Practice progression**

Item types: moment and torque computations (m = NIA, τ = mB sin θ, solve for any factor), energy problems (U = −mB cos θ, work between orientations, equilibrium classification), device-reasoning items (compass, galvanometer, motor with commutator), Earth-magnetism questions (declination/inclination, pole polarity, geodynamo versus buried magnet). Suggested item count: 10.

Begin with m = NIA and single-angle torques, add energy/work computations and equilibrium analysis, then device explanations culminating in the motor's commutator logic, ending with Earth-field items (dip, declination, polarity, reversals).

**Assessment objectives**

Formats: computation set, device-explanation prompts, conceptual quiz. Bloom alignment: Understand — students must explain dipole behavior (torque toward alignment, energy landscape) and Earth's dipole nature with its navigational consequences, beyond computing NIA.

Mastery signal: The student computes moments, torques, and orientation energies correctly, explains compass/galvanometer/motor action from the same torque, and answers Earth-field structure questions (geodynamo, pole polarity, declination/inclination), at 75% accuracy or better.

**Teacher notes**

Run the electric-dipole parallel explicitly (p ↔ m, pE sin θ ↔ mB sin θ, same energy landscape) — students who see the transplant learn this in half the time. The motor is the payoff lesson: build it as 'alignment torque + commutator = alignment forever denied', ideally with a simple DC motor kit; the compass-versus-motor contrast in the worked example is the diagnostic question. For Earth, lead with the two surprises (the core is too hot for permanent magnetism → geodynamo; the Arctic pole is magnetically south) — surprise fixes memory. Declination matters practically (show a real map's declination rose); inclination explains counterweighted needles. Field reversals and seafloor striping make a superb capstone anecdote linking to plate tectonics.

**Student notes**

A current loop is a dipole: m = NIA, direction by the right-hand rule. Uniform fields exert TORQUE, not net force: τ = mB sin θ (max broadside, zero aligned), with energy U = −mB cos θ (aligned = stable valley, opposed = unstable hilltop; flipping costs 2mB). Compass: dipole settling into the valley. Motor: commutator flips I every half-turn so the valley is never reached. Earth: a tilted dipole from the geodynamo (liquid-iron currents — the core is too hot for permanent magnets); compasses point to magnetic north (declination away from true north), needles dip (inclination), and the Arctic pole is magnetically a SOUTH pole.

**Prerequisite graph**

- Requires: phys.em.magnetic-materials
- Unlocks (future prerequisite links): none yet mapped
- Cross-topic connections (graph cross-links): none
- Narrative: The dipole transplants the electric dipole's mathematics (phys.em.electric-dipole) into magnetism, powered by the force-on-current physics of phys.em.magnetic-force (τ = NIAB is F = BIL summed around the loop). Domains from magnetic materials (phys.em.magnetic-materials) are these moments en masse; the motor here is the generator's twin, inverted by Faraday's law (phys.em.faradays-law). Earth's field returns as the aurora's guide (phys.em.magnetic-force) and paleomagnetism's clock in earth science.

**Teaching hints — review triggers**

- phys.em.magnetic-materials

**Spaced repetition / revision guidance**

Revise after 1 day, 1 week, and 1 month: one m-τ-U computation chain, one compass-versus-motor explanation, one Earth-field fact check (geodynamo, declination/inclination, pole polarity). Monthly, restate the transplant — same dipole story as electrostatics, new field — and the motor's one-sentence secret: alignment perpetually denied.

---

### Magnetic Flux

*Concept ID: `phys.em.magnetic-flux` · Difficulty: proficient · Bloom level: apply · Mastery threshold: 0.8 · Estimated study time: 3h*

**Learning objective.** Students will be able to define magnetic flux Φ = BA cos θ through a surface, use the weber as its unit, compute flux for tilted and rotating loops and multi-turn coils (flux linkage NΦ), identify the three ways flux changes (B, A, or θ), and state that flux through any closed surface is zero (no monopoles).
